Distinguishing al-Mi'yar and al-Mihakk: Purpose and Terminology
Author enumerates two main differences between the two logical works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare al-Mi'yar and al-Mihakk based on their approach to philosophical terminology and identify which work is appropriate for students seeking an accessible introduction vs. those needing detailed engagement.

Core Idea
Two works, two audiences: al-Mi'yar engages philosophers; al-Mihakk frees logic from jargon.

In this passage, the author highlights the first major difference between al-Ghazali's two logical works. While both aim to teach logic, they differ fundamentally in their treatment of technical philosophical language. Understanding this difference is crucial for selecting the right text for your level and purpose.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two works mentioned
Locate 'al-Mi'yar' and 'al-Mihakk' in the Arabic passage. Note that the author also mentions other names for al-Mihakk (e.g., 'Majallat al-Fahm fi Asl al-'Ulum').
These are two of al-Ghazali's works on logic.
2
Analyze the first difference regarding terminology
The passage states that al-Mihakk did not concern itself with logical terminologies from philosophy books (لم يعن بالمصطلحات المنطقية الدائرة في كتب الفلسفة), while al-Mi'yar engaged with the philosophers' language and even defined their terms.
Consider why al-Ghazali might have chosen different approaches.
3
Connect to the author's purpose
The author explains that al-Mihakk aims to plant this science away from the whirlpool of technical terms, so students do not cling to form at the expense of meaning. Al-Mi'yar, by contrast, was written to debate the philosophers on their own ground.
This shows al-Ghazali's pedagogical strategy for different audiences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرق ما بين «المعيار» و «المحاك» جاء في أسماء ( المِحَك ) التي تداولتها أروقة المكتبات : مجلة الفهم في أصل العلوم » ، « مقدمة العلوم » ، وهما اسمان يُوحيان بتعميم ( المِحَك ) ككتاب يُرجع إليه في فن المنطق ولكن ما هي أبرز الفوارق بينه وبين « المعيار » ؟ ويمكن إجمال هذه الفوارق فيما يلي : أولاً - لم يعنَ ( المِحَك » بالمصطلحات المنطقية الدائرة في كتب الفلسفة ؛ إذ لم يكن القصد حوار الفلاسفة ، بل غرس هذا العلم - الذي استعصت أبحاثه على كثير من الطلبة ـ بعيداً عن دوّامة الاصطلاحات ، ونأياً بهم أن يتعلقوا بالمبنى على حساب المعنى ، وهذا ما لم يلتزمه الإمام في ( المعيار » ففيه التزم لغة القوم ، وحاورهم بها ، بل وحدَّ لهم اصطلاحاتهم التي كانت كرسائل خفية يُبعث بها ، ملوّحاً بتغيير لغة الجدل يومها ؛ حيث كان قد تفشّى بينهم أن الفقهاء لم يعرفوا اصطلاحنا ، ولم يدركوا مقاصدنا ، فكانت لهم سهام ( التهافت » و « المقاصد » و « المعيار ) بالمرصاد ثانياً - وجازة المادة وغزارتها معاً في « المِحَك » ، بينما نجد المعيار » يسرح فيه قلم الإمام ، ويبسط القول حتى يرى أنه قد أشبع البحث ، وهذا وذاك له أهميته عند طالب العلم ؛ إذ ) المِحَك ( كلقطة العَجْلانِ ، لا يتركها على أي حال ، و المعيار » كالشارح لما هنالك
Difference between al-Mi'yar and al-Mihakk. Among the names of al-Mihakk that have circulated in library corridors: 'Majallat al-Fahm fi Asl al-'Ulum', 'Muqaddimat al-'Ulum' - two names that suggest the generalization of al-Mihakk as a book referenced in the art of logic. But what are the most prominent differences between it and al-Mi'yar? These differences can be summarized as follows: First - al-Mihakk did not concern itself with logical terminologies circulating in philosophy books; for the intent was not dialogue with philosophers, but to plant this science - whose investigations were difficult for many students - far from the whirlpool of technical terms, and to prevent them from clinging to the form at the expense of meaning. The Imam did not adhere to this in al-Mi'yar, for in it he adhered to the language of the people (philosophers) and debated with them using it, and even defined their terminologies that were like secret letters sent, hinting at changing the language of debate at that time; for it had become widespread among them that the jurists did not know our terminology and did not grasp our intentions, so the arrows of al-Tahafut, al-Maqasid, and al-Mi'yar were on the lookout. Second - the conciseness of material and its richness together in al-Mihakk, whereas we find al-Mi'yar allowing the Imam's pen to roam and to elaborate until he thinks he has exhausted the research. Both have their importance for the student of knowledge: for al-Mihakk is like the quick catch, one does not leave it aside in any case, and al-Mi'yar is like the commentator on what is there.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines the differences between two of Imam al-Ghazali's logical works. It first notes that al-Mihakk has also been known by other names, suggesting it is a general reference in logic. The main differences are two: (1) al-Mihakk avoids technical philosophical jargon, aiming to make logic accessible, while al-Mi'yar engages with the philosophers on their own terms, using their language and even defining their terms. (2) al-Mihakk is concise yet rich, whereas al-Mi'yar is expansive. The author uses analogies: al-Mihakk is like a quick catch (لقطة العجلان) that one never drops, while al-Mi'yar is like a commentator (كالشارح) explaining the material. This passage helps the student understand the complementary nature of these two books: one for quick reference and one for detailed study.

Key Terms
المعيار
المحاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the author describe al-Mi'yar in terms of style?
A) It is concise and avoids technical terms
B) It is like a quick catch (لقطة العجلان)
C) It allows the Imam's pen to roam and elaborate
D) It is a commentary on al-Mihakk

Key Takeaway
Al-Mihakk is a concise, jargon-free introduction to logic, while al-Mi'yar is a detailed engagement with philosophical terminology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Suppose a student is confused by logical terms like 'al-hadd al-awwal' and 'al-hadd al-thani'. Which of al-Ghazali's works would you recommend? Justify your answer using the passage.
?
Explain why al-Ghazali wrote two different logic books based on the author's analysis in this passage.

Complementary Pedagogical Styles: Conciseness in al-Mihakk and Elaboration in al-Mi'yar
Author enumerates two main differences between the two logical works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the pedagogical benefits of concise vs. expansive logical works using the example of al-Ghazali's Mahakk al-Nazar and Mi'yar al-Ilm.

Core Idea
al-Mihakk is the 'quick catch'; al-Mi'yar is the 'detailed commentator'.

The second major difference between these two works is their length and depth. The author uses vivid analogies to capture their complementary roles: al-Mihakk is the essential quick reference, while al-Mi'yar is the comprehensive commentary. Grasping this helps you navigate Islamic texts of varying detail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the descriptions of each work's style
The passage says al-Mihakk has 'جازة المادة وغزارتها' (conciseness and richness), while al-Mi'yar 'يسرح فيه قلم الإمام ويبسط القول' (allows the Imam's pen to roam and elaborate).
Contrast the brevity of al-Mihakk with the expansiveness of al-Mi'yar.
2
Analyze the analogies used
Al-Mihakk is compared to 'لقطة العجلان' (the quick catch) that one never drops, and al-Mi'yar to 'الشارح' (the commentator). These analogies emphasize that al-Mihakk is indispensable for immediate understanding, while al-Mi'yar provides detailed explanation.
Think about how these roles complement each other in learning.
3
Discuss when each style is beneficial
A concise work like al-Mihakk is ideal for beginners or for quick review, whereas an expansive work like al-Mi'yar is suited for advanced students or for deep research.
This mirrors the structure of many madrasa curricula: short matn followed by detailed sharh.

The Purpose of Ghazali's Terminological Table
Ghazali lists his preferred logical terms alongside the common terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ghazali reformulated logical terminology and identify the three intellectual traditions he aimed to unify.

Core Idea
Ghazali's table fuses the terms of logicians, jurists, and theologians into one system.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a two-column table: common logical terms on the left, and his preferred equivalents on the right. This reform was driven by his project to reconcile the diverse terminologies used in falsafa, kalam, and fiqh. Understanding his table is key to reading his logical works without confusion.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three traditions
Scan the table for terms marked 'تبعاً للفقهاء' (following the jurists) and note the presence of common logical terms like 'المشكك' and 'المحمول'. This shows Ghazali is borrowing from and merging three fields.
2
Identify terms Ghazali kept unchanged
Some terms like 'القياس الشرطي المتصل' are retained from common logic, showing Ghazali did not abandon all standard terminology.
3
Identify terms Ghazali replaced
Look at pairs like 'المشكك' replaced by 'المتشابه' or 'المحمول' replaced by 'حكم' (following jurists). These changes often align with usage in kalam or fiqh.
4
Assess the pedagogical value
Consider how this table would help a student trained in one tradition read texts from another. It serves as a translation guide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المصطلح الدائر في عموم كتب المنطق النظريات دلالة الالتزام المشكك المحمول مطلوبات دلالة الالتزام أيضاً، ودلالة الاستتباع المتشابه حكم (تبعاً للفقهاء) المحكوم عليه (تبعاً للفقهاء) المحمول عليه (الموضوع) القضية الكلية القضية الجزئية القضية المطلقة العامة القضية المطلقة الخاصة القضية الشخصية القضية المعيَّنة، وقد أبقى المهملة على اسمها الشكل الأول والثاني والثالث والرابع النظم الأول والثاني والثالث وأسقط الرابع الشكل الأول (خصوصاً) النظم الأول الأوضح القياس الشرطي المتصل القياس الشرطي المنفصل الحد الأوسط المقدمة الصغرى المقدمة الكبرى التالي التجريبيات برهان اللّم برهان الإن نمط التلازم نمط التعاند علة (تبعاً للفقهاء) المقدمة الأولى المقدمة الثانية اللازم، أو التابع، وأبقى المقدم مقدماً اطراد العادات قياس العلة قياس الدلالة القضية السالبة القضية الموجبة القضية النافية القضية المثبتة
The common term in the general logical books: the theories; connotation; equivocal; predicate; objectives; connotation also, and implication; the ambiguous; judgment (following the jurists); the judged upon (following the jurists); the predicated upon (subject); universal proposition; particular proposition; absolute general proposition; absolute specific proposition; individual proposition; determinate proposition, and he left the indeterminate on its name; first, second, third, and fourth figures; first, second, and third moods, and he dropped the fourth; first figure (especially); clearest mood; conditional conjunctive syllogism; conditional disjunctive syllogism; middle term; minor premise; major premise; the consequent? (empirical matters?); demonstration of the reason; demonstration of the fact; pattern of concomitance; pattern of opposition; cause (following the jurists); first premise; second premise; necessary, or consequent, and he left the antecedent as antecedent; consistency of habits; syllogism of cause; syllogism of indication; negative proposition; affirmative proposition; negative proposition; affirmative proposition.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents a table of terminological equivalents. The left column (common terms in logical works) is paired with al-Ghazali's own terminology on the right. Al-Ghazali here attempts to unify the disparate terminologies of theologians (mutakallimun), jurists (fuqaha'), and logicians (mantiqiyyun). For example, the common term 'دلالة الالتزام' (connotation) is rendered by Ghazali as 'دلالة الالتزام أيضاً، ودلالة الاستتباع' (connotation also, and implication). The common term 'المشكك' (equivocal) is replaced by 'المتشابه' (ambiguous). The common term 'المحمول' (predicate) is replaced by 'المحمول عليه' (subject? but careful: Ghazali uses the juristic term for subject). Many terms are retained, such as 'القضية الكلية' (universal proposition) and 'الشكل الأول' (first figure). The table covers key logical concepts: kinds of propositions, syllogistic figures, moods, types of demonstration, and patterns of argument. The student should memorize the correspondence to understand Ghazali's unique style in his logical works.

Key Terms
دلالة الالتزام (dalalat al-iltizam): In this context, it is the common logical term for connotation (as opposed to denotation); Ghazali pairs it with his broader term including implication. [Note: Stable term in logic, but Ghazali extends it to include implication.]
المشكك (al-mushakkik): Common term for equivocal or ambiguous term; Ghazali replaces it with 'المتشابه'. [Note: In standard logic, al-mushakkik is used for equivocal; Ghazali's usage of al-mutashabih is influenced by juristic terminology.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali replace 'المشكك' with 'المتشابه'?
A) Because he disliked the sound of المشكك
B) To align with juristic usage of 'المتشابه'
C) Because المشكك is ambiguous in Arabic
D) He did not replace it

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's table is a deliberate effort to harmonize logical terms across disciplines; mastering it unlocks his logical corpus.

Proposition Types in Ghazali's Terminology
Ghazali lists his preferred logical terms alongside the common terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to match common logical terms for proposition types (universal, particular, absolute, individual, determinate) to Ghazali's preferred terms.

Core Idea
Ghazali retains the common names for proposition types but distinguishes between general and specific absolute propositions.

In his table, Ghazali lists several proposition types: universal, particular, absolute general, absolute specific, individual, and determinate. He also mentions that he left the indeterminate (المهملة) on its name. Understanding these terms is essential for parsing his syllogistic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common terms for propositions
Look for 'القضية الكلية', 'القضية الجزئية', 'القضية المطلقة العامة', 'القضية المطلقة الخاصة', 'القضية الشخصية', 'القضية المعيَّنة'. These are the common terms.
2
Find Ghazali's equivalents
The table pairs each common term directly with Ghazali's term. In most cases they are identical; note the addition of 'العامة' and 'الخاصة' for absolute propositions.
3
Note the exception for 'المهملة'
Ghazali explicitly says he kept the indeterminate on its name, meaning no change.
4
Apply to a proposition
Given a proposition like 'Every human is mortal', classify it in both common and Ghazali's terms.
Use Islamic examples: 'Every Muslim must pray' is universal affirmative.

Source Text

This passage presents a table of terminological equivalents. The left column (common terms in logical works) is paired with al-Ghazali's own terminology on the right. Key Terms
القضية الكلية (al-qadiyya al-kulliyya): Universal proposition; Ghazali retains the term. [Note: No shift.]
القضية المطلقة العامة (al-qadiyya al-mutlaqa al-‘amma): Absolute general proposition; Ghazali adds 'العامة' to distinguish from specific absolute. [Note: Addition of specificity.]

Checkpoint
Which proposition type does Ghazali leave unchanged from the common term?

Key Takeaway
Ghazali preserves most proposition types but differentiates absolute propositions into general and specific.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the Arabic proposition 'بعض الحوادث لا تبدأ بعلة' (Some events do not have a beginning cause), identify its proposition type in both common and Ghazali's terms.

Syllogistic Figures and Conditionals in Ghazali's Table
Ghazali lists his preferred logical terms alongside the common terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ghazali's terms for the four figures, moods, conditional conjunctive and disjunctive syllogisms, and his omission of the fourth figure.

Core Idea
Ghazali reduces the four figures to three by dropping the fourth and renames the first figure as the 'clearest mood'.

In the syllogistic section of his table, Ghazali maps the common names for figures ('الشكل الأول والثاني والثالث والرابع') to his own names ('النظم الأول والثاني والثالث') and explicitly says he dropped the fourth. He also distinguishes conditional conjunctive and disjunctive syllogisms, and defines terms like middle term, minor and major premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common figure names
The common terms are 'الشكل الأول والثاني والثالث والرابع'.
2
Find Ghazali's equivalents
Ghazali uses 'النظم الأول والثاني والثالث' for the first three figures, and drops the fourth.
3
Note the special status of the first figure
Ghazali calls the first figure 'خصوصاً النظم الأول الأوضح' (especially the first figure, the clearest).
4
Identify the conditional syllogisms
Common terms 'القياس الشرطي المتصل' (conjunctive) and 'القياس الشرطي المنفصل' (disjunctive) are retained by Ghazali.

The common term in the general logical books: the theories; connotation; equivocal; predicate; objectives; connotation also, and implication; the ambiguous; judgment (following the jurists); the judged upon (following the jurists); the predicated upon (subject); universal proposition; particular proposition; absolute general proposition; absolute specific proposition; individual proposition; determinate proposition, and he left the indeterminate on its name; first, second, third, and fourth figures; first, second, and third moods, and he dropped the fourth; first figure (especially); clearest mood; conditional conjunctive syllogism; conditional disjunctive syllogism; middle term; minor premise; major premise; the consequent? (empirical matters?); demonstration of the reason; demonstration of the fact; pattern of concomitance; pattern of opposition; cause (following the jurists); first premise; second premise; necessary, or consequent, and he left the antecedent as antecedent; consistency of habits; syllogism of cause; syllogism of indication; negative proposition; affirmative proposition; negative proposition; affirmative proposition.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents a table of terminological equivalents. The left column (common terms in logical works) is paired with al-Ghazali's own terminology on the right. Al-Ghazali here attempts to unify the disparate terminologies of theologians (mutakallimun), jurists (fuqaha'), and logicians (mantiqiyyun). For example, the common term 'دلالة الالتزام' (connotation) is rendered by Ghazali as 'دلالة الالتزام أيضاً، ودلالة الاستتباع' (connotation also, and implication). The common term 'المشكك' (equivocal) is replaced by 'المتشابه' (ambiguous). The common term 'المحمول' (predicate) is replaced by 'المحمول عليه' (subject? but careful: Ghazali uses the juristic term for subject). Many terms are retained, such as 'القضية الكلية' (universal proposition) and 'الشكل الأول' (first figure). The table covers key logical concepts: kinds of propositions, syllogistic figures, moods, types of demonstration, and patterns of argument. The student should memorize the correspondence to understand Ghazali's unique style in his logical works.

Key Terms
النظم الأول (al-nazm al-awwal): Ghazali's term for the first figure of categorical syllogism. [Note: Replaces the common 'الشكل الأول'.]
القياس الشرطي المتصل (al-qiyas al-sharti al-muttasil): Conditional conjunctive syllogism; retained from common logic. [Note: No shift.]

The Broad Definition of 'Ish'ar (Indication)
Author defines 'ish'ar (indication) as encompassing all modes of conveying meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five modes of 'ish'ar (indication) according to al-Ghazali and explain how they encompass all means of conveying meaning.

Core Idea
Indication is broader than explicit text—it includes context, inference, action, and gesture.

In Islamic legal theory, deriving rulings depends on understanding how texts and actions convey meaning. Al-Ghazali here defines 'ish'ar (indication) as encompassing all modes of perception—not just explicit statements. This expanded view prevents a narrow literalism and allows for deeper legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question being answered
The author begins with 'If you say: What is the meaning of your statement 'ash'ara'?' This frames the definition. Notice that the answer lists multiple modes.
2
Extract the five modes of indication
From the Arabic: 'خطاب صريح أو قرينة أو معنى مستنبط أو فعل أو إشارة' (explicit address, contextual clue, inferred meaning, action, gesture). Write them down.
These are the components of the definition.
3
Understand the scope
Al-Ghazali concludes: 'فالإشعار يعم جميع المدارك' (indication encompasses all modes of perception). Reflect on why a broad definition is necessary for legal reasoning.
It prevents missing evidence sources.
4
Connect to usul al-fiqh
Consider how each mode applies: explicit text (Qur'an, Hadith), contextual clue (occasion of revelation, qarina), inferred meaning (qiyas), action (Prophet's actions), gesture (silent approval). This is foundational for deriving rulings.
The definition legitimizes multiple sources of law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: ما معنى قولك: أشعر؟ فجوابه: أنه عُرف بدلالة من خطاب صريح أو قرينة أو معنى مستنبط أو فعل أو إشارة، فالإشعار يعم جميع المدارك. فإن قلت: ما معنى قولك: عليه عقاب في مناه؟ أي: جريانه سبب العقاب في الآخرة. فإن قلت: ما المراد بكونه سبباً؟ فالمراد ما يفهم من قولنا: الأكل سبب الشبع، وحز الرقبة سبب الموت، والضرب سبب الألم. فإن قلت: فلو كان سبباً لكان لا يرجى التفويل؟ كان يجب أن يعاقب لا محالة؟ فاقول: ليس كذلك، إذ لا يفهم من قولنا: الضرب سبب الألم، والدواء سبب الشفاء، أن ذلك واجب في كل شخص معين مشار إليه، بل يجوز أن يفرض في المحل أمور تدفع المسبب، ولا يدل ذلك على بطلان السببية. فرب دواء لا ينفع، ورب ضرب لا يُدرك المضروب ألمه لكونه مشغول النفس بأمر هام كمن يُرح في حال قتال وهو لا يحس في الحال به. وكما أن العلة قد تستحكم في دفع أثر الدواء، فكذلك قد يكون في سر باطنه خلاق وفضلة وخصال محمودة عند الله مرضية توجب ذلك العفو عن جريمته، ولا يوجب خروج الجريمة عن كونها سبب العقاب.
If you say: What is the meaning of your statement 'ash'ara' (it indicates)? The answer: It is known by a signification from an explicit address, or a contextual clue, or an inferred meaning, or an action, or a gesture. So 'ish'ar (indication) encompasses all modes of perception. If you say: What is the meaning of your statement 'upon it is a punishment in its purport'? That is, its occurrence is a cause of punishment in the Hereafter. If you say: What is meant by its being a cause? The meaning is what is understood from our saying: Eating is a cause of satiety, cutting the neck is a cause of death, and beating is a cause of pain. If you say: If it were a cause, then one would not hope for postponement? It would be necessary that he be punished inevitably. I say: It is not so, because we do not understand from our saying 'beating is a cause of pain' and 'medicine is a cause of health' that this is necessary in every specific individual pointed to. Rather, it is possible that there exist in the subject matters that repel the effect, and that does not indicate the nullity of causality. For sometimes medicine does not benefit, and sometimes a beating does not cause the beaten person to feel pain because he is preoccupied with an important matter, like one who is being killed during battle and does not feel it at the moment. And just as a hindrance may strongly prevent the effect of medicine, similarly there may be in his inner being worthy traits and praiseworthy qualities acceptable to Allah that necessitate the forgiveness of his crime, and that does not necessitate the crime's being removed from being a cause of punishment.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section on the concepts of 'ish'ar (indication) and 'sabab' (cause) within the context of legal or theological reasoning. The author uses a question-and-answer format. He first clarifies that 'indication' is a broad term encompassing any sign or evidence, not just explicit textual statements. It includes contextual clues (qarina), inferred meanings, actions, and gestures. This is a key logical concept in usul al-fiqh for deriving rulings. Then he addresses the meaning of 'cause' (sabab), explaining that it is not a deterministic, invariable relation. Using everyday examples like eating causing satiety and beating causing pain, he argues that exceptions do not invalidate causality; instead, there may be impediments (mawani') that prevent the effect. He analogizes this to divine forgiveness: even if a sin is a cause for punishment, repentance or other meritorious acts may avert it. This is a nuanced understanding of causality that preserves both responsibility and the possibility of divine pardon, reflecting al-Ghazali's Ash'ari theology.

Key Terms
إشعار
دلالة
خطاب صريح
قرينة
معنى مستنبط
فعل
إشارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Indication ('ish'ar) in al-Ghazali's framework is a comprehensive concept covering every means of conveying meaning, which is foundational for legal reasoning.

Causality (Sabab) and the Possibility of Exceptions
Author defends the concept of causality against the objection that exceptions invalidate it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Ghazali's concept of 'sabab' (cause) as non-necessary, subject to impediments, using the example of sin and forgiveness.

Core Idea
A cause normally leads to an effect, but impediments can prevent it—this does not invalidate causality.

Al-Ghazali addresses a theological objection: if sin is the cause of punishment, how can forgiveness occur? He argues that causality is not deterministic; exceptions due to impediments do not negate the causal relationship. This preserves both divine justice and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector says: 'If it were a cause, then one would not hope for postponement? It would be necessary that he be punished inevitably.' The assumption is that a cause must necessitate its effect.
This is a deterministic view of causality.
2
Understand al-Ghazali's response
He uses everyday examples: medicine does not always cure, beating does not always cause pain. The cause normally leads to the effect, but impediments (mawani') can block it. Thus, exceptions do not nullify the causal link.
Examples are from common experience.
3
Apply the analogy to sin and punishment
Sin is a cause of punishment, but repentance, good deeds, or divine grace can be impediments that prevent the actual punishment. This explains how forgiveness is possible without negating causality.
This preserves both divine justice and mercy.
4
Contrast with necessary causality
Al-Ghazali's view is consistent with Ash'ari occasionalism: causes are not necessary but habitual (custom of Allah). The cause-effect relationship is regular but not inviolable.
This is a key theological stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Understanding Ish'ara and Sabab in Legal Reasoning
Author defines ish'ara and sabab via dialogue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the scope of ish'ara (indication) and explain how sabab (cause) functions in Islamic legal causality, including the possibility of impediments.

Core Idea
Legal causality is not mechanical: a cause regularly brings its effect but can be blocked.

In this passage, the author engages in a dialectical explanation of what it means for an action to 'indicate' punishment and how it serves as a cause. He defines ish'ara broadly to include all valid modes of knowing the ruling, then defends the concept of sabab against the objection that causes necessitate effects. This clarification is crucial for understanding how actions can be forbidden without fatalism, aligning with Ash'ari occasionalism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms being defined
The passage defines two key terms: ish'ara (indication) and sabab (cause). Locate the statements that define each. Ish'ara is defined by enumeration of its types; sabab is defined through analogy.
2
Extract the enumeration of ish'ara
The author lists five forms of ish'ara: explicit address (khiṭāb ṣarīḥ), context (qarīna), derived meaning (ma'na mustanbaṭ), action (fi'l), and gesture (ishāra). Note that this is a broad definition covering all valid modes of knowing the ruling.
3
Understand the definition of sabab through analogy
The author defines sabab by giving common examples: eating causes satiety, cutting the neck causes death, striking causes pain. This shows that sabab is a regular causal relationship, not a logical necessity.
4
Analyze the objection
An interlocutor objects: if an action is a cause of punishment, then punishment must follow inevitably. Identify this objection in the text: 'If it were a cause, then change would not be hoped for.'
5
Examine the author's response
The author denies necessity, explaining that impediments (e.g., distraction, good deeds) can block the effect without invalidating the causal relationship. This preserves legal causality while avoiding fatalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والى ما اشعر بعقاب في الآخرة على فعله وهو المسمى محذوراً أو حراماً أو معصية فان قلت ما معنى قولك اشعر قلت انه عرف بدلالة من خطاب صريح او قرينة او معنى مستنبط او فعل او اشارة فالاشعار يعم جميع المدارك فان قلت ما معنى قولك عليه عقاب في الآخرة ان اجرائه سبب العقاب في الآخرة فان قلت ما المراد بكونه سببا فالمراد ما يفهم من قولنا الاكل سبب الشبع وحز الرقبة سبب الموت والضرب سبب الالم فان قلت فلو كان سببا لكان لا يرجى التفويل كان يجب ان يعاقب لا محالة فاقول ليس كذلك اذ لا يفهم من قولنا الضرب سبب الالم والدواء سبب الشفاء ان ذلك واجب في كل شخص معين مشار اليه بل يجوز ان يفرض في المحل امور تدفع المسبب ولا يدل ذلك على بطلان السببية قرب دواء لا ينفع ورب ضرب لا يدرك المضروب لكونه مشغول النفس بامر هام كمن رح في حال قتال وهو لا يحس في الحال به وكما ان العلة قد تستكمل دفع اثر الدواء فكذلك قد يكون في سر باطنه خلاق وضية وخصال محمودة عند الله مرضية توجب ذلك العفو عن جريمته ولا يوجب خروج الجريمة عن كونها سبب العقاب
And (the thing) that indicates a punishment in the Hereafter for its action, and it is called harmful, forbidden, or sin. If you say: What is the meaning of your statement 'indicates'? I say: It is known by indication from an explicit address, a context, a derived meaning, an action, or a gesture; thus indication comprehends all means. If you say: What is the meaning of your statement 'there is a punishment upon it in the Hereafter'? [I say]: That performing it is the cause of punishment in the Hereafter. If you say: What is meant by it being a cause? The meaning is what is understood from our statement: eating is the cause of satiety, cutting the neck is the cause of death, and striking is the cause of pain. If you say: If it were a cause, then change would not be hoped for, and it would be necessary that he be punished inevitably. I say: It is not so, for it is not understood from our statement that striking is the cause of pain and medicine is the cause of healing that that is necessary in every specific individual pointed to. Rather, it is possible that in the subject there are things that repel the effect, and that does not indicate the invalidity of causality. Perhaps medicine does not benefit, and perhaps a strike does not affect the struck person because he is preoccupied with an important matter, like one who is struck during battle and does not feel it at the moment. And just as the 'illa may perfect the repelling of the medicine's effect, likewise there may be in the secret of his inner self praiseworthy qualities pleasing to God that necessitate forgiveness for his crime, and that does not necessitate the crime ceasing to be a cause of punishment.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory discussion. The author begins by defining what it means for an action to 'indicate' punishment in the Hereafter: it includes any action that is known through explicit text (nass), context (qarina), derived meaning (ma'na mustanbat), action (fi'l), or gesture (ishara). This enumeration covers all ways of knowing the legal status of an act. Then the author clarifies that 'indicating punishment' means that the action is a 'cause' (sabab) of punishment. He defines cause through common examples: eating causes satiety, cutting the neck causes death, striking causes pain. A potential objection arises: if it is a cause, then punishment should be inevitable, but we know that sometimes people are forgiven. The author responds that causality in this context does not entail necessity in every specific case; other factors can block the effect (e.g., a strike may not cause pain if the person is distracted). Similarly, good deeds and sincere repentance can avert punishment, but that does not negate the action being a cause. This preserves the general causal relationship while allowing for exceptions due to mercy or mitigating factors. The discussion is important for usul al-fiqh and ethics, as it explains how actions can be judged as harmful or forbidden without fatalism.

Key Terms
اشعار
سبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Explicit address (khiṭāb ṣarīḥ)
B) Action (fi'l)
C) Personal opinion (ra'y)
D) Gesture (ishāra)

Key Takeaway
Ish'ara covers all valid indications of ruling, and sabab denotes a general causal relationship that can admit exceptions without undermining causality.

The Four Types of Words in Relation to Meanings
Author classifies words into four categories based on their relation to meanings, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between the four types of words (synonymous, distinct, univocal, equivocal) and apply this distinction to avoid errors in Islamic reasoning.

Core Idea
Confusing univocal with equivocal words undermines precise reasoning in Islamic sciences.

In this passage, the author introduces a foundational classification for understanding how words relate to their meanings. He divides multiple words for multiple meanings into four categories: synonymous (mutarādifah), distinct (mutabāyinah), univocal (mutawāṭi'ah), and equivocal (mushtarakah). This classification is critical in Islamic jurisprudence and theology, where a word's category determines how it is interpreted. A misclassification can lead to serious errors, as the author warns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Locate the four Arabic terms: al-mutarādifah, al-mutabāyinah, al-mutawāṭi'ah, al-mushtarakah. Note that the author invents these terms for this classification.
2
Define each category precisely
For each category, extract the author's definition and examples. Synonyms: different words, one meaning. Distinct: different words, different meanings. Univocal: one word, multiple individuals sharing a meaning. Equivocal: one word, completely different objects.
Pay attention to the key difference: do different words refer to a single meaning? or same word to multiple individuals sharing a meaning?
3
Focus on univocal vs equivocal
The author emphasizes that univocal words share a common meaning (ma'nā) for which the name was coined, while equivocal words share absolutely nothing in definition and reality.
This is the crucial distinction that often causes confusion.
4
Analyze the example of 'color'
The author uses 'lawn' (color) applied to black and white. It is univocal because both share the meaning of colorness. Compare with 'ayn' which is equivocal (eye, spring, gold, etc.).
Understand why 'color' is not equivocal, despite applying to different shades.
5
Recognize the potential error
The author warns that confusing univocal with equivocal leads to error in intellectual matters. Some mistakenly think 'color' is equivocal like 'ayn'. Know how this error occurs and why it matters in Islamic sciences.
This error can affect legal rulings and theological conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم الثالث: أن الألفاظ المتعددة بالإضافة إلى المسميات المتعددة على أربعة منازل، فلنخترع لها أربعة ألفاظ؛ وهي: المترادفة، والمتباينة، والمتواطئة، والمشتركة.
أما المترادفة: فنعني بها الألفاظ المختلفة في الصيغة المتواردة على مسمى واحد؛ كالخمر والعقار، والليث والأسد، والسهم والنشاب، وبالجملة: كل اسمين عبرت بهما عن معنى واحد يتناوله أحدهما من حيث يتناوله الآخر من غير فرق.. فهما مترادفان.
وأما المتباينة: فنعني بها الأسامي المختلفة للمعاني المختلفة؛ كالسواد، والقدرة، والأسد، والمفتاح، والسماء، والشجر، والأرض، وسائر الأسامي، وهي الأكثر.
وأما المتواطئة: فهي الأسامي التي تطلق على أشياء متغايرة بالعدد، ولكنها متَّفقة بالمعنى الذي وضع الاسم له؛ كاسم (الرجل) فإنه يطلق على زيد وعمرو وبكر وخالد، وكاسم (الجسم) فإنه يطلق على الإنسان والسماء والأرض؛ لاشتراك هذه الأعيان في معنى الجسمية التي وضع اسم الجسم بإزائها. وكل اسم مطلق ليس بمعين كما سبق.. فإنه ينطلق على آحاد مسمياته الكثيرة بطريق التواطؤ، فاسم (اللون) للبياض والسواد بطريق التواطؤ؛ فإنها متفقة في المعنى الذي به سمي اللون لونا، وليس بطريق الاشتراك ألبتة.
وأما المشتركة: فهي الأسامي التي تنطلق على مسميات مختلفة لا تشترك في الحد والحقيقة ألبتة؛ كاسم (العين) للعضو الباصر، والميزان، والموضع الذي ينفجر منه الماء وهي العين الفوارة، وللذهب، والشمس، وكاسم (المشتري) لقابل عقد البيع، وللكوكب الذي هو في السماء المعدود عند المنجمين من السعود.
ولقد ثار من التباس المشتركة بالمتواطئة غلط كثير في التباس العقليات، حتى ظن جماعة من ضعفاء العقول أن السواد لا يشارك البياض في اللونية إلا من حيث الاسم، وأن ذلك كمشاركة الذهب للحدقة الباصرة في اسم (العين)، وكمشاركة قابل البيع للكوكب في اسم (المشتري).
The third division: Multiple words in relation to multiple objects are of four ranks. Let us invent four terms for them: synonymous, distinct, univocal, and equivocal.
As for synonymous: we mean by it the different words in form that refer to a single object; like khamr and 'uqār, and layth and asad, and sahm and nushshāb. In short: every two names by which you express a single meaning, one of them applies in the same way as the other without difference—they are synonymous.
As for distinct: we mean by it the different names for different meanings; like blackness, power, lion, key, sky, tree, earth, and the rest of names, and they are the most numerous.
As for univocal: they are names that apply to things differing in number, but they agree in the meaning for which the name was coined; like the name 'man'—it applies to Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr, and Khalid, and like the name 'body'—it applies to human, sky, and earth, due to the sharing of these entities in the meaning of bodily substance for which the name 'body' was coined. Every absolute name not specified, as previously, applies to the individuals of its many objects by way of univocity. So the name 'color' applies to whiteness and blackness by way of univocity; for they agree in the meaning by which color is called color, and not by way of equivocation at all.
As for equivocal: they are names that apply to different objects that share absolutely nothing in definition and reality; like the name 'ayn' for the seeing organ, the scale, the place from which water bursts forth (the gushing spring), gold, and the sun, and like the name 'mustarī' for the receiver of a sales contract, and for the planet that is in the sky counted among the auspicious ones by astrologers.
And indeed, from the confusion of equivocal with univocal, much error has arisen in intellectual matters, to the point that a group of weak intellects thought that blackness does not share whiteness in 'colorness' except from the standpoint of the name, and that this is like the participation of gold with the seeing eyeball in the name 'ayn', and like the participation of the receiver of sale with the planet in the name 'mustarī'.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of words from the perspective of their relationship to multiple referents. The author divides words into four categories: synonymous (mutarādifah), distinct (mutabāyinah), univocal (mutawāṭi'ah), and equivocal (mushtarakah). He begins by defining synonymous words: these are different verbal forms that refer to a single meaning, such as 'khamr' and 'uqār' (both meaning wine), or 'layth' and 'asad' (both meaning lion). The key is that they are interchangeable without any difference in reference. Next, distinct words are those where different names correspond to different meanings, like 'blackness', 'power', 'lion', etc. These are the most common. Then he turns to univocal words, which are names that apply to multiple individuals differing in number but sharing the same underlying meaning. For example, the name 'man' applies to Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr, and Khalid—they all share the meaning of humanness. Similarly, 'body' applies to any physical substance. Univocal terms are contrasted with equivocal terms: equivocal names apply to completely different entities that share no common definition or reality. The author gives the example of 'ayn', which can mean the eye, a scale, a spring, gold, or the sun. Another example is 'mustarī', which can mean a buyer or a planet. The passage concludes with a cautionary warning: confusing univocal and equivocal terms leads to serious errors in intellectual matters. Some weak minds mistakenly think that 'blackness' and 'whiteness' share the name 'color' only equivocally, like 'ayn' refers to gold and the eye. In reality, color is univocal because both black and white share the meaning of colorness. The author underscores that the error arises from failing to distinguish between a common meaning (univocal) and mere verbal coincidence (equivocal). In Islamic sciences, this distinction is crucial: for example, 'ṣalāh' is equivocal between ritual prayer and connection (in some contexts), while 'zakāt' is univocal when applied to different types of obligatory charity.

Key Terms
المترادفة
المتباينة
المتواطئة
المشتركة

B) 'Rijāl' (men) applied to Zayd and 'Amr versus 'ʿAyn' for eye and for spring.

Key Takeaway
The fourfold classification of words—synonymous, distinct, univocal, equivocal—is essential for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences; confusing univocal and equivocal terms leads to intellectual errors.

The Three Relations of Meanings: Equal, More General, More Specific
Author classifies meaning relations into equal, more general, and more specific with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether two meanings are equal, more general, or more specific in extension, using examples from Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Core Idea
Meanings compare by extension: equal, broader, or narrower—key for valid syllogisms.

In this passage, the author introduces a fundamental classification: when comparing two meanings that expressions indicate, the relation is one of three types. This is essential for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic disciplines like kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh. Let's examine the definitions and examples carefully.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings compared
Find the two terms (e.g., 'body' and 'occupying space') that the author contrasts.
The terms are usually indicated by phrases like 'if you compare X to Y'.
2
Test if every instance of the first is an instance of the second
Ask: Does every X (e.g., every body) belong to Y (occupy space)? If yes, X is included in Y.
Use real examples from the passage or your own knowledge.
3
Test if every instance of the second is an instance of the first
Ask the reverse: Does every Y belong to X? If both directions hold, the relation is equal.
If only one direction holds, one is more general and the other more specific.
4
Determine the relation
Equal if both directions hold; more general if the first includes the second; more specific if the second includes the first.
Use the author's terminology: musāwī, aʿamm, akhaṣṣ.
5
Practice with the given examples
Re‑read the examples: body vs. occupying space (equal on some views), existence vs. body (more general), motion vs. body (more specific).
Note the nuance: the author identifies with a particular kalām school on substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيمُ الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّ المعاني التي يُدَلُّ عليها بالألفاظ إذا نُسِبَ الأعم والأخص بعضها إلى بعض .. وُجِدَ : إمَّا مساوياً لها ، وإمَّا أعم منها ، وإمَّا أخص منها ، وهذا ممَّا يُحتاجُ إلى معرفتِهِ في القياس . فإذا نسبت الجسم إلى المتحيّز .. وجدته مساوياً له ، لا يزيدُ التمثيل للمساوي ولا ينقص ؛ إذْ كلُّ جسم متحيّز ، وكلُّ متحيّز جسم ، هذا على رأْيِ مَنْ يقولُ : إِنَّ كلَّ متحيّز منقسمٌ ، وأَمَّا نحنُ .. فننسبُ التحيز إلى الجوهر (۱) ، فنرى التحيز مساوياً لهُ . وأمَّا إذا نسبنا الوجودَ إلى الجسمِ .. وجدناه أعم منهُ ، فَرُبَّ موجود ليس بجسم . وأما إذا نسبنا الحركة إلى الجسم .. وجدناها أخص منه ، فربَّ جسم ليس بمتحرك بالفعل ؛ كالأرض عند عدم الزلزلة .
The first division: Indeed the meanings that are indicated by expressions, when the more general and the more specific are compared to each other, are found: either equal to it, or more general than it, or more specific than it. And this is something that is needed for knowledge in syllogism. So if you compare body to occupying space, you find it equal to it, the representation of the equal neither increases nor decreases; since every body is occupying space, and everything that occupies space is a body. This is according to the opinion of one who says that everything that occupies space is divisible. As for us, we compare occupying space to substance (1), and we see occupying space equal to it. And if we compare existence to body, we find it more general than it, for perhaps there exists something that is not a body. And if we compare motion to body, we find it more specific than it, for perhaps there is a body that is not actually moving, like the earth in the absence of an earthquake.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the first division of meanings in terms of generality and specificity. The author states that when two meanings are compared, the relationship can be one of three types: equal, more general, or more specific. This classification is crucial for syllogistic reasoning. The author then provides concrete examples. For equality, he uses the example of 'body' and 'occupying space' (tahayyuz), noting that according to some, every body is occupying space and vice versa. However, he adds a nuance: he himself considers 'occupying space' to be equal to 'substance' (jawhar) in the sense of the indivisible atom. For the more general relation, he compares 'existence' to 'body', showing that existence is broader because there are existents that are not bodies (e.g., spiritual substances). For the more specific relation, he compares 'motion' to 'body', showing that motion is more specific because not all bodies are in motion, like the earth when there is no earthquake. The examples illustrate the logical relationships needed for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic philosophy and theology.

أعم
أخص

Key Takeaway
Meanings are related by extension; identifying whether they are equal, more general, or more specific is crucial for the validity of syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Distinguishing Sense, Imagination, and Intellect
Author classifies cognitive faculties into sense, imagination, and intellect, then adds the thinking power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sensed, imagined, and intellectual objects based on the conditions of perception and the faculty involved.

Core Idea
Perception is graded: sense requires presence, imagination retains absence, intellect abstracts further.

The author introduces a threefold classification of meanings (المعاني) based on how they are perceived: through the senses, through imagination, or through the intellect. Understanding this distinction is fundamental to the logical theory of concepts, as it clarifies how different types of knowledge are acquired and how they relate to one another. This lesson will guide you through the key differences, especially between sense and imagination, using the vivid examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Locate the threefold division
Find the sentence where the author states that meanings are divided into three types. Notice the terms محسوسة, متخيَّلة, and معقولة.
2
Understand the condition for sense perception
The author uses the example of sight: the visible object must be present. What happens when the object is absent? It nullifies sight but not the image.
3
Contrast imagination with sense
Imagination retains the image even when the object is absent. The author gives the horse example: the horse recognizes barley because its image persists in imagination. This shows imagination is not dependent on presence.
4
Identify the intellect as a separate faculty
The author introduces a third power, 'aql, which is unique to humans. Its perception of images is even more distinct from imagination than imagination is from sight. What makes it distinct? It is not merely retention but abstract understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم الثالث : أن المعاني باعتبار أسبابها المدركة لها ثلاثة : محسوسة ، ومتخيَّلةٌ ، ومعقولةٌ ، ولنصطلح على تسمية سبب تقسيم المعاني باعتبار طرق إدراكها الإدراك قوَّةً ، فنقولُ : في حدقتِكَ معنى بهِ تميَّزَتِ الحدقةُ عَنِ الجبهة حتَّى صرت تبصرُ بهِ ، وإذا بطلَ ذلكَ مِنَ الأعمى .. بطل الإبصار ، والحالة التي تدركها عند الإبصار شرطها وجود المبصر ، فلو انعدم المبصَرُ .. انعدم الإبصارُ وبقيَتْ صورته في دماغِكَ وتلك الصورة لا تفتقر إلى وجودِ المتخيَّل ، بل عدمه وغيبته لا تنفي الحالة المسمَّاةَ تخيُّلاً ، وتنفي الحالة المسماة إبصاراً . ولمَّا كنتَ تُحِسُّ بالتخيُّل في دماغك لا في فخذك وبطنك فاعلم أنَّ في هذا الدماغ غريزةً وصفةً بها تهيَّاً للتخيل ، وبها باينَ البطن والفخذ ، كما باينَتِ العينُ الجبهة والعَقِبَ في الإبصار بمعنى اختص به لا محالة . والصبي في أوَّلِ نشوئِهِ تقوى فيهِ قوَّةُ الإبصارِ دونَ قوَّةِ التخيُّل ، ولذلك إذا أُولِعَ بشيءٍ فغيَّبتَهُ عنه وشغلته بغيره .. اشتغل به . وربَّما يحدث في الدماغ مرضٌ يفسد القوَّةَ الحافظة للخيال ولا يفسد الإبصار ، فيرى الشيء ولكنْ كُلَّما يُغيَّبُ عَنْ عَينِهِ ينساه . وهذه القوة تشارك البهيمة فيها الإنسان ، ولذلك مهما رأى الفرسُ الشعير .. تذكَّرَ صورتهُ التي كانَتْ لَهُ في دماغِهِ ، فعرفَ أَنَّهُ للإنسان موافق وأنَّهُ مستلَةٌ لديهِ ، فبادر إليهِ ، ولوْ كانَتِ الصورة لا تثبتُ في خياله .. لكانَتْ رؤيتُهُ لهُ ثانياً كرؤيتِهِ لهُ أَوَّلاً حتَّى لا يبادر إليهِ ما لم يجرّبهُ بالذوقِ مرَّةً أخرى . قوة التعقل ذاتية ثمَّ فيكَ قوَّةٌ ثالثة شريفةٌ ، بها يباينُ الإنسانُ البهيمةَ تُسمَّى عقلاً ، ومحلُّهُ : إِمَّا دماغُكَ أوْ قلبُكَ ، وعند من يرى النفس جوهراً قائماً بذاتِهِ غير متحيّز (۱) محله النفس . ولها ـ أعني : قوَّةَ العقل - إدراك وتأثير بالتخيلاتِ مباين لإدراكِ التخيل مباينةً أشدَّ مِن مباينة إدراك التخيل لإدراك البصر ؛ إذ لم يكن بين التخيل والإبصارِ فرقُ إلَّا أنَّ وجودَ المبصَرِ وحضورَهُ كانَ شرطاً لبقاء الإبصارِ ، ولم يكن شرطاً لبقاء التخيُّل ، وإلَّا .. فصورةُ الفرس تدخلُ في الإبصارِ معَ قدْرِ مخصوص ولون مخصوص وبعد منك مخصوص ، ويبقى في التخيُّل ذلكَ القَدْرُ وذلكَ البُعد وذلكَ الشكل والوضعُ حَتَّى كَأَنَّكَ تنظرُ إِليهِ . القوة المفكرة ولعمري ؛ فيك قوَّةٌ رابعةٌ تُسمَّى المفكّرَةَ ، شأنها أنَّها تقدر على تفصيل الصور التي في الخيال وتقطيعها وتركيبها. وليس لها إدراك شيءٍ آخر ، ولكن إذا حضر في الخيال صورة إنسان .. قدرَ على أن يجعلَه نصفين ، فيصوّر نصف إنسان ، وربَّما ركَّبَ شخصاً نصفُهُ مِنْ إنسانٍ ونصفُهُ مِنْ فرس ، وربَّما
The third division: that meanings, considering their perceiving causes, are three: sensed, imagined, and intellectual. Let us agree to name the cause of the division of meanings considering the ways of their perception 'perception' as a power. We say: in your eyeball there is a meaning by which the eyeball is distinguished from the forehead, so that you see by it. When that is nullified in the blind, sight is nullified. The state that you perceive during sight has as its condition the existence of the visible object. If the visible object is absent, sight is nullified, but its image remains in your brain; that image does not require the existence of the imagined object; rather, its absence and non-presence does not negate the state called imagination, but it negates the state called sight. Since you feel imagination in your brain, not in your thigh or abdomen, know that in this brain there is an instinct and quality by which it is prepared for imagination, and by it the abdomen and thigh are distinguished, just as the eye is distinguished from the forehead and heel in sight by a meaning it is specialized with necessarily. In a child at the beginning of his development, the power of sight strengthens without the power of imagination; therefore, if he becomes attached to something and you hide it from him and distract him with something else, he is occupied with it. Perhaps a disease occurs in the brain that corrupts the power that preserves the imagination but does not corrupt sight, so he sees a thing, but whenever it is removed from his eye, he forgets it. This power man shares with animals; therefore, whenever a horse sees barley, it remembers its image that was in its brain, and knows that it is agreeable to man and that it is his stored[?], so it hastens to it. If the image were not fixed in its imagination, then seeing it a second time would be like seeing it the first time, so that it would not hasten to it unless it had tasted it once more. The power of intellect is essential. Then in you there is a third noble power, by which man is distinguished from animals, called intellect. Its locus is either your brain or your heart; according to those who see the soul as a self-subsistent, non-spatial substance, its locus is the soul. It — that is, the power of intellect — has perception and influence on images that is more distinct from the perception of imagination than the distinction of imagination's perception from sight's perception. Indeed, there is no difference between imagination and sight except that the existence and presence of the visible object is a condition for the persistence of sight, but not a condition for the persistence of imagination. Otherwise, the image of the horse enters sight with a specific magnitude, specific color, and specific distance from you, but remains in imagination with that magnitude, that distance, that shape and position, as if you are looking at it. The thinking power. By my life! In you there is a fourth power called the thinking power; its function is that it can detail the images in the imagination, cut them up, and combine them. It does not perceive anything else. But if an image of a man is present in the imagination, it can make it into two halves, so it pictures half a man; perhaps it composes a person half of whom is a man and half of whom is a horse.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a classification of cognitive faculties. The author begins with a third division: meanings (المعاني) are divided by their perceiving causes into three: sensed (محسوسة), imagined (متخيَّلة), and intellectual (معقولة). He then redefines 'perception' as a power (قوة) to explain the differences. Using the eye as an example, he describes sight: the eyeball is distinguished from the forehead by a special meaning that allows sight. Sight requires the existence of the visible object; if the object is absent, sight ceases but its image remains in the brain. This persistence is the imagination (التخيُّل): it does not require the object's presence. He then locates imagination in the brain, not in other body parts, and distinguishes it from abdominal or thigh sensations. In children, sight develops before imagination. Diseases can corrupt memory of images (the power preserving imagination) while leaving sight intact, so one sees but forgets immediately. Man shares imagination with animals: a horse recognizes barley because its image persists in its memory. Then the author introduces the intellect (العقل), a power exclusive to humans that distinguishes them from animals. Its locus is either brain, heart, or the soul (according to those who consider the soul a substance). Intellect's perception of images is more distinct than imagination's perception relative to sight. The difference between imagination and sight is solely that sight requires the object's presence; imagination does not. For example, a horse's image enters sight with specific magnitude, color, and distance, but in imagination it retains those details. Finally, a fourth power is introduced: the thinking power (القوة المفكرة). It does not perceive new things but can break down, separate, and combine images from the imagination, as in picturing half a man or a centaur. This power is the basis for complex mental constructs.

The Thinking Power: Combiner and Divider of Images
Author introduces the fourth cognitive power: the thinking power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the unique function of the thinking power (al-quwwa al-mufakkira) and explain how it differs from other cognitive faculties.

Core Idea
The thinking power does not perceive – it combines and divides what imagination already holds.

After discussing sense, imagination, and intellect, the author introduces a fourth cognitive power: the thinking power (القوة المفكرة). This faculty does not bring new objects into perception but works on the images stored in imagination. It can dissect a single image into parts or combine multiple images into new composites. This ability is the basis for creativity and abstract reasoning. This lesson will examine its function and its relationship to the other faculties.

Guided Path
1
Locate the fourth power
Find where the author introduces the fourth power. Note its name and the word 'رابعة' (fourth).
2
Understand its function
The text says: 'شأنها أنَّها تقدر على تفصيل الصور التي في الخيال وتقطيعها وتركيبها'. What does this mean? It can detail, cut, and combine images.
3
Note what it cannot do
It does not perceive anything else. So it is not a new source of information; it manipulates existing images.
4
Apply the examples
The author gives examples: making half a man, or composing a creature half man and half horse. What does this show about the power's creative capacity?

إدراك وتأثير بالتخيلاتِ مباين لإدراكِ التخيل مباينةً أشدَّ مِن مباينة إدراك التخيل لإدراك البصر ؛ إذ لم يكن بين التخيل والإبصارِ فرقُ إلَّا أنَّ وجودَ المبصَرِ وحضورَهُ كانَ شرطاً لبقاء الإبصارِ ، ولم يكن شرطاً لبقاء التخيُّل ، وإلَّا .. فصورةُ الفرس تدخلُ في الإبصارِ معَ قدْرِ مخصوص ولون مخصوص وبعد منك مخصوص ، ويبقى في التخيُّل ذلكَ القَدْرُ وذلكَ البُعد وذلكَ الشكل والوضعُ حَتَّى كَأَنَّكَ تنظرُ إِليهِ . القوة المفكرة ولعمري ؛ فيك قوَّةٌ رابعةٌ تُسمَّى المفكّرَةَ ، شأنها أنَّها تقدر على تفصيل الصور التي في الخيال وتقطيعها وتركيبها. وليس لها إدراك شيءٍ آخر ، ولكن إذا حضر في الخيال صورة إنسان .. قدرَ على أن يجعلَه نصفين ، فيصوّر نصف إنسان ، وربَّما ركَّبَ شخصاً نصفُهُ مِنْ إنسانٍ ونصفُهُ مِنْ فرس ، وربَّما
The third division: that meanings, considering their perceiving causes, are three: sensed, imagined, and intellectual. Let us agree to name the cause of the division of meanings considering the ways of their perception 'perception' as a power. We say: in your eyeball there is a meaning by which the eyeball is distinguished from the forehead, so that you see by it. When that is nullified in the blind, sight is nullified. The state that you perceive during sight has as its condition the existence of the visible object. If the visible object is absent, sight is nullified, but its image remains in your brain; that image does not require the existence of the imagined object; rather, its absence and non-presence does not negate the state called imagination, but it negates the state called sight. Since you feel imagination in your brain, not in your thigh or abdomen, know that in this brain there is an instinct and quality by which it is prepared for imagination, and by it the abdomen and thigh are distinguished, just as the eye is distinguished from the forehead and heel in sight by a meaning it is specialized with necessarily. In a child at the beginning of his development, the power of sight strengthens without the power of imagination; therefore, if he becomes attached to something and you hide it from him and distract him with something else, he is occupied with it. Perhaps a disease occurs in the brain that corrupts the power that preserves the imagination but does not corrupt sight, so he sees a thing, but whenever it is removed from his eye, he forgets it. This power man shares with animals; therefore, whenever a horse sees barley, it remembers its image that was in its brain, and knows that it is agreeable to man and that it is his stored[?], so it hastens to it. If the image were not fixed in its imagination, then seeing it a second time would be like seeing it the first time, so that it would not hasten to it unless it had tasted it once more. The power of intellect is essential. Then in you there is a third noble power, by which man is distinguished from animals, called intellect. Its locus is either your brain or your heart; according to those who see the soul as a self-subsistent, non-spatial substance, its locus is the soul. It does not perceive anything else. But if an image of a man is present in the imagination, it can make it into two halves, so it pictures half a man; perhaps it composes a person half of whom is a man and half of whom is a horse.

What Is Mantiq?

The Proposition and Its Parts: A Cross-Disciplinary Terminology
Author defines the proposition and lists technical names for its parts across disciplines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two parts of a proposition using the terminology of grammarians, theologians, jurists, and logicians, and distinguish the proposition's contextual names based on its role in reasoning.

Core Idea
The proposition's two parts are the same logical structure, but each discipline names them differently.

This lesson introduces the fundamental building block of logic—the proposition (qadiyya). The author defines it as the composite of a judgment (hukm) and the subject of that judgment (mahkum 'alayh), following juristic terminology. However, different Islamic sciences have their own terms for these two parts. Understanding these equivalences is crucial for reading texts across disciplines without confusion. We will also learn how the proposition is renamed depending on whether it is used as a premise, conclusion, claim, or demand.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of the proposition
Find how the author defines qadiyya. Look for the key phrase 'مجموع الحكم والمحكوم عليه' (the sum of the judgment and that over which it is a judgment).
2
Extract the two parts of the proposition
What are the two components? Note that in the author's chosen terminology, they are called hukm (judgment) and mahkum 'alayh (subject of judgment).
3
Map the terminologies across disciplines
List the pairs: grammarians (mubtada'/khabar), theologians (mawsuf/sifa), jurists (hukm/mahkum 'alayh), and logicians (mawdu'/mahmul). Notice which the author adopts.
4
Differentiate the contextual names for the proposition
Understand when a proposition is called muqaddima (premise), natija (conclusion), da'wa (claim), or matlub (demand) based on its role in reasoning.
5
Apply the analysis to an Islamic example
Use the example 'The world is originated' (العالم حادث) and identify its parts using each discipline's terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تركيب القضية جزأيها الفصل الثالث من فن السوابق في أحكام المعاني المؤلفة تأليفاً يتطرق إليه التصديق والتكذيب كقولنا مثلاً : العالم حادثُ ، والباري تعالى قديم ؛ فإِنَّ هذا يرجعُ إلى تأليف القوَّةِ المفكّرةِ بينَ معرفتين لذاتين مفردين ونسبة أحدِهِما إلى الآخر بالإثباتِ ، فإن قلت : العالم ليس بقديم ، والباري ليس بحادث .. كانَتِ النسبة نسبة النفي . وقد التأمَ هذا القولُ مِن جزأين يُسمّي النحويون أحدهما : والخلاف في تسمية مبتدأ ، والآخر : خبراً ، ويُسمّي المتكلّمون أحدهما : موصوفاً ، والآخر : صفةً ، ويُسمّي الفقهاء أحدهما : حكماً ، والآخرَ : محكوماً عليه ، ويُسمّي المنطقيُّونَ أحدَهُما : موضوعاً ، وهو المخبَرُ عنه ، والآخر : محمولاً ، وهو الخبرُ . باعتبارات معينة ولنصطلح نحنُ على تسمية الفقهاء ، فنسميهما حكماً ومحكوماً عليه ، ولنسم مجموع الحكم والمحكوم عليه قضيةً . تنوع أسماء القضية ولنسمها إذا استعملناها في سياق قياس : مقدمة ، وإذا استفدناها مِنْ قياس : نتيجةً ، وما دامَ غير مستنتج مِنَ القياسِ : دعوى إن كان لنا خصم ، ومطلوباً إن لم يكن لنا خصم ؛ فإن هذهِ الاصطلاحاتِ إذا لمْ تُحَرَّرْ .. اختلطت المخاطبات والتعليماتُ ،
The composition of the proposition and its two parts. Chapter Three of the Branch of Premises: On the rules of meanings composed as a composition to which affirmation and denial pertain. As our statement for example: 'The world is originated, and the Exalted Creator is eternal'; for this refers to the composition by the thinking faculty of two cognitions of two singular essences and the relation of one of them to the other by affirmation. If you say: 'The world is not eternal, and the Exalted Creator is not originated,' then the relation is the relation of negation. And this statement is composed of two parts. The grammarians call one of them — and there is a dispute in naming — 'mubtada' (subject) and the other 'khabar' (predicate). The theologians (mutakallimun) call one of them 'mawsuf' (described) and the other 'sifa' (attribute). The jurists (fuqaha') call one of them 'hukm' (judgment) and the other 'mahkum 'alayh' (that over which the judgment is made). And the logicians call one of them 'mawdu'' (subject), which is that which is reported about, and the other 'mahmul' (predicate), which is the report. With specific considerations, and we hereby adopt the terminology of the jurists, so we call them 'hukm' and 'mahkum 'alayh', and we call the sum of the judgment and that over which it is a judgment a 'qadiyya' (proposition). [Section on] The variation of names of the proposition: And we call it, when we use it in the context of a syllogism, a 'muqaddima' (premise); and when we derive it from a syllogism, a 'natija' (conclusion); and as long as it is not deduced from a syllogism, it is a 'da'wa' (claim) if we have an opponent, and a 'matlub' (demand) if we have no opponent. For these terminologies, if not clarified, discussions and teachings become confused.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and terminological analysis of the proposition (qadiyya). It opens by identifying the proposition as a composite of two parts, and that composition is subject to truth and falsity (tasdiq wa takdhib). The author gives an Islamic example: 'The world is originated' and 'The Exalted Creator is eternal' — both are propositions where the thinking faculty (al-quwwa al-mufakkira) combines two cognitions and affirms a relation. Negation is also shown. The two parts are then named differently by different disciplines: grammarians call them mubtada' and khabar (subject and predicate), theologians call them mawsuf and sifa (described and attribute), jurists call them hukm and mahkum 'alayh (judgment and subject of judgment), and logicians call them mawdu' and mahmul (subject and predicate). The author then chooses to adopt the jurists' terminology for his own usage, calling the two parts 'hukm' and 'mahkum 'alayh', and the composite 'qadiyya'. Furthermore, he notes that the proposition receives additional names based on its role in reasoning: when used as a component of a syllogism, it is a muqaddima (premise); when derived from a syllogism, it is a natija (conclusion); when not derived and with an opponent, it is a da'wa (claim); and without opponent, it is matlub (demand). The passage emphasizes the importance of precise terminology to avoid confusion.

Key Terms
القضية
المحمول
الموضوع
الحكم
مقدمة
نتيجة
دعوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) نتيجة (natija)
C) مقدمة (muqaddima)
D) مطلوب (matlub)

Key Takeaway
The proposition is defined as the combination of a judgment and its subject, and it receives different names depending on the discipline and its logical role.

If you are defending it against an opponent, what would you call it?

What Is Mantiq?

Definition of Qiyas and Its Three Types
Author defines qiyas and states it has three distinct patterns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reproduce the definition of qiyas and list its three fundamental patterns from which all others derive.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a logical force that compels a conclusion from premises.

This passage marks the transition from discussing propositions to discussing syllogisms. The author gives a precise definition of qiyas as a composite of premises that necessarily yield a conclusion, and then reveals that this definition encompasses three distinct patterns. Understanding this foundation is critical before studying the detailed rules of valid inference.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the definition
Read the Arabic definition word by word: 'qadaya ulifat ta'lifan yalzamu min taslimiha bi-d-darura qadiya ukhra'. Note that 'bi-d-darura' indicates the entailment is necessary. This is the core of qiyas.
2
Identify the two arts
The author divides the art into two sections: the construction (nazm) of the qiyas and the criterion (mihak) of that construction, which is the premises. Understand that validity depends on both structure and content.
3
Note the multiplicity of patterns
The author says qiyas is not a single pattern; it has three types of different origins. All other forms reduce to these three. This is an organizing principle for the rest of the subject.
4
Relate to the earlier definition of proposition
Recall that a proposition (qadiya) is a statement that can be true or false. Here, multiple propositions are composed; the composition is what produces the new proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني مِن مِحَاكِ القِيَاسِ في المقاصد
وهو طرفان
أحدُهُما : في نظم القياس .
والثاني : في مِحَكِ النظم وشرطِهِ ، وهو المقدّماتُ .
واعلم أني أعني بالقياس : قضايا أُلِفَتْ تأليفاً يلزمُ مِنْ تسليمها بالضرورة قضية أخرى ، وهذا ليس يتحد نمطه ، بلْ يرجعُ إلى ثلاثة أنواع مختلفةِ المآخذ ، والبقايا ترجع إليها .
The second art of the Criterion of Syllogism concerning the aims. It has two sections: one on the construction of the syllogism, and the second on the criterion of the construction and its condition, which is the premises. Know that I mean by 'syllogism': propositions composed in a composition that necessarily entails another proposition from their acceptance. This is not a single pattern; rather it goes back to three types of different origins, and the rest go back to them.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines the logical syllogism (qiyas) as a structured set of premises that, when accepted, necessarily produce another proposition (the conclusion). It then clarifies that this definition is not monolithic but comprises three fundamental patterns, to which all other forms can be reduced. The passage also sets out the two main sections of this art: the construction of the syllogism (the concrete arrangement of premises) and the criterion for evaluating that construction, which depends on the quality of the premises themselves. This is a foundational definitional passage that the student should memorize.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, how many primary patterns does qiyas have?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is a composition of propositions that necessarily entails a conclusion, and it manifests in three fundamental patterns.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
2. Identify which of the three patterns (not yet specified) your syllogism belongs to after studying the following lessons; for now, state the conclusion and explain why it follows necessarily.

Distinguishing the Two Premises in a Syllogism
Author defines the two premises based on their relation to the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premise is the first and which is the second in any categorical syllogism by examining their relation to the conclusion.

Core Idea
The first premise carries the subject, the second carries the judgment – derived from the conclusion’s order.

In a syllogism, the two premises are not interchangeable. The author defines them by looking at the conclusion: which premise contains the subject (mahkum 'alayh) and which contains the predicate (hukm)? This lesson will help you identify each premise correctly using a fiqh example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion's subject and predicate
Look at the conclusion, e.g., 'Nabidh is forbidden.' The subject is 'nabidh' (the thing being judged) and the predicate is 'forbidden' (the judgment).
2
Find the premise containing the subject
The first premise is the one that mentions the subject (nabidh) without containing the predicate (forbidden). For example, 'Nabidh is an intoxicant.'
This premise cannot contain the judgment of the conclusion.
3
Find the premise containing the judgment
The second premise is the one that contains the predicate (forbidden) without containing the subject. For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.'
This premise cannot contain the subject of the conclusion.
4
Apply to a new syllogism
Given a new conclusion, such as 'This wine is haram', write a first premise (e.g., 'This wine is an intoxicant') and a second premise (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram'). Verify each premise's content.
Practice ensures mastery of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمقدِّمة التي فيها تعرُّض للمحكوم عليه نسمّيها : المقدمة الأولى ، والتي فيها الحكم نسمّيها : المقدمة الثانية ؛ اشتقاقاً مِنْ ترتيب أجزاء النتيجة . فإنَّا نقولُ في النتيجة : ( فالنبيذُ حرام ) فيكونُ ( النبيذُ ( أولاً ، و( الحرام ) ثانياً . والمقدمة التي فيها المحكوم عليه .. لا يُتصوَّرُ أن يكونَ فيها الحكم وهي مقدمة ، والتي فيها الحكم .. لا يُتصوَّرُ أن يكون فيها المحكوم عليه وهي مقدمةٌ ، بل هما خاصتان للمقدمتين .
As for the premise in which there is mention of the subject of judgment, we call it: the first premise; and the premise in which there is the judgment, we call it: the second premise; deriving from the arrangement of the parts of the conclusion. For we say in the conclusion: 'So nabidh is forbidden.' So 'nabidh' is first, and 'the forbidden' is second. And the premise that contains the subject of judgment cannot be imagined to contain the judgment while being a premise, and the premise that contains the judgment cannot be imagined to contain the subject of judgment while being a premise; rather, they are two specialties of the two premises.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is definitional. It defines the two premises of a syllogism in terms of their relationship to the conclusion. The author explains that the premise which mentions the subject (al-mahkum 'alayh) of the conclusion is called the first premise, and the premise which contains the judgment (al-hukm) is called the second premise. This naming is derived from the order of the parts in the conclusion. The author gives the example from fiqh: the conclusion 'Nabidh is forbidden' yields that 'nabidh' is the subject and 'forbidden' is the predicate. So the first premise would be something like 'Nabidh is intoxicating' and the second premise 'Every intoxicating is forbidden.' The author then emphasizes that the first premise cannot contain the judgment, and the second premise cannot contain the subject; these are characteristics of the premises. This is a foundational conceptual distinction in Aristotelian logic, used here in the context of Islamic legal reasoning.

Key Terms
المقدمة الأولى
المقدمة الثانية
المحكوم عليه
الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what distinguishes the first premise from the second premise?
A) The first premise contains the judgment, the second contains the subject
B) The first premise contains the subject, the second contains the judgment
C) The first premise is always affirmative, the second is always universal
D) The first premise is always particular, the second is always universal

Key Takeaway
The first premise contains the subject of the conclusion, the second contains the predicate; they are distinct and cannot be swapped.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the conclusion 'This wine is haram.' Write a first premise that contains the subject and a second premise that contains the judgment. Ensure neither premise contains the other's distinguishing element.
