Refuting the Fallacy Against Tawatur
Refutation of a flawed argument against tawatur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the fallacy in the argument that tawatur cannot yield knowledge because each individual report can be erroneous.

Core Idea
A premise true for isolated individuals may lose its validity when conditions change.

The author presents a common argument against the epistemic value of tawatur and then exposes its logical flaw. Understanding this refutation is crucial for recognizing similar fallacies in reasoning about collective evidence, especially in hadith and testimony sciences.

Guided Path
1
Extract the opponent's argument
Identify the claim: tawatur does not bring knowledge. Find the premises: each individual report does not bring knowledge (since error is possible), and adding non-knowledge parts cannot yield knowledge.
The opponent assumes that the epistemic status of the whole is the sum of the parts.
2
Locate the hidden condition
Note that the proposition 'a single person's statement can be erroneous' is treated as absolute. But the author points out that this is true only when the person is isolated (منفرد بقوله). The condition 'isolation' is crucial.
The hidden condition is the key to refuting the argument.
3
Observe the condition's invalidation
When reports gather, the condition of isolation is removed. The multitude (الجمع) cancels the possibility of error collectively, even though each individual alone might err. Therefore the premise no longer applies.
The argument commits the fallacy of composition: what holds for parts does not necessarily hold for the whole.
4
Generalize the lesson
This refutation shows that tawatur can yield certain knowledge. More broadly, it warns against ignoring context-dependent conditions in logical arguments.
This is a classic example of how dialectical reasoning can be deconstructed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كمَنْ يقولُ مثلاً : التواترُ لا يورث العلم؛ لأنَّ قولَ الواحد لا يورثُهُ ، وكذلكَ الثاني ، فقد انضم ما لا يوجب إلى ما لا يوجب فالمجموع لا يزيد على الآحادِ ؛ لأنَّ قولَ الواحد لا يوجب العلم ؛ إذْ يجوزُ عليهِ الغلط ، وكذا كلُّ واحدٍ يجوز عليه الغلط والكذبُ ، ويأخذ قولنا : إنَّ الواحد يجوز عليه الغلط .. قضيَّةً يظنُّ أنَّها صادقةٌ بإطلاق ، وليس كذلكَ ، بل الصادقُ : أنَّ قولَ الواحدِ المنفردِ بقولِهِ يحتملُ الغلط ، والجمعُ يخرجُهُ عن الانفراد ، وكلُّ واحدٍ لا يوجب قوله العلم بشرط ألا يكونَ معَهُ الآخرونَ ، فإذا اجتمعوا .. بطل هذا الشرطُ .
وهذا مِنَ المثارات العظيمة للغلط
وللتصديق بالمشهورات أسباب كثيرة يطولُ إحصاؤُها ، ولا أن تُتَّخِذَ مقدِّماتُ القياس اليقيني منها ألبتة
ينبغي
وأكثرُ أقيسة الجدليينَ مِنَ المتكلمين والفقهاء في تفيي المشهورات مقدمات الفقهاء مجادلاتهم وتصانيفهِمْ .. مؤلَّفَةٌ مِنْ مقدّمات مشهورة فيما والمتكلمين بينَهُمْ ، سلَّموها لمجرَّدِ الشهرة ، وذهلوا عن سبيها ، فلذلك نرى أقيستَهُمْ تنتج نتائج متناقضةً ، فيتحيَّرون فيها ، وتتخبَّطُ عقولهم في تنقيحها .
Like one who says, for example: 'Tawatur does not bring knowledge because the statement of a single person does not bring it, and likewise the second, so what does not cause has been added to what does not cause, so the totality does not exceed the individuals; because the statement of a single person does not cause knowledge, since error is possible for him, and likewise every single person error and falsehood are possible for him.' And he takes our statement 'the single person error is possible for him' as a proposition he thinks is absolutely true, but it is not so. Rather, the true [proposition] is: the statement of a single person isolated in his statement is liable to error, and the multitude removes him from isolation. And every single person's statement does not cause knowledge on the condition that the others are not with him; so when they gather, this condition is invalidated. This is among the great origins of error. For assent to mashhurat there are many causes whose enumeration would be long, and they should never be taken as premises of certain syllogism. It is necessary. And most of the syllogisms of the dialecticians among the mutakallimun and fuqaha' in the ... of mashhurat, the premises of the fuqaha' in their debates and their works, are composed of mashhurat premises among them, which they accepted merely for their fame and were heedless of their cause. Therefore we see their syllogisms produce contradictory results, and they become perplexed in them, and their minds become confused in refining them.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a common argument against the knowledge-producing nature of tawatur (recurrent testimony). The author first presents the argument in the voice of an opponent: tawatur cannot bring knowledge because each individual report alone does not bring knowledge (since each individual can err), and adding non-knowledge-producing parts cannot yield knowledge. The author then exposes the fallacy: the proposition 'a single person can err' is taken as absolute, but it is true only when that person is isolated. When many report the same thing, the condition of isolation is removed, and the collective judgment changes. The author then warns that this is a major source of error. Next, he states that mashhurat (commonplace propositions) have many causes for being accepted, and they must not be used as premises in certain (yaqini) syllogisms. He then criticizes the dialecticians (mutakallimun and fuqaha') for relying on mashhurat premises in their debates and works, accepting them merely due to their fame and ignoring their basis, leading to contradictory conclusions and confusion.

Key Terms
التواتر
المشهورات
القياس اليقيني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the individual reports are not actually erroneous.
B) Because the condition 'isolated' is removed when the reports are combined.
C) Because tawatur is a type of logical proof that requires no conditions.
D) Because the argument is based on a false premise about error.

Key Takeaway
The fallacy of ignoring contextual conditions can lead to rejecting sound collective evidence; the author shows how tawatur escapes this fallacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'The testimony of a single witness is unreliable; therefore, the testimony of many witnesses is also unreliable because it is just an aggregation of single testimonies.' Identify the fallacy using the author's analysis.

Warning Against Mashhurat in Certain Syllogisms
Warning against using mashhurat as premises in certain syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mashhurat from yaqini premises and explain why mashhurat should not be used in syllogisms aiming at certainty.

Core Idea
Mashhurat are plausible but not certain; using them in certain syllogisms leads to contradictions.

After refuting the tawatur argument, the author turns to a broader methodological warning: mashhurat (commonplace propositions) have many causes for acceptance, but they must never be used as premises in certain (yaqini) syllogisms. He observes that many dialectical arguments among mutakallimun and fuqaha' rely on such premises, leading to contradictory results and confusion.

Guided Path
1
Define mashhurat
Mashhurat are widely accepted propositions that are not necessarily true but are accepted due to custom, fame, or social conditioning. They are distinct from yaqiniyyat (certain premises) like awwaliyyat (self-evident truths).
This term is standard in Islamic logic; al-Ghazali classifies mashhurat as one type of muhsillat (persuasive premises).
2
Understand the author's prohibition
The author states that mashhurat should never be taken as premises for a certain syllogism (al-qiyas al-yaqini). This is because such syllogisms require premises that are absolutely certain to yield certain conclusions.
The prohibition is absolute: 'لا أن تُتَّخَذَ مقدِّماتُ القياس اليقيني منها ألبتة'.
3
Identify the consequence of misuse
When scholars use mashhurat premises, they produce contradictory conclusions (نتائج متناقضة) because the premises are not fixed. This leads to perplexity and mental confusion in refining the arguments.
The author attributes this error to accepting premises based solely on fame (الشهرة) without investigating their epistemic basis.
4
Apply the warning to dialectical practice
The author specifically criticizes dialecticians (متكلمون and فقهاء) who rely on mashhurat in their debates and writings. This serves as a caution for any scholar constructing logical arguments.
The lesson is to always check the epistemic quality of premises before using them in rigorous reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كمَنْ يقولُ مثلاً : التواترُ لا يورث العلم؛ لأنَّ قولَ الواحد لا يورثُهُ ، وكذلكَ الثاني ، فقد انضم ما لا يوجب إلى ما لا يوجب فالمجموع لا يزيد على الآحادِ ؛ لأنَّ قولَ الواحد لا يوجب العلم ؛ إذْ يجوزُ عليهِ الغلط ، وكذا كلُّ واحدٍ يجوز عليه الغلط والكذبُ ، ويأخذ قولنا : إنَّ الواحد يجوز عليه الغلط .. قضيَّةً يظنُّ أنَّها صادقةٌ بإطلاق ، وليس كذلكَ ، بل الصادقُ : أنَّ قولَ الواحدِ المنفردِ بقولِهِ يحتملُ الغلط ، والجمعُ يخرجُهُ عن الانفراد ، وكلُّ واحدٍ لا يوجب قوله العلم بشرط ألا يكونَ معَهُ الآخرونَ ، فإذا اجتمعوا .. بطل هذا الشرطُ .

Like one who says, for example: 'Tawatur does not bring knowledge because the statement of a single person does not bring it, and likewise the second, so what does not cause has been added to what does not cause, so the totality does not exceed the individuals; because the statement of a single person does not cause knowledge, since error is possible for him, and likewise every single person error and falsehood are possible for him.' And he takes our statement 'the single person error is possible for him' as a proposition he thinks is absolutely true, but it is not so. Rather, the true [proposition] is: the statement of a single person isolated in his statement is liable to error, and the multitude removes him from isolation. And every single person's statement does not cause knowledge on the condition that the others are not with him; so when they gather, this condition is invalidated. This is among the great origins of error. For assent to mashhurat there are many causes whose enumeration would be long, and they should never be taken as premises of certain syllogism. It is necessary. Therefore we see their syllogisms produce contradictory results, and they become perplexed in them, and their minds become confused in refining them.

Analogical Extension by Dropping Attributes
Classification of rulings and methods of analogical extension

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two ways of analogical extension (ilhaq) in Islamic legal theory and apply the principle of dropping attributes to expand a ruling.

Core Idea
Analogous rulings expand by subtracting attributes, not by adding reasons.

In Islamic legal reasoning, not all rulings come directly from texts; many are extended through analogy (qiyas). This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Mahakk al-Nazar presents a key insight: analogical extension often requires deliberately ignoring some attributes of the original case to allow the ruling to apply more broadly. Understanding this method is essential for grasping how jurists derive rules for new situations without explicit textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of legal rulings
The author divides legal rulings into those known directly from an original source (ma'lum bi-asl) and those known through analogical extension (mulhaq bi-asl). Find the sentence in the Arabic that makes this division.
2
Understand the principle of dropping attributes
The author states that analogical extension requires 'dropping some attributes of the original from the degree of consideration' (حذف بعض أوصاف الأصل عن درجة الاعتبار). Examine the examples given (barley, chickpeas, garment) to see how dropping an attribute (e.g., 'being barley') allows the ruling to expand to other items.
3
Distinguish the two ways of analogical extension
The first way (tariq) does not require stating the common cause ('illa jami'a) but merely deletes the differentiating attribute. The second way is not fully described in this passage but would involve explicit mention of the cause. Locate the beginning of the discussion of the two ways in the Arabic.
4
Analyze the degrees within the first way
The first way has three degrees; only two are given. The highest degree (awla) is when the ruling in the attached case is more deserving than in the original. The second degree (musawat) is equality. Identify the examples for each: adultery vs. lawful intercourse for awla, female slave vs. male slave for musawat.
The third degree (adna) is missing from the text; it would be when the attached case is less deserving.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس
في حصر مدارك الأقيسة الفقهية و اشتنبيه على مجمل يزيد الانتفاع بها على مجلدات تحفظ من الأصول الرسمية
فنقولُ : الحكمُ الشرعي تارةً يكون مدرَكهُ أصلاً ، وتارةً يكون أصول معرفة الحكم
مدركُهُ معقول أصل .
فالمعلومُ بأصل : كالعلم بوجوبِ الكفارة على مَنْ أفطر بالجماع في نهار رمضانَ ، ويكونُ الأصلُ فيهِ : إِمَّا قول ، أو فعل ، أو إشارةً ، أو تقريرُ مِنْ صاحب الشرع صلواتُ اللهِ عليهِ ، ليسَ تفصيلُهُ مِنْ غرضنا .
وأما الملحق بالأصل : فله أقسام ، وتشترك في أمرٍ واحدٍ ، وهوَ أنَّ مِنْ ضرورتِهِ حذْفَ بعض أوصاف الأصلِ عَنْ درجةِ الاعتبارِ ؛ حتَّى يَتَّسعَ الحكمُ ؛ فإنَّ انساعَ الحكم بحذفِ الأوصاف ، وإنَّ نقصان الوصفِ يزيدُ في الموصوفِ ؛ أَي : في عمومِهِ ؛ فإِنَّ غيرَ القوت لا يُلحق بالقوت ما لمْ يُسقَطِ اعتبارُ كونِهِ بُرْاً في حكم الربا ، والحِمّص لا تلتحقُ بهِ المكيلاتُ ما لم يُحذفِ اعتبار كونِهِ مطعوماً ، والثوبُ على مذهب ابن الماجشون لا يلتحق به ما لمْ يُسقَطِ اعتبارُ كونِهِ مقدَّراً ، وهكذا كلُّ مَنْ زادَ إلحاقه .. زادَ حذفه .
طريقا الإلحاق فإذا عرفت هذا .. فاعلم أنَّ للإلحاق طريقين :
بالأصل في الحكم
الشرعي
أحدُهُما : أَلَّا يتعرّض الملحِقُ إِلَّا لحذفِ الوصفِ الفارق بينَ الملحَقِ والملحَقِ بهِ : فأَمَّا العلَّةُ الجامعة .. فلا يتعرَّضُ لها ألبتةَ ، ومع ذلك فيصح إلحاقه .
درجات الإلحاق وهذا له ثلاث درجات :
بحذف
أولوية الحكم في الملحق
المساواة بين
الملحق والملحق به
أعلاها : أن يكون الحكم في الملحَقِ أولى : كما إذا قلت : جامَعَ الأعرابي أهلَهُ ؛ فلزمته الكفارةُ ، فمَنْ يزني أولى بأنْ تلزمَهُ ؛ لأن الفارق بينَ جماع الأهل والأجنبي كونه حلالاً ، وهذا أولى بالإسقاطِ والحذفِ في وجوبِ الكفارةِ مِنَ الاعتبار
والدرجة الثانيةُ : أن يكونَ بطريق المساواة : كما قال النبي صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : ( مَنْ أَعْتَقَ شِرْكَاً لَهُ فِي عَبْدِ .. قُوْمَ عَلَيْهِ ) ، فقلنا : الأمةُ كذلكَ مِنْ غير أن نبين ها هنا علة سراية
Chapter Six: On enumerating the means of legal analogies and a reminder of a summary that increases benefit from them over volumes preserved from formal principles. We say: The legal ruling is sometimes its basis an original source, and sometimes its basis is the rational meaning of an original source. What is known by an original source: like the knowledge of the obligation of expiation on one who breaks his fast by intercourse during the day of Ramadan, and its original source is either a saying, or an act, or an indication, or an approval from the Lawgiver, peace be upon him; its detail is not our purpose. As for the analogically attached to the original: it has divisions, and they share one matter, which is that from its necessity is the dropping of some attributes of the original from the degree of consideration so that the ruling expands; for the expansion of the ruling by dropping attributes, and the deficiency of the attribute increases in the described, i.e., in its generality. For non-sustenance cannot be attached to sustenance unless consideration of its being barley is dropped in the ruling of riba, and chickpeas are not attached to measurable things unless consideration of its being a food is dropped, and a garment according to the school of Ibn al-Majishun is not attached unless consideration of its being measured by length is dropped, and so on, the more one extends its attachment, the more one extends its deletion. The two ways of analogical attachment: Since you have known this, know that for analogical attachment there are two ways: One of them: that the one making the attachment does not address except the deletion of the differentiating attribute between the analogically attached and the original case; as for the common cause, he does not address it at all, and even so his attachment is valid. The degrees of analogical attachment: And this has three degrees: The highest: that the ruling in the attached case is more deserving: as when you say: A Bedouin had intercourse with his wife, so expiation is incumbent on him, then whoever commits adultery is more deserving that it be incumbent on him; because the difference between intercourse with the wife and with an unlawful woman is that it is lawful, and this is more deserving of being dropped and deleted in the obligation of expiation than consideration. The second degree: that it is by way of equality: as the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Whoever frees his share in a slave, then it is valued for him.' We say: the female slave is likewise, without specifying here the cause of the transfer of ownership.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage, from Ibn Taymiyya's Mahakk al-Nazar, is a definitional classification of the sources of legal rulings (ahkam shar'iyya) into two broad categories: those known directly from an original source (ma'lum bi-asl) and those known through analogical extension (mulhaq bi-asl). The author gives an example of direct knowledge: the obligation of expiation (kaffara) for breaking the fast by intercourse in Ramadan, which is established by an explicit text (qawl), action (fi'l), indication (ishara), or approval (taqrir) from the Prophet. The detail of these is not the focus. The main discussion is on analogical attachment (ilhaq). The key principle for analogical extension is that it necessarily requires dropping some attributes of the original case from consideration to allow the ruling to expand. The author illustrates this with fiqh examples: non-barley grains cannot be attached to barley in the ruling of riba unless the attribute of 'being barley' is dropped; chickpeas (or other staples) are not attached to measurable foods unless the attribute of 'being a food' is dropped; and a garment, according to Ibn al-Majishun, is not attached unless the attribute of 'being measured by length' is dropped. In general, the more one extends the analogy, the more attributes one must drop. The author then presents two ways (tariqan) of analogical attachment. The first way is that the one making the analogy (mulhiq) only addresses the deletion of the differentiating attribute between the original (mulhaq bihi) and the attached (mulhaq), without needing to state the common cause ('illa jamia). This way has three degrees. The highest degree is when the ruling in the attached case is more deserving (awla) than in the original. The example: a Bedouin who has intercourse with his wife (halal) incurs expiation, so a person who commits adultery (haram) is even more deserving of expiation because the differentiating attribute (lawfulness) is more appropriately dropped. The second degree is when the ruling is equal (musawat), as in the hadith: freeing one's share in a slave requires valuation and freeing the rest; this ruling extends equally to a female slave (umm walad or similar) without explicitly stating the cause of the transfer of ownership. The third degree is not mentioned in the given text; it is presumably a lesser degree of analogy where the attached case is less deserving than the original. This classification shows how fiqh analogical reasoning operates by selectively disregarding attributes to apply rulings more broadly, and it distinguishes between the method of deleting the differentia without stating the common cause and the degrees of priority.

Key Terms
الحكم الشرعي
الأصل
الإلحاق
العلة الجامعة
الوصف الفارق

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the highest degree of analogical extension (ilhaq) when using the first way?
A) The ruling in the attached case is equal to the original.
B) The ruling in the attached case is more deserving (awla) than the original.
C) The ruling in the attached case is less deserving than the original.
D) The ruling in the attached case is unknown until the common cause is stated.

Key Takeaway
Analogical extension in Islamic law often proceeds by deleting the differentiating attribute rather than explicitly stating a common cause, with degrees of priority (awla), equality (musawat), or lower priority (adna).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the fiqh ruling that the sale of dates for dates must be equal in weight (riba al-fadl). Explain how you would extend this ruling to raisins using the first way of ilhaq, specifying which attribute you would drop and to what degree (awla, musawat, or adna) the extension belongs.
?
Consider the hadith: 'Do not sell gold for gold except equal for equal.' Using Ibn Taymiyya's principle of dropping attributes, explain how a jurist might extend this ruling to silver coins (dirhams) by deleting the attribute of 'being gold' and identify which degree of ilhaq this exemplifies.

What Is Mantiq?

Methods and Degrees of Analogical Reasoning (Ilhaq)
Author explains the two methods of ilhaq and their three degrees with examples from fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two methods of ilhaq and identify the three degrees of analogical extension using fiqh examples from the passage.

Core Idea
Analogical reasoning can proceed by removing irrelevant differences without stating the common cause.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya explains a practical approach to analogical reasoning (ilhaq) used by jurists. Rather than requiring explicit mention of the common cause (ʻilla), he shows that deleting the distinguishing attribute (al-wasf al-fariq) between cases suffices, and outlines three degrees of such extension. The passage uses vivid fiqh examples to illustrate priority and equality in analogical rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods of ilhaq
The passage states that there are two methods of attachment (ilhaq). The first method, which it describes in detail, is to delete the distinguishing attribute between the original and the new case without mentioning the common cause. The second method is implied as the one that does mention the common cause. Notice how the author emphasizes that not stating the cause does not invalidate the analogy.
The text explicitly mentions the first method; the second is inferred by contrast.
2
Understand the first method: deleting the distinguishing attribute
Focus on the statement: 'أَلَّا يتعرّض الملحِقُ إِلَّا لحذفِ الوصفِ الفارق' – the one making the analogy only engages in deleting the distinguishing attribute. The common cause is not addressed at all. This method relies on the idea that irrelevant differences should be ignored, not on finding a positive common cause.
3
Recognize the three degrees of analogy
The text says ilhaq has three degrees. The highest is 'الأولوية' (priority) where the new case is more deserving of the ruling. The second is 'المساواة' (equality) where the ruling applies equally. The third degree is not fully spelled out due to text corruption, but it is implied as a lower degree where the analogy is weaker.
Look for the phrases 'أعلاها' and 'الدرجة الثانية'.
4
Apply the degrees to the examples
The passage gives two examples: (1) priority: a Bedouin who had intercourse with his wife must pay expiation; a fornicator is even more deserving because the difference (lawful vs. unlawful) should be dropped. (2) equality: the hadith about freeing a share in a slave is extended to a female slave because the only difference is femininity, which does not affect the ruling. Identify which degree each example illustrates.
The third degree may be illustrated by a case where the analogy is possible but weaker, though not explicitly given here.

Source Text

لم يُسقَطِ اعتبارُ كونِهِ مقدَّراً ، وهكذا كلُّ مَنْ زادَ إلحاقه .. زادَ حذفه . طريقا الإلحاق فإذا عرفت هذا .. فاعلم أنَّ للإلحاق طريقين : بالأصل في الحكم الشرعي أحدُهُما : أَلَّا يتعرّض الملحِقُ إِلَّا لحذفِ الوصفِ الفارق بينَ الملحَقِ والملحَقِ بهِ : فأَمَّا العلَّةُ الجامعة .. فلا يتعرَّضُ لها ألبتةَ ، ومع ذلك فيصح إلحاقه . درجات الإلحاق وهذا له ثلاث درجات : بحذف [؟] أولوية الحكم في الملحق المساواة بين الملحق والملحق به أعلاها : أن يكون الحكم في الملحَقِ أولى : كما إذا قلت : جامَعَ الأعرابي أهلَهُ ؛ فلزمته الكفارةُ ، فمَنْ يزني أولى بأنْ تلزمَهُ ؛ لأن الفارق بينَ جماع الأهل والأجنبي كونه حلالاً ، وهذا أولى بالإسقاطِ والحذفِ في وجوبِ الكفارةِ مِنَ الاعتبار والدرجة الثانيةُ : أن يكونَ بطريق المساواة : كما قال النبي صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : ( مَنْ أَعْتَقَ شِرْكَاً لَهُ فِي عَبْدٍ .. قُوْمَ عَلَيْهِ « ، فقلنا : الأمةُ كذلكَ مِنْ غير أن نبين ها هنا علة سراية العتق ، كما لم نبيِّنُ في المثال السابق علة وجوب الكفَّارة . ولكن نعلم أنَّهُ لا فارقَ إِلَّا الأنوثة ، والأنوثة لا مدخل لها في التأثير فيما يرجعُ إلى حكم الرق والعشق ، بل ذلك كما لو حكم صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلّم بالعتق في غلام كبير . لاكُنَّا نقضي بهِ في الصغير ، ولؤ قضى في تركي .. لقضينا به في الهندي ، وعلمنا أنَّهُ مساوله وإذا سجد رسولُ اللهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ لسهوه في الصبح .. نعلمُ أنّ الظهر وسائر الأوقاتِ في معناه ؛ إذ اختلافُ الوقتِ نعلم قطعاً أنَّهُ لا يؤثِرُ في جبرِ النقصان . ولقد زلَّ مَنْ قالَ : إِنَّ إلحاق الأمةِ بالعبد للقرب ، فإنَّ القربَ مِنَ الجانبين على وتيرة واحدة . ولؤ وردَ نص في أنَّ الأمةَ تجبر على النكاح ـ وهو ثابت بالإجماع ... للكُنَّا لا نقول : العبد في معناه . ولكن مأخذ هذا العلم : أنَّهُ تكرر على أفهامنا مِنْ أحكام الشرع وعادته في قضيَّةِ الرقِ والعتقِ .. أنَّه يسلك بالذكر والأنثى فيه مسلكاً واحداً ؛ كما يسلك بالأبيض والأسود مسلكاً واحداً ولو لم نعرف هذا مِنْ عادتِهِ بطولِ ممارسة أحكام العتق والرق . لكانَ لا يتضحُ ذلكَ ألبتةَ ، ولمْ يظهر أنَّه يسلك بالذكر مسلك
The consideration of it being estimated is not dropped. And likewise, whoever adds to its attachment increases its deletion. The two methods of attachment: Since you know this, know that attachment has two methods: by the original in the legal ruling. One of them is that the one attaching does not engage except in deleting the distinguishing attribute between the attached and the attached-to. As for the common cause, he does not engage with it at all, yet his attachment is valid. The degrees of attachment: This has three degrees: by deleting [؟] priority of the ruling in the attached, equality between the attached and the attached-to. The highest of them is that the ruling in the attached is more deserving: as when you say: A Bedouin had intercourse with his wife, so expiation is incumbent on him. Then one who commits fornication is more deserving that it be incumbent on him, because the difference between intercourse with one's wife and with a stranger is that it is lawful, and this is more deserving of dropping and deletion in the obligation of expiation than consideration. The second degree is that it be by way of equality: as the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Whoever frees a share he has in a slave, it is evaluated against him.' So we say: The slave woman is likewise, without our specifying here the cause of the transmission of emancipation, just as we did not specify in the previous example the cause of the obligation of expiation. But we know that there is no difference except femininity, and femininity has no influence in matters pertaining to the ruling of slavery and emancipation. Rather, that is as if the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, had ruled on emancipation for a large boy; we would then judge the same for a small one. And if he had ruled for a Turk, we would judge the same for an Indian, and we would know that it is its equal. And when the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, prostrated for forgetfulness in the morning prayer, we know that noon and the rest of the times are in its meaning, because the difference of time we know definitely does not affect the compensation of deficiency. And one who said that attaching the slave woman to the slave due to proximity has indeed erred, for proximity from both sides is on a single course. And if a text had come that the slave woman is compelled to marry — and it is established by consensus — we would not say: The slave is in its meaning. Rather, the source of this knowledge is that repeated in our minds from the rulings of the Sharia and its custom in the matter of slavery and emancipation is that it treats male and female in it equally, just as it treats black and white equally. And if we had not known this from His custom by long practice of the rulings of emancipation and slavery, that would not be clear at all, and it would not appear that He treats the male the same.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the methods and degrees of analogical reasoning (ilhaq) in Islamic law. The author, likely Ibn Taymiyya, begins by stating a principle: the consideration of the attribute being estimated is not dropped; adding to the attachment increases its deletion. He then introduces the two methods of ilhaq. The first method is that the one performing the analogy (mulhiq) only deletes the distinguishing attribute (wasf fariq) between the original case (mulhaq bihi) and the new case (mulhaq), without explicitly stating the common cause ('illa). Despite not stating the cause, the analogy is valid. The passage then mentions that this method has three degrees, though the OCR is corrupt at this point. The degrees listed are: priority of the ruling in the attached (awlawiyya), equality (musawa), and a third possibly 'by deleting priority' or another. The highest degree is when the ruling in the new case is more deserving (awla), illustrated by the example of a Bedouin who had intercourse with his wife, requiring expiation, whereas one who commits fornication is even more deserving of expiation because the difference (lawful vs. unlawful) is more deserving of being dropped. The second degree is equality, illustrated by the hadith on setting free a share in a slave; the analogy extends to a female slave (umm walad) because the only difference is femininity, which does not affect the ruling. The author then argues against those who claim the analogy of the female slave to the male slave is due to proximity (qurb), noting it is based on the custom of Sharia treating male and female equally in slavery/emancipation rulings, just as it treats black and white equally. The passage concludes that this knowledge is acquired through repeated practice of Sharia rulings, not through mere causal reasoning.

الوصف الفارق
العلة الجامعة
الأولوية
المساواة

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's method of ilhaq allows analogical reasoning without explicit 'illa, relying on deleting irrelevant differences, and recognizes three degrees of strength: priority, equality, and a lower degree.

Three Types of Definition: Lafzi, Rasmi, and Haqiqi
Author defines three kinds of hadd and outlines their conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of definition (lafzi, rasmi, haqiqi) and state the condition for a real definition.

Core Idea
Definitions are of three kinds: verbal, descriptive, and real—each serves a different epistemic purpose.

In this lesson, we examine the author's classification of definitions into three types: verbal, descriptive, and real. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly analysing definitions in Islamic sciences, where confusing a descriptive definition with a real one can lead to errors in reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of definition
The author lists three types: hadd lafzi (verbal), hadd rasmi (descriptive), and hadd haqiqi (real). Notice that each serves a different purpose.
2
Distinguish the purpose of each type
The verbal definition just explains the word. The descriptive definition distinguishes the thing using accidents (e.g., khamr's frothing). The real definition seeks the essence (e.g., khamr as intoxicating grape juice).
3
Recognize the condition for a real definition
The author states that a real definition must include all essential properties (jami' al-dhatiyyat). The example of animal shows that 'body' alone is incomplete; both 'body' and 'moving by will' are needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحد اللفظ (۱) الثاني : أن يطلب لفظاً محرّراً يتميَّرُ بِهِ المسؤول عنهُ عنْ غیره بكلام جامع مانع كيفما كانَ الكلام ، سواء كان عبارةً عَن لوازمِهِ ، أَوْ ذاتيَّاتِهِ ) : كقول القائل : ما الخمرُ ؟ أَوْ : ما حدُّ الخمرِ ؟ فيُقالُ : هوَ المائعُ الذي يقذفُ بالزبد ، ثمَّ يستحيلُ إلى الحموضة ، ويحفظ في الدن والمقصود : ألا يتعرَّضَ لذاتيَّاتِهِ ، ولكن يجمعُ مِنْ عوارضهِ ولوازمه ما يساوي بجملتِهِ الخمرَ ، بحيثُ لا يخرجُ عنه خمرٌ ، ولا يدخلُ فيهِ ما ليس بخمرٍ • والثالثُ : أن يطلب بهِ ماهيَّةَ الشيء وحقيقة ذاتِهِ : كقول القائل : ما الخمرُ ؟ فيُقالُ : ( هو شراب مسكر معتصَرٌ مِنَ العنب ) فيكون ذلك كاشفاً عنْ كُنْهِ حقيقتِهِ الذاتيّةِ ، ويتبعُهُ أيضاً أنَّهُ تمييز جامع مانع ، ولكن ليس المقصود التمييز ، بل تصورُ كُنْه الشيء وحقيقتِهِ ، ثمَّ التمييز يتبعه لا محالة . واسم الحدّ في العادةِ قد يُطلق على هذه الأجوبة الثلاثة على والرسمي والحقيقي سبيل الاشتراك ، فلنخترع لكل واحد اسماً : اللفظ ولنسم الأوّلَ : حداً لفظياً ؛ إذ السائل ليسَ يطلبُ إِلَّا شرحَ ولنسم الثاني : حدّاً رسمياً ؛ إذ هو طلب مترسّم بالعلم غيرِ متشوّف إلى دَرَكِ حقيقةِ الشيء . (۱) في ( د ) : ( ذاتياً له ) بدل ( ذاتياته ) . ۱۸٠ ولنسم الثالث : حداً حقيقياً ؛ إذ مطلبُ الطالبِ فِيهِ دَرَكُ حقيقة الشيء . * (1) الحقيقي وهذا الثالثُ شرطُهُ : أن يكون مشتملاً على جميع ذاتيات تمثيل للفرق بين الشيء ؛ فإِنَّهُ لَوْ سُئِلَ عَنْ حدِ الحيوانِ ، فقال : جسمٌ .. فقد فقد والر جاءَ بوصفٍ كافٍ لوْ كانَ ذلك كافياً في الجمع والمنعِ ، ولكنَّه ناقص ، بلْ حقُّهُ أن يضيف إليهِ : ( المتحرّك بالإرادةِ ) فإِنَّ كُنْهَ حقيقة الحيوان يدركُهُ العقلُ بمجموع الأمرينِ . وأمَّا المترسّمُ الطالب للتمييز .. فيكفيه قولُكَ : ( حسّان ) وإن لم تقل : ( جسم ) أيضاً .
The verbal definition (1) Second: that one requests a precise wording by which the respondent is distinguished from others by a comprehensive and exclusive statement, whatever the statement may be, whether it is about its necessary attributes or its essential attributes: as when one says: 'What is khamr?' or 'What is the definition of khamr?' So it is said: 'It is the liquid that froths, then turns to sourness, and is stored in a jar.' The intention: not to touch upon its essentials, but to gather from its accidents and necessary attributes what equals in its totality khamr, such that no khamr is excluded and nothing that is not khamr enters into it. Third: that one requests thereby the quiddity of the thing and the reality of its essence: as when one says: 'What is khamr?' So it is said: 'It is an intoxicating drink pressed from grapes.' So that reveals the core of its essential reality, and it is also followed by a distinguishing property that is comprehensive and exclusive, but the intended purpose is not distinction, rather the conception of the core of the thing and its reality, then the distinction follows necessarily. And the term 'definition' in common usage may be applied to these three answers, namely the descriptive and the real, by way of homonymy, so let us invent a name for each: Let us call the first: verbal definition; since the questioner only requests explanation of the utterance. Let us call the second: descriptive definition; since it is a request marking by knowledge not aspiring to grasp the reality of the thing. (1) In (D): 'essential to it' instead of 'its essentials'. 180 Let us call the third: real definition; since the request of the student therein is the grasp of the reality of the thing. * (1) The real. And this third, its condition is: that it includes all the essentials. An example showing the difference between the thing: for if one were asked about the definition of animal and said 'body'... he has indeed brought a sufficient description if that were sufficient in comprehensiveness and exclusiveness, but it is deficient. Rather, it is right that he add to it: 'moving by will', for the core of the reality of animal is perceived by the intellect through the combination of the two. As for the one who marks the request for distinction, your saying 'sensitive' suffices him, even if you do not also say 'body'.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of definition (hadd) in Islamic logic. The author begins by explaining the second type (the first is not detailed here, probably the verbal definition itself). The second type is a request for a precise wording that distinguishes the object from others by a comprehensive and exclusive description, using either essential or accidental properties. The example given is the definition of khamr (wine) as 'the liquid that froths, turns sour, and is stored in a jar' – this uses accidental properties, not the essence. The third type seeks the quiddity and essence itself, as in 'khamr is an intoxicating drink pressed from grapes.' The author then notes that the term 'definition' is commonly applied to all three, but for clarity he assigns specific names. The first is 'verbal definition' (hadd lafzi) – merely explaining the word. The second is 'descriptive definition' (hadd rasmi) – a request for a marker, not aiming at the true reality. The third is 'real definition' (hadd haqiqi) – aiming at the core of the thing. The condition for a real definition is that it includes all essential properties. For example, defining animal requires both 'body' and 'moving by will' because the intellect grasps the reality only through the combination. In contrast, a descriptive definition suffices with a single accidental property like 'sensitive' (hassas). This demonstrates the difference in depth between descriptive and real definitions.

Key Terms
الحد اللفظي
الحد الرسمي
الحد الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main difference between a descriptive definition (hadd rasmi) and a real definition (hadd haqiqi)?
A) The descriptive definition uses only properties that are essential, while the real definition uses accidents.
B) The descriptive definition is based on Allah's names, while the real definition is from human intellect.
C) The descriptive definition aims only at distinguishing the thing by its accidents or necessary properties, while the real definition aims at revealing the essence and includes all essential properties.
D) The descriptive definition is limited to inanimate objects, while the real definition applies to living beings.

Key Takeaway
A real definition must include all essential properties of the thing to reveal its quiddity, while a descriptive definition suffices with accidental or necessary attributes for mere distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What Is Mantiq?

Understanding the Real Definition and Its Conditions
Author defines the real definition and its conditions, and relates other questions to the 'whether' demand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions of a real definition and distinguish it from other types of demands (matalib) in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A real definition must include all essential properties, not just a distinguishing mark.

In this passage, the author classifies the types of questions seekers ask and focuses on the 'real definition' (hadd haqiqi) as the third demand. He explains that to capture the reality of a thing, a definition must include all its essential properties (dhatiyyat). By analyzing his example of 'animal', you will learn how to evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third demand
The author names the third type of demand as the 'real definition'. The seeker's goal here is to attain the reality (haqiqa) of the thing. Recognise that this is distinct from other demands like 'why' or 'which'.
2
Find the condition of a real definition
The author states that a real definition must include 'all the essential properties that distinguish the thing'. Essential properties (dhatiyyat) are those without which the thing would not be what it is.
3
Apply the condition to an example
Consider the definition of 'animal'. The author says saying only 'body' is insufficient because it does not exclude plants and minerals. The complete definition requires adding 'voluntarily moving'. This combination of genus (body) and differentia (voluntarily moving) gives the essence.
For mere distinction (tamyiz), a single property like 'sentient' may suffice, but for a real definition all essentials are needed.
4
Contrast with other demands
The author lists other demands: 'why' asks for a cause and is answered by demonstration; 'which' distinguishes a known totality; and other forms like 'how', 'where', 'when' reduce to the 'whether' demand seeking an attribute. Notice that each demand has its proper answer type.
5
Relate to the classification of questions
Understand that every scientific investigation can be framed as one of these demands. The real definition answers the 'what is it?' question comprehensively, while other questions probe different aspects of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنسم الثالث : حداً حقيقياً ؛ إذ مطلبُ الطالبِ فِيهِ دَرَكُ حقيقة الشيء . * (1) الحقيقي وهذا الثالثُ شرطُهُ : أن يكون مشتملاً على جميع ذاتيات تمثيل للفرق بين الشيء ؛ فإِنَّهُ لَوْ سُئِلَ عَنْ حدِ الحيوانِ ، فقال : جسمٌ .. فقد فقد والر جاءَ بوصفٍ كافٍ لوْ كانَ ذلك كافياً في الجمع والمنعِ ، ولكنَّه ناقص ، بلْ حقُّهُ أن يضيف إليهِ : ( المتحرّك بالإرادةِ ) فإِنَّ كُنْهَ حقيقة الحيوان يدركُهُ العقلُ بمجموع الأمرينِ . وأمَّا المترسّمُ الطالب للتمييز .. فيكفيه قولُكَ : ( حسّان ) وإن لم تقل : ( جسم ) أيضاً . وأمَّا المطلب الثالثُ : فمطلبُ ( لِمَ ) : وهو سؤالٌ عَنِ العلة ، مطلب (٢)- وجوابه بالبرهان ، وقد سبق ( بطل وأمَّا الرابع : فهو مطلبُ ) أي :( : وهو الذي يُطلب به تمييز ما ( أتي ) عُرِفَ جملتُهُ عمَّا اختلط بهِ ؛ كما إذا قيلَ : ما الشجرُ ؟ فقلت : إِنَّهُ جسم ، فيبقى أن يُقالَ : أَيُّ جسم هوَ ؟ فنقول : هوَ نامِ . وأمَّا مطلب ( كيف ) و ( أينَ ) و ( متى ) وسائر صيغ السؤال فداخل في مطلب ( هل ) والمطلوب به صفة الموجود . غالب المطالــ - عدا ما ذكر - راجع لمطلب ( هل )
And let us name the third: a real definition; because the seeker's demand in it is to attain the reality of the thing. This third's condition is that it includes all the essential properties that distinguish the thing; for if he were asked about the definition of animal, and he said 'body'... he has come with a sufficient description if that were sufficient in inclusion and exclusion, but it is deficient. Rather, he must add to it 'voluntarily moving', for the essence of the reality of animal is grasped by the intellect through the combination of the two. As for the one who seeks distinction, your saying 'sentient' suffices him, even if you do not also say 'body'. As for the third demand, it is the demand for 'why', which is a question about the cause, and its answer is by demonstration, and it has preceded. And as for the fourth, it is the demand for 'which', which is that by which is sought the distinction of what whose totality is known from what is mixed with it; as when it is said 'what is tree?' and you say 'it is a body', then it remains to be said 'which body is it?' and we say 'it is growing'. And as for the demand for 'how', 'where', 'when', and all other forms of question, they are included in the demand for 'whether', and what is sought by them is the attribute of the existent. Most demands, except what is mentioned, refer back to the demand for 'whether'.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage classifies the different types of questions (matalib) and their appropriate answers, focusing on the real definition (hadd haqiqi). The author begins by naming the third type of demand as 'real definition', where the seeker aims to grasp the reality of the thing. He then explains its condition: it must include all essential properties (dhatiyyat) that distinguish the thing from others. For example, if someone asks for the definition of 'animal', saying 'body' alone is incomplete because it does not exclude plants and minerals. One must add 'voluntarily moving' (mutaharrik bi'l-irada) to cover the essential difference. However, for someone merely seeking a distinguishing mark (tamyiz), a single property like 'sentient' (hassas) might suffice. The author then lists other demands: the demand for 'why' (lima) which asks for the cause and is answered by demonstration (burhan); the demand for 'which' (ayy) which distinguishes a known totality from what it might be mixed with (e.g., asking 'which body' after knowing something is a body); and finally, the demands for 'how', 'where', 'when', etc., which all fall under the demand for 'whether' (hal) and seek an attribute of the existent. The passage concludes by stating that most demands revert to the question of 'whether'.

Key Takeaway
A real definition (hadd haqiqi) must include all essential properties (dhatiyyat) that constitute the thing's reality, not merely a distinguishing mark.

What Is Mantiq?

Distinguishing Essential from Accidental for Real Definition
Author states the need to distinguish essential and accidental attributes for real definition, then divides the essential into genus and species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential (dhati), necessary (lazim), and accidental (aradi) attributes, and identify genus and species in a real definition.

Core Idea
Real definition requires separating what constitutes quiddity from what merely accompanies it.

This passage establishes a critical principle for the logician: before constructing a real definition, you must be able to separate the essential attributes that make a thing what it is from the accidental ones that simply accompany it. The author contrasts three types of definitions—verbal, descriptive, and real—and stresses that only the real definition penetrates to the quiddity (mahiyya). Understanding the division of essential attributes into genus and species is the foundation of all precise definition in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of attributes
The author mentions essential (dhati), necessary (lazim), and accidental (aradi) attributes. For a given concept, list attributes that belong to its reality (essential), those that necessarily accompany it (necessary), and those that are incidental (accidental). For example, for 'human', 'rational' is essential, 'laughing' is accidental.
Note that 'necessary' attributes are distinct from essential, as they are not constitutive but inseparable.
2
Distinguish the three types of definition
The author enumerates: verbal definition (hadd lafzi – suits a language beginner), descriptive definition (rasmi – easy, merely provides inclusion/exclusion), and real definition (hadd haqiqi – difficult but precious, expresses the quiddity). Recognize which type is being used in a given instance.
Only real definition requires deep logical insight.
3
Understand that only essential attributes answer 'what is it?'
The author states that the answer to 'what is it?' (ma huwa) must consist solely of essential attributes (dhati). For the concept 'prayer' (salat) in fiqh, the essential attributes include intention and the specific actions; the fact that it is performed at certain times is accidental.
This is the key to knowing which attributes belong in a real definition.
4
Divide the essential into general and specific
The essential (dhati) is divided into the general (dhati 'amm) called genus (jins), and the specific (dhati khass) called species (naw'). For 'human', the genus is 'animal' and the species (specific essential) is 'rational'. The genus is broader, the species is narrower and distinguishes the thing within the genus.
This division is the basis for the Porphyrian tree used in Islamic logic.
5
Identify the highest genus (jins al-ajnas)
If a genus has no higher genus above it, it is called 'genus of genera' (jins al-ajnas). Example: 'substance' (jawhar) in metaphysics is often considered the highest genus for material things in Islamic philosophy.
This concept helps in building the hierarchy of genera and species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله الله القانون الثاني في ضرورة التفريق بين الذاتيات والعرضيات (۱) أنَّ الحادَّ ينبغي أن يكونَ بصيراً بالفرْقِ بين الصفات الذاتية واللازمةِ والعَرَضِيةِ كما ذكرناه في الفنِ الأَوَّلِ من الكتاب أعني به : طالب الحد الحقيقي . (۲) أمَّا الأَوَّلُ اللفظي .. فيليقُ بساذج اللغة (٢) ، وأما الرسمي .. فمؤنته قليلةٌ ، والأمرُ فيهِ سهل ؛ فإنَّ طالبه قانع بالجمع والمنع بأي لفظ كانَ ، وإنَّما العويص العزيز الحد الذي سميناه : حقيقياً ، بیان معنى الماهية وليس ذلكَ إلا ذكر كمال المعاني التي بها قوامُ ماهيَّة الشيء وأعني بالماهيّةِ : ما يطلبُ القائلُ بقولِهِ : ( ما هوَ ) فإنَّ هذهِ صيغة طالب لحقيقة الشيء ، فلا يدخلُ في جواب الماهيَّةِ إِلَّا الذاتي الذات والذاتي ينقسم : إلى عام ، ويُسمَّى : جنساً . وإلى خاص ، ويُسمَّى : نوعاً فإنْ كانَ الذاتي العام لا أعم منه . . سُمِّيَ : جنس الأجناس .
Allah, Allah. The second principle on the necessity of distinguishing between essential and accidental attributes: (1) That the definer should be insightful about the difference between essential, necessary, and accidental attributes, as we mentioned in the first section of the book, meaning: the seeker of the real definition. (2) As for the first, the verbal [definition], it suits the simpleton of language; and as for the descriptive [definition], its burden is light and the matter is easy, for its seeker is content with inclusion and exclusion by any wording; but the difficult and precious definition is the one we called 'real': the expression of the meaning of quiddity (mahiyya), and that is nothing but the mention of the complete meanings by which the quiddity of the thing is constituted. And by quiddity I mean what the speaker seeks by his saying 'what is it?' For this is the formula of the seeker of the reality of the thing, so nothing enters into the answer of the quiddity except the essential. The essential (dhati) is divided: into general, called genus (jins), and into specific, called species (naw'). If the general essential has nothing more general than it, it is called the genus of genera (jins al-ajnas).
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage articulates the second principle: the necessity of distinguishing between essential (dhati), necessary (lazim), and accidental (aradi) attributes for the purpose of real definition. The author emphasizes that a definer must be insightful about these differences. He then contrasts three types of definition: the verbal (lafzi) definition, which suits the simple language user; the descriptive (rasmi) definition, which is easy and satisfies the seeker with mere inclusion and exclusion; and the real definition (hadd haqiqi), which is difficult but precious. The real definition is the expression of the quiddity (mahiyya) — the answer to 'what is it?'. Only essential properties (dhati) can enter into the answer to quiddity. The essential (dhati) is then divided into two: the general essential (al-dhati al-'amm), called genus (jins), and the specific essential (al-dhati al-khass), called species (naw'). If the general essential has nothing more general than it, it is called the genus of genera (jins al-ajnas), i.e., the highest genus.

جنس الأجناس

A) Any definition that includes inclusion and exclusion.
B) The expression of the quiddity by mentioning the complete essential meanings.
C) The verbal definition appropriate for the simpleton.
D) A definition that uses only the species.

Key Takeaway
The real definition of a thing must consist of its essential attributes (dhati), which are divided into general (genus) and specific (species); only these answer the question 'what is it?'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Determine whether the definition given by classical jurists (e.g., 'washing certain limbs with intention') is a real definition or a descriptive one.

The First Condition of Real Definition: Genus and Differentiae
Author introduces the third canon and presents first condition: gather all parts from genus and differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in a definition and verify that all essential parts are included.

Core Idea
A real definition requires both genus and differentia; omitting either fails to capture the essence.

The author introduces the third canon of logic, which concerns the proper construction of a definition. He emphasizes that a real definition must include both the broader category (genus) and the specific distinguishing feature (differentia). Without these, the definition is merely nominal. This lesson unpacks the first condition: gather all parts from genus and differentiae.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish real vs. verbal definition
The passage states that if you omit the required functions, the definition becomes verbal/descriptive. Recognize that a real definition must express the true essence.
2
Identify the required components
The first function is to gather the parts from the genus and the differentiae. In the example, the genus is 'body' (jism) and the differentia is 'growing' (naami).
3
Test the definition for exclusivity
If only 'body' is given, the definition applies to stones as well, so it fails. The differentia 'growing' is added to exclude non-growing things.
This shows the necessity of the differentia for precision.
4
Ensure completeness of essential attributes
The author stresses that all essential attributes (dhatiyyat) must be included, no matter how many. A real definition must fully capture the essence without omitting any necessary part.
This condition prevents incomplete definitions.

القانون الثالث في كيفية صناعة الحد أنَّ ما وقع السؤالُ عَن ماهِيَّتِهِ وأردت أن تحده حداً حقيقياً .. فعليكَ فيهِ وظائف لا يكونُ الحدُّ حقيقياً إلَّا بها ، فإن تركتها .. سميناهُ رسميّاً أو لفظياً، وخرجَ عَنْ كونِهِ مُعْرِباً عَنْ حقيقة الشيء ، ومصوّراً كُنْه معناه في نفس السائل : الحد الحقيقي الأَوَّلُ : أن تجمعَ أجزاء الحدِ مِنَ الجنس والفصول . فإذا قيلَ لكَ مشيراً إلى ما ينبتُ مِنَ الأرض : ما هوَ ؟ فلا بدَّ وأن تقول : ( جسم ) ، ولكن لو اقتصرت عليه .. بطل عليك بـ ( الحجر ) ، فتحتاجُ إلى الزيادةِ ، فتقولُ : ( نام ) ، فتحترزُ عمَّا لا ينمو ، فهذا الاحترازُ يُسَمَّى : فَضلاً ؛ إِذْ فصلْتَ بهِ المحدود عَنْ غيرِهِ . ثمَّ شرطُكَ أن تذكر جميعَ ذاتيَّاتِهِ وإِنْ كانَتْ أَلفاً ، ولا تبالي بالتطويل .
The third canon on how to make a definition: that when a question is asked about the quiddity of something and you want to define it with a real definition, then upon you are functions without which the definition is not real. If you omit them, we call it a nominal or verbal definition, and it departs from being expressive of the reality of the thing and depicting the inner meaning of it in the mind of the questioner. The first real definition: that you gather the parts of the definition from the genus and the differentiae. So if you are asked, while pointing to something that grows from the ground, 'What is it?', then you must say 'body', but if you limit yourself to it, you are refuted by 'stone', so you need the addition, so you say 'growing', thereby guarding against what does not grow. This guarding is called a differentia, because you have separated the defined from other things. Then it is your condition that you mention all of its essential properties even if they are a thousand, and pay no attention to prolixity.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage introduces the third canon of logic concerning the construction of a definition (hadd). The author begins by distinguishing a real definition (hadd haqiqi) from a nominal or verbal one. A real definition requires fulfilling certain functions (waza'if); otherwise, the result is merely a verbal description that does not convey the true essence. The first function is to assemble all the parts of the definition from the genus (jins) and the differentiae (fusul). The author illustrates with an example: when asked about a growing thing from the earth, the proper definition begins with the genus 'body' (jism), but this alone is insufficient because it would apply to stones as well. Hence, the differentia 'growing' (naami) is added to exclude non-growing things. This addition is called a differentia (fasl) because it separates the defined from others. The author further stresses that all essential attributes (dhatiyyat) must be included, no matter how many, without concern for length.

جنس
فصل

C) You have a differentia.
D) You have a verbal definition but it is still real.

Key Takeaway
A real definition must include genus and all essential differentiae to accurately represent the essence.

Why Simple Concepts Cannot Be Really Defined
Explains why simple (uncomposable) meanings lack real definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a simple concept like 'al-mawjūd' cannot have a real definition and distinguish between real definition, verbal explanation, and description.

Core Idea
A simple concept has no essential parts, so it cannot be defined by ḥadd ḥaqīqī.

This passage tackles a fundamental puzzle in logic: How can you define something that has no parts? The author introduces the sixth principle, stating that a meaning with absolutely no composition — like 'existence' — cannot be given a real definition. Instead, we can only explain it by offering a synonym or describing its necessary concomitants. Understanding this principle prevents us from seeking essential definitions where none exist, which is critical for rigorous reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author states that a simple meaning (المعنى المفرد) — one with no composition — cannot be defined by a real definition (حد حقيقي). Locate this claim in the opening sentences of the Arabic.
The claim is explicit in the first line: 'أن المعنى الذي لا تركيب فيه ألبتة لا يمكن حده إلا بطريق شرح اللفظ، أو بطريق الرسم، وأما الحد الحقيقي فلا'.
2
Distinguish the two alternative methods
Note the two methods that are possible: verbal explanation (شرح اللفظ) and description (الرسم). Verbal explanation is simply substituting a more familiar synonym. Description uses non-essential attributes (necessary concomitants or accidents).
3
Examine the example of 'al-mawjūd'
The author takes 'the existent' (الموجود) as a paradigm simple concept. When asked for its definition, one can only say 'a thing' or 'the established' — a synonym. If one tries to define it by saying 'the known' or 'the mentioned' with a condition to exclude the non-existent, that is a description (رسم), not a real definition.
This shows that even a plausible definition of a simple concept ends up either as a synonym swap or as a mention of non-essential attributes.
4
Analyze the deeper reason
The author explains that a real definition requires composition of essential attributes (ذاتيات). For example, blackness (السواد) has attributes like being a color (essential), being known (necessary but non-essential), and being bright (accidental). A real definition would compile the essential attributes from the most general to the most specific. But a simple concept like existence has no such composition of essential attributes — it is the starting point of all explanation. Therefore, no real definition is possible.
The analogy of defining a house by its sides (جهات) illustrates that definition requires multiple boundaries; a simple concept has none.
5
Apply the criterion
Test any concept you encounter: does it have multiple essential attributes that compose its essence? If yes, a real definition is possible (e.g., 'human' = 'rational animal'). If not, like 'existence' or 'oneness', it can only be verbally explained or described.
This criterion is crucial for distinguishing between definable and indefinable terms in Islamic logic.

العجز عن
المفرد
١٩٧
The simple meaning.
The sixth principle: On that the simple meaning is [defined] only by verbal explanation or by description, and not by real definition.
That the meaning which has absolutely no composition cannot be defined except by way of verbal explanation or by way of description. As for real definition — no.
The simple meaning is like 'the existent'. So if it is said to you: 'What is the definition of the existent?', you have no way to it except that you say: 'It is a thing' or 'the established', so you would have substituted a name with a synonymous name. Perhaps they are equal in conveying understanding, and perhaps one of them is more obscure in linguistic usage, like one who says: 'What is al-‘uqār?' and we say: 'It is wine', and 'What is al-qaswarah?' and we say: 'It is the lion.' And this too is only good on the condition that what is mentioned in the answer is more well-known to the questioner than what is mentioned in the question. Then it is nothing but a verbal explanation. Otherwise, whoever seeks a summary of the essence of the lion — it is not summarized in his mind except by saying: 'A beast with such-and-such characteristics.' As for repeating synonymous expressions — it does not suffice him.
And if you said: 'The definition of the existent is that it is the known or the mentioned', and you restricted it with a condition by which you guarded against the non-existent — you would have mentioned something of its necessary concomitants and corollaries. Then your definition would be a descriptive definition, not expressing the essence, so it is not real.
Therefore, the existent has no definition; for it is the starting point of every explanation, so how can it be explained in itself?
Explanation of why the simple meaning has no real definition.
And we only said: The simple meaning has no real definition, because the meaning of one's saying 'What is the definition of the thing?' is like your saying 'What is the definition of this house?' And the house has multiple sides to which the boundaries extend, so the delimitation of the house is by mentioning its united and multiple sides, by which — I mean the house — is enclosed and bounded. So if it is said: 'What is the definition of blackness?' it is as if one asks thereby about the meanings and realities whose combination completes the reality of blackness. For blackness is blackness, color, existent, accident, visible, known, described, mentioned, one, many, bright, glossy, dull, and other such attributes with which it is described. Some of these attributes are accidental and disappear; some are necessary and do not disappear but are not essential — like its being known, one, and many; and some are essential, without understanding which blackness cannot be conceived — like its being a color. So the seeker of definition is as if saying: To how many meanings do the boundaries of the reality of blackness terminate? So that we may collect those multiple meanings for him in a summary: by beginning with the most general and ending with the most specific, without touching on accidents. And perhaps he also demands that one not touch on necessary concomitants either, but only essences. So if the meaning is not composed of multiple essential attributes like existence — how can its definition be conceived? And the question about it is like one's saying: 'What is the definition?' — a wonderful example illustrating the inability to define the simple.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage explains the sixth principle regarding the definition of simple (uncomposable) concepts. The author begins by stating that a meaning which has absolutely no composition (لا تركيب فيه ألبتة) cannot be given a real definition (حد حقيقي). The only possible ways are either verbal explanation (شرح اللفظ) — substituting a synonymous term — or description (رسم) — mentioning non-essential attributes. The author then provides examples: the simple meaning is like 'the existent' (الموجود). If asked to define it, one can only say 'a thing' or 'the established', which is merely a synonym. Similarly, 'al-‘uqār' is defined as 'wine', and 'al-qaswarah' as 'lion'. This is only beneficial if the answer is better known than the question. The author then critiques this method: if one truly wants to grasp the essence of a lion, repeating synonyms is useless; one must describe it as a predator with specific qualities. Next, the author argues that if one tries to define 'the existent' as 'the known' or 'the mentioned' with a condition to exclude the non-existent, that is a descriptive definition (رسمي), not a real definition (حقيقي), because it mentions necessary concomitants (لوازم) rather than essence (ذات). Therefore, the existent has no real definition because it is the starting point of all explanation. The author then provides a deeper explanation: asking for the definition of a simple thing is like asking for the bounds of a house — the house has many sides that define it. Similarly, blackness (السواد) has multiple attributes: some accidental (like brightness), some necessary but not essential (like being known), and some essential (like being a color). The real definition seeks to compile the essential attributes (الذاتيات) from the most general to the most specific, excluding accidents and even necessary concomitants. But if a meaning like existence has no such composition of essential attributes, it cannot be defined. The author concludes with an analogy: the question about defining a simple concept is itself a demonstration of the impossibility — a excellent example illustrating the incapacity to define a simple term.

Key Takeaway
A simple (uncomposable) concept cannot have a real definition because it lacks the multiple essential attributes needed to define it; only verbal explanation or description is possible.

The Homonymy Trap in Defining 'Hadd'
Author identifies the root cause of false disputes in defining 'hadd'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a dispute about a definition arises from homonymy (ism mushtarak) rather than from actual disagreement.

Core Idea
Agreement on the subject is a prerequisite for genuine disagreement.

This passage addresses a common logical error: treating two different definitions as competing when they actually refer to different things that share a name. The author illustrates this with examples from Islamic legal and theological discourse, showing that the third party's error is a failure to recognize homonymy. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to detect false disputes in scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions on defining 'hadd'
Read the first part of the Arabic: one group says definition is the essence itself, another says it is an explanatory word that includes and excludes, and a third treats this as a real conflict. Mark which opinions the author critiques and why.
2
Analyze the author's critique of the third opinion
The author points out that disagreement is only possible when both parties refer to the same thing. But here, the first two definitions are not about the same subject—they are homonyms. Note the phrase: 'they did not converge on a single thing.'
3
Examine the homonymy examples
The author gives examples: 'al-mukhtar' can mean the one with free will or the one left to his own opinion; 'al-ayn' can mean the eye or gold coin. Both are distinct realities sharing a name. Relate this back to the definitions of 'hadd'.
4
Extract the rule (qa'ida)
The author concludes: 'Understand this, for it is a very beneficial rule.' The rule is that false disputes arise from ignoring homonymy. State this rule in your own words.

الفصل الثاني من محاك الحد في الامتحان الأول في تعريف الحد
اختلف الناسُ في حد ( الحد ) :
فمِنْ قائل يقولُ : حدُّ الشيء : هوَ حقيقته ونفسه وذاته .
ومِنْ قائل يقولُ : حدُّ الشيء : هوَ اللفظ المفسِّرُ لمعناه على وجه يجمع ويمنعُ .
ومِنْ قائل ثالث : يقدِرُ هذه مسألة خلافية ، فينصر أحد الحدين على الآخر ! وانظرْ كيف تخبَّطَ عقلُ هذا الثالث ، فلم يعلم أنَّ الاختلاف لا يتصور الاختلاف إلا بعد التوافق . إنَّما يُتصوَّرُ بعدَ التوارد على شيءٍ واحدٍ ، وهذان قد تباينا وتباعدا ، على شيء واحد وما تواردا .
وإنَّما منشأ هذا الغلط : الذهولُ عَنْ معرفةِ الاسم المشتركِ الذي ذكرناه ، فإِنَّ مَنْ يحدُّ ( المختار ) بأنَّهُ القادر على تركِ الشيء وفعله .. ليس مخالفاً لمَنْ يحدُّهُ بأنَّهُ الذي خُلِّيَ ورأيَهُ ، كما أَنَّ مَنْ حدَّ ( العينَ ) بأنَّهُ العضو المدركُ للألوان بالرؤية .. لم يخالف مَنْ حدَّ ( العينَ ) بأنَّهُ الجوهرُ المعدني الذي هو أشرفُ النقودِ ، بل حد هذا أمراً مبايناً لحقيقة الأمر الآخر ، وإنَّما اشتركا في اسم ( العين ، والمختار ) فافهم هذا ؛ فإنَّهُ قانون كثير النفع .
فإن قلت : فما الصحيح عندَكَ في حدّ الحد ؟ فاعلمْ : أَنَّ كلَّ مَنْ طلب المعاني مِنَ الألفاظ .. ضاع وهلكَ وكانَ كمَن استدبر المغرب وهو يطلبُهُ ، ومَنْ قَرَّرَ المعاني أَوَّلاً في عقلِهِ بلا لفظ ، ثمّ أتبعَ المعاني الألفاظ .. فقد اهتدى .

مراتب الوجود
فلنقرّر المعاني ، فنقولُ : الشيءُ له في الوجودِ أربع مراتبَ :
الأولى : حقيقة في نفسه .
الثانية : ثبوتُ مثالِ حقيقتِهِ في الذهنِ ، وهو الذي يعبر عنه بالعلم .
الثالثةُ : تأليف مثالِهِ بحروف تدلُّ عليه ، وهوَ العبارة الدالة على المثال الذي في النفس .
والرابعةُ : تأليف رقوم تُدرَكُ بحاسة البصر دالة على اللفظ ، وهي الكتابة ، والكتابة تبعُ اللفظ ؛ إذ تدلُّ عليه ، واللفظ تبعُ العلم ؛ إذْ يدلُّ عليه ، والعلمُ تَبعُ المعلوم ؛ إذْ يطابقه ويوافقه . وهذهِ الأربعةُ متطابقةٌ متوازيةٌ ، إلَّا أَنَّ الأَوَّلين وجودانِ حقيقيانِ
The second chapter of the examination of definition, in the first test on defining 'hadd'.
People differed regarding the definition of 'hadd':
One group says: The definition of a thing is its reality, itself, and its essence.
Another group says: The definition of a thing is the word that explains its meaning in a way that includes and excludes.
A third group considers this a controversial issue and champions one of the two definitions over the other! Look how confused the mind of this third person is—he did not know that disagreement is only conceivable after agreement. Indeed, it is only conceivable after converging on a single thing, but these two have diverged and are far apart; they did not converge on a single thing.
The source of this error is forgetfulness concerning the knowledge of the homonym (al-ism al-mushtarak) that we mentioned. For the one who defines 'the chosen one' (al-mukhtar) as 'the one capable of abandoning or doing a thing' is not contradicting the one who defines it as 'the one left to his own opinion'. Similarly, the one who defines 'the eye' (al-'ayn) as 'the organ that perceives colors by sight' is not contradicting the one who defines 'the eye' as 'the metallic substance that is the noblest of coins'. Rather, each defined something distinct from the reality of the other, and they only share the name 'al-'ayn' and 'al-mukhtar'. So understand this, for it is a very beneficial rule.
If you ask: So what is correct in your view regarding the definition of 'hadd'? Know that whoever seeks meanings from words is lost and ruined, like one who faces away from the west while seeking it. But whoever first establishes the meanings in his mind without words, then follows the meanings with words—he has been guided.

The levels of existence:
Let us establish the meanings, and say: A thing has four levels of existence:
First: its reality in itself.
Second: the establishment of a form of its reality in the mind, which is called knowledge.
Third: the composition of its form with letters that indicate it, which is the expression indicating the form in the soul.
Fourth: the composition of marks perceived by the sense of sight that indicate the word, and that is writing. Writing is subordinate to speech, for it indicates it; speech is subordinate to knowledge, for it indicates it; and knowledge is subordinate to the known, for it corresponds and agrees with it. These four are parallel and aligned, except that the first two are real existences.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage defines and critiques the concept of 'hadd' (definition) and then provides the correct methodology. The author begins by presenting three opinions: (1) definition as the essence itself, (2) definition as an explanatory word that includes all instances (jami') and excludes non-instances (mani'), and (3) a misguided third view that treats the two definitions as competing doctrines. The author harshly criticizes the third view, pointing out that disagreement presupposes a shared subject; the first two definitions are not about the same thing—they are homonyms. The author illustrates with examples from Islamic theology and law: 'al-mukhtar' (the chosen one) can refer to the one with free will (qadir) or the one left to his own devices (mukhayyā), and 'al-'ayn' (the eye) can refer to the physical organ or gold coin. These are different realities sharing a name. Thus, the dispute is not real but arises from ignoring homonymy (al-ism al-mushtarak).
Next, the author answers the question of the correct definition of 'hadd' itself. The key principle: do not seek meanings from words; rather, first fix meanings in the mind without words, then attach words to those meanings. This is the path of guidance. To illustrate, the author presents the four levels of existence (maratib al-wujud): (1) external reality, (2) mental concept (knowledge), (3) spoken expression, (4) written script. These four are parallel and correspond, but the first two are real existences (external and mental), while speech and writing are derivative. The hierarchy: writing points to speech, speech points to knowledge, knowledge points to the known. This shows that definitions are ultimately of mental concepts, not of words.

The Four Levels of Existence and the Correct Method of Definition
Author presents the four levels of existence as the foundation for correct definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to arrange the four levels of existence in order and explain why definitions should be built from mental concepts first.

Core Idea
Meanings are prior to words; seek definitions from the mind, not from dictionary entries.

After critiquing the false dispute, the author presents the correct methodology: begin with the meaning in the mind, then attach words to it. To illustrate, he introduces the four levels of existence: external reality, mental concept (knowledge), spoken expression, and written script. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for constructing sound definitions and avoiding the error of 'seeking meanings from words.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the four levels of existence
Read the Arabic list: (1) reality itself, (2) its form in the mind (knowledge), (3) spoken expression indicating that form, (4) written script indicating the speech. Note the order and the relationships.
2
Understand the hierarchy
The author explains: writing depends on speech, speech depends on knowledge, knowledge depends on the known (external reality). This is a causal chain. The first two levels (external and mental) are 'real existences' (wujud haqiqi), while the latter two are derivative.
3
Apply the principle to definition
The author's methodological rule: 'Whoever seeks meanings from words is lost; whoever first establishes meanings in his mind without words, then follows the meanings with words, is guided.' This means definitions should be constructed from the mental concept (level 2) and then expressed in words (level 3).
4
Practice with an Islamic example
Consider the term 'salat'. Its external reality is the act of worship with specific pillars. The mental concept is the understanding of that act. The spoken definition is the verbal expression 'prayer consisting of...' The script is the written word. The correct definition starts from the mental concept, not from the dictionary.
