الفصل الثاني من محاك الحد في الامتحان الأول في تعريف الحد
اختلف الناسُ في حد ( الحد ) :
فمِنْ قائل يقولُ : حدُّ الشيء : هوَ حقيقته ونفسه وذاته .
ومِنْ قائل يقولُ : حدُّ الشيء : هوَ اللفظ المفسِّرُ لمعناه على وجه يجمع ويمنعُ .
ومِنْ قائل ثالث : يقدِرُ هذه مسألة خلافية ، فينصر أحد الحدين على الآخر ! وانظرْ كيف تخبَّطَ عقلُ هذا الثالث ، فلم يعلم أنَّ الاختلاف لا يتصور الاختلاف إلا بعد التوافق . إنَّما يُتصوَّرُ بعدَ التوارد على شيءٍ واحدٍ ، وهذان قد تباينا وتباعدا ، على شيء واحد وما تواردا .
وإنَّما منشأ هذا الغلط : الذهولُ عَنْ معرفةِ الاسم المشتركِ الذي ذكرناه ، فإِنَّ مَنْ يحدُّ ( المختار ) بأنَّهُ القادر على تركِ الشيء وفعله .. ليس مخالفاً لمَنْ يحدُّهُ بأنَّهُ الذي خُلِّيَ ورأيَهُ ، كما أَنَّ مَنْ حدَّ ( العينَ ) بأنَّهُ العضو المدركُ للألوان بالرؤية .. لم يخالف مَنْ حدَّ ( العينَ ) بأنَّهُ الجوهرُ المعدني الذي هو أشرفُ النقودِ ، بل حد هذا أمراً مبايناً لحقيقة الأمر الآخر ، وإنَّما اشتركا في اسم ( العين ، والمختار ) فافهم هذا ؛ فإنَّهُ قانون كثير النفع .
فإن قلت : فما الصحيح عندَكَ في حدّ الحد ؟ فاعلمْ : أَنَّ كلَّ مَنْ طلب المعاني مِنَ الألفاظ .. ضاع وهلكَ وكانَ كمَن استدبر المغرب وهو يطلبُهُ ، ومَنْ قَرَّرَ المعاني أَوَّلاً في عقلِهِ بلا لفظ ، ثمّ أتبعَ المعاني الألفاظ .. فقد اهتدى .

مراتب الوجود
فلنقرّر المعاني ، فنقولُ : الشيءُ له في الوجودِ أربع مراتبَ :
الأولى : حقيقة في نفسه .
الثانية : ثبوتُ مثالِ حقيقتِهِ في الذهنِ ، وهو الذي يعبر عنه بالعلم .
الثالثةُ : تأليف مثالِهِ بحروف تدلُّ عليه ، وهوَ العبارة الدالة على المثال الذي في النفس .
والرابعةُ : تأليف رقوم تُدرَكُ بحاسة البصر دالة على اللفظ ، وهي الكتابة ، والكتابة تبعُ اللفظ ؛ إذ تدلُّ عليه ، واللفظ تبعُ العلم ؛ إذْ يدلُّ عليه ، والعلمُ تَبعُ المعلوم ؛ إذْ يطابقه ويوافقه . وهذهِ الأربعةُ متطابقةٌ متوازيةٌ ، إلَّا أَنَّ الأَوَّلين وجودانِ حقيقيانِ
The second chapter of the examination of definition, in the first test on defining 'hadd'.
People differed regarding the definition of 'hadd':
One group says: The definition of a thing is its reality, itself, and its essence.
Another group says: The definition of a thing is the word that explains its meaning in a way that includes and excludes.
A third group considers this a controversial issue and champions one of the two definitions over the other! Look how confused the mind of this third person is—he did not know that disagreement is only conceivable after agreement. Indeed, it is only conceivable after converging on a single thing, but these two have diverged and are far apart; they did not converge on a single thing.
The source of this error is forgetfulness concerning the knowledge of the homonym (al-ism al-mushtarak) that we mentioned. For the one who defines 'the chosen one' (al-mukhtar) as 'the one capable of abandoning or doing a thing' is not contradicting the one who defines it as 'the one left to his own opinion'. Similarly, the one who defines 'the eye' (al-'ayn) as 'the organ that perceives colors by sight' is not contradicting the one who defines 'the eye' as 'the metallic substance that is the noblest of coins'. Rather, each defined something distinct from the reality of the other, and they only share the name 'al-'ayn' and 'al-mukhtar'. So understand this, for it is a very beneficial rule.
If you ask: So what is correct in your view regarding the definition of 'hadd'? Know that whoever seeks meanings from words is lost and ruined, like one who faces away from the west while seeking it. But whoever first establishes the meanings in his mind without words, then follows the meanings with words—he has been guided.

The levels of existence:
Let us establish the meanings, and say: A thing has four levels of existence:
First: its reality in itself.
Second: the establishment of a form of its reality in the mind, which is called knowledge.
Third: the composition of its form with letters that indicate it, which is the expression indicating the form in the soul.
Fourth: the composition of marks perceived by the sense of sight that indicate the word, and that is writing. Writing is subordinate to speech, for it indicates it; speech is subordinate to knowledge, for it indicates it; and knowledge is subordinate to the known, for it corresponds and agrees with it. These four are parallel and aligned, except that the first two are real existences.

These are different realities sharing a name. Thus, the dispute is not real but arises from ignoring homonymy (al-ism al-mushtarak).

The Four Stages of Existence and the Definition of Hadd
Author establishes four stages and derives ḥadd from manʿ

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the four stages of existence and explain why the term ḥadd is derived from manʿ.

Core Idea
Definition is not about words; it captures the exclusive reality preserved across all representations.

In this passage, the author critiques those who seek definitions from words, using the ambiguous term 'al-ʿayn' as a warning. He then establishes a foundational principle: meanings must be fixed in the mind first, then expressed in words and writing. To ground the concept of definition (ḥadd), he presents four stages of existence and shows that the exclusive property (manʿ) present in the external reality is transmitted through each stage. Understanding this helps you avoid confusing linguistic definitions with real ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four stages of existence
List the four stages: external reality (ḥaqīqa), mental image (knowledge/ʿilm), verbal expression (lafẓ), and writing (kitāba). Note their order: reality → mind → speech → script.
The first two are real and unchanging; the last two are conventional and vary.
2
Understand the correspondence between stages
Each stage corresponds to the previous: knowledge corresponds to reality, expression corresponds to knowledge, writing corresponds to expression. This chain preserves the property of exclusivity.
Correspondence means that each stage mirrors the exclusive nature of the prior stage.
3
Derive the meaning of ḥadd from manʿ
The term ḥadd is derived from manʿ (prevention). Find prevention at each stage: reality is exclusive; knowledge matches that exclusive reality; expression and writing mirror that exclusivity. Thus, a proper definition (ḥadd) is the articulation of that exclusive reality.
The author argues that definition is primarily about the reality or its mental image, not about words or writing.
4
Apply the principle to evaluate definitions
Test any definition by checking whether it captures the exclusive property of the thing (the manʿ). If it relies on linguistic convention only, it fails. For example, defining 'al-ʿayn' as 'the noblest currency' misses the real essence.
This principle is the basis for criticism in Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحريجة: فما العمدة في تعريف الحد؟ مَنْ حدَّ العينَ بأنَّهُ الجوهر المعدني الذي هو أشرفُ النقودِ، بل حد هذا أمراً مبايناً لحقيقة الأمر الآخر، وإنَّما اشتركا في اسم العين، والمختار فافهم هذا؛ فإنَّهُ قانون كثير النفع. فإن قلت: فما الصحيح عندَكَ في حدّ الحد؟ فاعلمْ: أَنَّ كلَّ مَنْ طلب المعاني مِنَ الألفاظ ضاع وهلكَ وكانَ كمَن استدبر المغرب وهو يطلبُهُ، ومَنْ قَرَّرَ المعاني أَوَّلاً في عقلِهِ بلا لفظ، ثمّ أتبعَ المعاني الألفاظ فقد اهتدى. فلنقرّر المعاني، فنقولُ: الشيءُ له في الوجودِ أربع مراتبَ: الأولى: حقيقة في نفسه. الثانية: ثبوتُ مثالِ حقيقتِهِ في الذهنِ، وهو الذي يعبر عنه بالعلم. الثالثةُ: تأليف مثالِهِ بحروف تدلُّ عليه، وهوَ العبارة الدالة على المثال الذي في النفس. والرابعةُ: تأليف رقوم تُدرَكُ بحاسة البصر دالة على اللفظ، وهي الكتابة، والكتابة تبعُ اللفظ؛ إذ تدلُّ عليه، واللفظ تبعُ العلم؛ إذْ يدلُّ عليه، والعلمُ تَبعُ المعلوم؛ إذْ يطابقه ويوافقه. وهذهِ الأربعةُ متطابقةٌ متوازيةٌ، إلَّا أَنَّ الأَوَّلين وجودانِ حقيقيانِ لا يختلفان بالأعصار والأمصار، والآخرين - وهما اللفظ والكتابة - تختلفُ بالأعصار والأمم؛ لأنَّها موضوعةٌ بالاختيار، ولكن الأوضاع وإن اختلفَتْ صورها فهي متفقةٌ في أنَّها قُصِدَ بها مطابقة الحقيقة. ومعلوم أنَّ الحدَّ مأخوذُ مِنَ المنع، وإِنَّما استُعير لهذهِ المعاني للمشاركة في معنى المنع، فانظر المنع أين تجده في هذه الأربعة. فإن ابتدأت بـ الحقيقة لم تشك في أنَّها حاصرة للشيء مخصوصةٌ بهِ؛ إذ حقيقة كلّ شيءٍ: خاصِيَّتُه التي له وليست لغيره، فإذاً الحقيقة جامعة مانعة. فإن نظرتَ إلى مثالِ الحقيقة في الذهن وهو العلم وجدته أيضاً كذلك؛ لأنَّهُ مطابق للحقيقة المانعة، والمطابقة توجب المشاركة في المنع من حيث حقيقته. وإن نظرْتَ إلى العبارةِ عَنِ العلم وجدتها أيضاً حاصرةً؛ النظر في حد الحق من حيث العلم به فإنَّها مطابقة للعلم المطابق للحقيقة، والمطابق للمطابق مطابق. وإن نظرت إلى الكتابة وجدتها مطابقة للفظ المطابق للعلم المطابق للحقيقة، فهي أيضاً مطابقة. فقد وُجِدَ المنعُ في الكلّ، إلَّا أنَّ العادةَ لم تجرِ بإطلاق اسمِ النظر في حد الحد من حيث الكتابة.
A remark: So what is the essential in defining 'al-ḥadd'? Whoever defined 'al-ʿayn' as the mineral substance that is the noblest of currencies, rather he defined this as something disparate from the reality of the other thing, and they only share in the name 'al-ʿayn'. And the chosen view — understand this, for it is a highly beneficial principle. If you say: What then is correct according to you in defining al-ḥadd? Know that everyone who seeks meanings from words is lost and destroyed, and is like one who faces the west while seeking it. And whoever establishes meanings first in his mind without a word, then follows the meanings with words, has been guided. So let us establish the meanings, and we say: A thing has four stages in existence: The first: a reality in itself. The second: the establishment of an image of its reality in the mind, which is called knowledge. The third: composing its image with letters that indicate it, which is the expression indicating the image in the soul. The fourth: composing marks perceived by the sense of sight indicating the utterance, which is writing. And writing follows utterance, for it indicates it; utterance follows knowledge, for it indicates it; and knowledge follows the known, for it corresponds and agrees with it. These four are mutually corresponding and parallel, except that the first two are real existences not differing with eras and climes, while the other two — utterance and writing — differ with eras and nations because they are conventionally posited. But the conventions, although their forms differ, agree in that they are intended to correspond to reality. And it is known that al-ḥadd is taken from al-manʿ (prevention), and it is only metaphorically applied to these meanings due to sharing in the meaning of prevention. So consider where you find prevention in these four. If you start with the reality, you do not doubt that it encompasses the thing, being particular to it; for the reality of each thing is its property that it has and not others. So the reality is comprehensive and preventive. If you look at the image of the reality in the mind, which is knowledge, you find it likewise; because it corresponds to the preventive reality, and correspondence necessitates participation in prevention in terms of its reality. If you look at the expression about knowledge, you find it also encompassing; consideration of the definition of truth in terms of knowledge of it: for it corresponds to knowledge which corresponds to reality, and that which corresponds to that which corresponds is corresponding. If you look at writing, you find it corresponding to utterance which corresponds to knowledge which corresponds to reality, so it is also corresponding. Thus prevention is found in all, except that custom does not extend to applying the name — consideration of the definition of al-ḥadd in terms of writing.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and methodological discussion on the nature of definition (ḥadd). It opens with a critique of those who seek meanings from words, using the example of the ambiguous term 'al-ʿayn' (which can mean eye, gold coin, etc.). The author argues that one must first establish meanings in the mind without words, then attach words to those meanings. This is a core principle in Islamic epistemology: meanings precede expressions. The author then presents the four stages of existence: (1) the reality itself in the external world, (2) its mental image (knowledge), (3) the verbal expression, and (4) the written script. These four are parallel, but the first two are absolute and unchanging, while the latter two vary by time and culture because they are conventional. The key insight is that all four stages are intended to correspond to the reality. The term 'ḥadd' is derived from 'manʿ' (prevention), referring to the exclusive property that distinguishes a thing. The author examines each stage and finds that prevention is present: the reality itself is unique; knowledge corresponds to that unique reality; expression corresponds to knowledge; writing corresponds to expression. Hence, prevention is preserved throughout. The conclusion is that the definition (ḥadd) properly applies to the reality or its mental image, not primarily to words or writing. This guards against the common misunderstanding that definition is about linguistic stipulation rather than capturing the essence.

علم (ʿilm)

Key Takeaway
A proper definition (ḥadd) is built on the exclusive reality of a thing, not on linguistic convention, and this exclusivity is preserved across all stages of existence from reality to writing.

The Meaning of 'Had' and Its Types
The author explains the concept of 'had' across four levels and distinguishes its verbal, descriptive, and essential senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of definition (verbal, descriptive, essential) and identify which type is being used in a given passage.

Core Idea
Definition is not just a word; it traces 'prevention' from reality through to speech.

This passage explains that the term 'had' (definition/limit) is rooted in the idea of prevention (man‘) — preventing confusion between things. The author traces this notion across four levels: reality itself, mental knowledge, verbal expression, and writing. While all share prevention, custom reserves the term 'had' for reality and expression. The passage then classifies verbal definitions into three types: verbal (lafẓī), descriptive (rasmī), and essential (ḥaqīqī). Understanding this taxonomy is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core notion of 'had'
The author derives 'had' from 'man‘' (prevention). Note that every level of 'had' must prevent confusion between the defined thing and other things.
2
Trace the four levels of 'had'
The four levels are: (1) reality itself (al-ḥaqīqah), (2) mental knowledge (al-‘ilm), (3) verbal expression (al-lafẓ), and (4) writing (al-kitābah). Understand how each 'prevents' in its own way: reality inherently, knowledge by matching reality, expression by matching knowledge, writing by matching expression.
3
Observe which levels are conventionally called 'had'
Custom applies 'had' only to reality and expression, not to mental knowledge or writing. The term is shared between these two, so it has two distinct definitions depending on context.
4
Distinguish the three types of verbal definition
Verbal definition (al-ḥadd al-lafẓī) merely substitutes a word with a clearer synonym (e.g., 'knowledge is cognition'). Descriptive definition (al-ḥadd al-rasmī) explains the thing by listing its essential or necessary properties on condition of universality and reversibility. Essential definition (al-ḥadd al-ḥaqīqī) indicates the complete essence (māhiyya) of the thing, and inherently possesses universality and reversibility.
5
Apply the classification to an example
Take the statement 'existent is the thing'. According to the passage, this is a verbal definition because it replaces 'existent' with the more familiar 'thing'. Check if it unites and prevents. Similarly, identify whether definitions of Islamic terms like 'ṣalāt' or 'zakāt' in fiqh manuals are descriptive or essential.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يختلفان بالأعصار والأمصار، والآخرين - وهما اللفظ والكتابة - تختلفُ بالأعصار والأمم؛ لأنَّها موضوعةٌ بالاختيار، ولكن الأوضاع وإن اختلفَتْ صورها .. فهي متفقةٌ في أنَّها قُصِدَ بها مطابقة الحقيقة . ومعلوم أنَّ الحدَّ مأخوذُ مِنَ المنع، وإِنَّما استُعير لهذهِ المعاني للمشاركة في معنى المنع، فانظر المنع أين تجده في هذه الأربعة . فإن ابتدأت بـ ( الحقيقةِ ) .. لم تشك في أنَّها حاصرة للشيء مخصوصةٌ بهِ؛ إذ حقيقة كلّ شيءٍ : خاصِيَّتُه التي له وليست لغيره، فإذاً ( الحقيقة ( جامعة مانعة . فإن نظرتَ إلى مثالِ (الحقيقة) في الذهن وهو العلم .. وجدته أيضاً كذلك؛ لأنَّهُ مطابق للحقيقة المانعة، والمطابقة توجب المشاركة في المنع من حيث حقيقته. وإن نظرْتَ إلى العبارةِ عَنِ ( العلم ) .. وجدتها أيضاً حاصرةً؛ فإنَّها مطابقة للعلم المطابق للحقيقة، والمطابق للمطابق مطابق. وإن نظرت إلى الكتابة .. وجدتها مطابقة للفظ المطابق للعلم المطابق للحقيقة، فهي أيضاً مطابقة. فقد وُجِدَ المنعُ في الكلّ، إلَّا أنَّ العادةَ لم تجرِ بإطلاق اسمِ الحدّ على الكتابةِ التي هي الرابعةُ، ولا على العلم الذي هو الثاني بلْ هوَ مشترك بينَ الحقيقةِ وبينَ اللفظ، وكلُّ لفظ مشترك بينَ حقيقتين .. فلا بد وأن يكونَ لهُ حدَّانِ مختلفان؛ كلفظ ( العين، والمختار ) ونظائرهما. فإذاً؛ عندَ الإطلاقِ على نفس الشيء .. يكون حدُّ ( الحدّ ) : أنه حقيقة الشيء وذاته، وعند الإطلاق الثاني .. يكون حدُّ ( الحد ) : أنَّهُ اللفظ الجامع المانع. إلَّا أنَّ الذين أطلقوه على اللفظ أيضاً اصطلاحُهُمْ مختلف كما ذكرناه في الحدّ اللفظي والرسمي والحقيقي . فحدّ ( الحدِ ) عندَ مَنْ يقنعُ بتكريرِ اللفظ؛ كقوله : الموجود هوَ الشيءُ، والحركةُ هيَ النُّقْلة، والعلم هو المعرفة .. هو تبديل اللفظ بما هو أوضحُ عندَ السائل على شرط يجمعُ ويمنعُ . وأمَّا حدُّ ( الحدِ ) عندَ مَن يقنعُ بالرسميَّاتِ .. فهوَ أَنَّهُ اللفظ الشارحُ للشيء بتعديدِ صفاتِهِ الذاتيّةِ أو اللازمة على وجه يميّزُهُ عَنْ غیره تمييزاً يطرد وينعكس . وأمَّا حدُّهُ عندَ مَن لا يطلقُ اسم الحد إلا على الحقيقي : ( أنه القولُ الدال على تمام ماهية الشيء )، ولا يُحتاج في هذا أن يُذكر الطرد والعكس؛ لأنَّ ذلك يتبع الماهية بالضرورة
They do not differ across eras and regions, and the other two — which are the expression and writing — differ across eras and nations because they are posited by choice, but the posited forms, even if their forms differ, agree in that they are intended to match reality. It is known that 'had' is derived from prevention, and it is only borrowed for these meanings due to sharing in the meaning of prevention. So look for where you find prevention in these four. If you begin with (the reality), you do not doubt that it encompasses the thing and is specific to it; for the reality of everything: its property that belongs to it and not to others. So then (the reality) is comprehensive and preventive. If you look at the example of (reality) in the mind, which is knowledge, you find it also likewise; because it matches the preventive reality, and matching necessitates participation in prevention according to its reality. And if you look at the expression about (knowledge), you also find it encompassing; for it matches the knowledge that matches the reality, and that which matches what matches is itself a match. And if you look at writing, you find it matching the expression that matches the knowledge that matches the reality, so it also is a match. So prevention is found in all, except that custom does not apply the name 'had' to writing which is the fourth, nor to knowledge which is the second; rather it is shared between reality and expression. And every expression shared between two realities must have two different definitions; like the word 'al-'ayn' and 'al-mukhtar' and their likes. So then: when applied to the thing itself, the definition of 'had' is: it is the reality of the thing and its essence. And when applied the second way, the definition of 'had' is: it is the comprehensive preventive expression. However, those who apply it to the expression also differ in their terminology as we mentioned regarding verbal, descriptive, and essential definition. So the definition of 'had' according to one who is satisfied with repeating the word — like his saying: 'the existent is the thing', 'motion is transference', 'knowledge is cognition' — is the substitution of the word with what is clearer to the questioner on condition that it unites and prevents. And as for the definition of 'had' according to one satisfied with descriptives, it is that it is the explicatory expression of the thing by enumerating its essential or necessary qualities in a way that distinguishes it from others with a distinguishing that is universal and reversible. And as for its definition according to one who does not apply the name 'had' except to the essential: 'it is the statement that indicates the complete essence of the thing', and in this there is no need to mention universality and reversibility because that follows the essence necessarily.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage clarifies the concept of 'had' (definition/limit) by tracing the notion of 'man' (prevention) across four levels: the reality itself (الحقيقة), its mental representation (العلم), the verbal expression (اللفظ), and writing (الكتابة). The author argues that all four share the property of delimiting or 'preventing' the thing from being conflated with others, but custom restricts the term 'had' to reality and expression. When applied to reality, 'had' means the essence itself. When applied to expression, it is a comprehensive and preventive statement. The author then distinguishes three types of verbal definition: 1) Verbal definition (الحد اللفظي) — merely replacing a word with a more familiar one, e.g., 'the existent is the thing'. 2) Descriptive definition (الحد الرسمي) — explaining the thing by listing its essential or necessary properties to distinguish it, with the conditions of universality (طرد) and reversibility (عكس). 3) Essential definition (الحد الحقيقي) — 'the statement that indicates the complete essence of the thing', which inherently possesses universality and reversibility because they follow from the essence. This taxonomy is crucial in Islamic logic for evaluating definitions.

Key Terms
الحد
المنع
الحقيقة
الحد اللفظي
الحد الرسمي
الحد الحقيقي

Key Takeaway
The three types of definition serve different purposes; only essential definition reveals complete essence, while descriptive and verbal definitions are useful for clarification.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
7 min
Identifying a Verbal Definition (al-Hadd al-Lafẓī)
Ibn Taymiyya rejects 'ilm = ma'rifa as a verbal definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a verbal definition (hadd lafzi) and explain why it fails to provide real knowledge of a thing's essence.

Core Idea
A verbal definition merely swaps words; it does not disclose quiddity.

Ibn Taymiyya begins his critique by examining a common definition of 'ilm as ma'rifa (cognition). He dismisses this as a verbal definition — the weakest kind — because it only replaces one word with a synonym. In this lesson, you will learn how to recognize such definitions and why they are insufficient for rigorous Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
In the first definition, 'ilm is being defined as ma'rifa. The definiendum is 'ilm, the definiens is ma'rifa.
Ma'rifa is considered synonymous with 'ilm in this context.
2
Check if the definiens is a synonym or equivalent
A verbal definition simply substitutes one word for another without adding new information. Ibn Taymiyya gives examples like defining 'lion' as 'layth'.
3
Test if it adds any new information
Even if elaborated as 'cognition of the known as it is', it remains tautological. True definition must explain the essence.
Compare with defining 'existent' as 'thing that has existence'.
4
Contrast with a real definition (hadd haqiqi)
A real definition would give genus and differentia, clarifying the nature of 'ilm, not just rename it.
Ibn Taymiyya implies this standard but does not provide his own definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الامتحان الثاني في تعريف العلم
اختلف في حد العلم : فقيل : هو المعرفة ، وهذا حد لفظي ، وهو أضعف أنواع الحد اللفظ أضعف أنواع الحدود فإنه تكرير لفظ بذكر ما يرادفه كما يقال حد الأسد الليث وحد العقار الخمر وحد الموجود الشيء وحد الحركة النقلة
ولا يخرج عن كونه لفظيا بأن يقال معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به لأنه في حكم التطويل والتكرير إذ المعرفة لا تطلق إلا على ما هو كذلك فهو كقول القائل حد الموجود الشيء الذي له ثبوت ووجود فإن هذا التطويل لا يخرجه عن كونه لفظيا
ولست أمنع من أن يسمى مسمى هذا حدا فلفظ الحد لفظ في اللغة مباح طافح يستعيره من يريده مما فيه نوع من المنع فلو سمى مسمى قلنسوة حدا من حيث إنه يمنع البرد لم يمنع منه
هذا إن كان الحد عنده عبارة عن لفظ مانع فإن كان عنده عبارة عن قول شارح لماهية الشيء مصور حقيقته في ذهن السائل فقد ظلم بإطلاق هذا الاسم على قوله العلم هو المعرفة
وقيل أيضا إنه الذي يعلم به وإنه الذي تكون الذات عالما وهذا أبعد من الأول فإنه مساو له في الخلو عن الشرح والدلالة على الماهية
ولكن قد يتوهم في الأول شرح اللفظ بأن يكون أحد اللفظين أخفى من الآخر فيشرح الأخفى بالأشهر وأصل الاشتقاق أما العالم ويعلم فهو مشتق من نفس العلم ومن أشكل عليه المصدر كيف يتضح لديه المشتق منه والمشتق أخفى من المشتق منه وهو كقول القائل في حد الفضة إنه الذي تصاغ منه الأواني الفضي
وقيل إنه الوصف الذي يتأنى للمتصف به إتقان الفعل وإحكامه وهذا ذكر لازم من لوازم العلم فيكون رسميا وهو أبعد مما قبله من حيث إنه أخص من العلم فإنه يتناول بعض العلوم ولكنه أقرب بوجه آخر مما قبله وهو أنه ذكر لازم قريب من الذات يفيد شرحا وبيانا ما بخلاف قوله ما يعلم به وما يكون الذات به عالما
The second examination concerning the definition of 'ilm. They differed concerning the definition of 'ilm. It was said: It is ma'rifa (cognition). This is a verbal definition (hadd lafzi), and it is the weakest of the types of verbal definition—the weakest of the types of definitions—because it is a repetition of a word by mentioning its synonym, as it is said: The definition of 'the lion' (al-asad) is 'the lion' (al-layth), the definition of 'wine' (al-‘uqar) is 'al-khamr', the definition of 'the existent' (al-mawjud) is 'the thing' (al-shay'), and the definition of 'motion' (al-haraka) is 'the movement' (al-nuqla). It is not removed from being verbal by saying 'the cognition of the known as it is' because it is in the category of elongation and repetition, since 'cognition' is not applied except to that which is so. So it is like the saying of someone: The definition of 'the existent' is 'the thing that has affirmation and existence', for this elongation does not remove it from being verbal. I do not prevent that someone name this a definition, for the word 'hadd' in language is a permissible, overused word, borrowed by whoever wants it for something in which there is a type of prevention. So if someone were to name a 'qalansuwa' (a hat) a 'hadd' because it prevents cold, he would not be prevented from it. This is if he considers 'hadd' to be an expression of a preventing word. But if he considers it an expression of a statement explaining the quiddity of a thing, depicting its reality in the mind of the questioner, then he has wronged by applying this name to his saying 'knowledge is cognition'. It was also said: It is that by which one knows, and that by which the essence is knowing. This is even more remote than the first, for it is equal to it in being empty of explanation and indication of quiddity. However, there might be imagined in the first a verbal explanation, by one of the two words being more obscure than the other, so the more obscure is explained by the more famous. The origin of derivation—as for 'the knower' and 'he knows', they are derived from the same 'ilm. If someone finds the [verbal] noun problematic, how can the derived word and that from which it is derived become clear to him while the derived is more obscure than that from which it is derived? This is like the saying of someone in the definition of silver: It is that from which silver vessels are made. This is a mention of a concomitant (lāzim). It was said: It is the description that enables the possessor to perfect his action and master it. This is a mention of a concomitant among the concomitants of 'ilm, so it is a formal definition (hadd rasmī). It is more remote than what preceded it in that it is more specific than 'ilm, for it applies to some sciences, but it is closer in another aspect than what preceded it: it is a mention of a concomitant near to the essence that gives some explanation and clarification, unlike his saying 'that by which one knows' and 'that by which the essence is knowing'.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of different definitions of 'ilm (knowledge). Ibn Taymiyya critiques three proposed definitions, evaluating their adequacy in terms of logical definition theory. The first definition identifies 'ilm with ma'rifa (cognition). He classifies this as a verbal definition (hadd lafzi), which merely repeats the meaning with a synonym. He argues it is the weakest type because it provides no new understanding, analogous to defining 'lion' as 'layth' or 'wine' as 'khamr'. Even if one elaborates by saying 'knowledge is cognition of the known as it is', it remains merely tautological, like defining 'existent' as 'a thing with existence'. Ibn Taymiyya then discusses the term 'hadd' itself, noting that in language it can mean 'prevention', but if one considers 'hadd' in the technical sense as an expression explaining the quiddity of a thing, then calling 'knowledge is cognition' a definition is unjust. The second definition states that 'ilm is 'that by which one knows' and 'that by which the essence becomes knowing'. Ibn Taymiyya deems it even worse than the first because it is equally devoid of explanation and, moreover, uses derived forms (world, yu'lamu) which presuppose the meaning of the root. He illustrates this fallacy by comparing it to defining silver as 'that from which silver vessels are made', which is a mere mention of a concomitant, not an explanation of essence. The third definition defines 'ilm as 'the description enabling the possessor to perfect and master action'. This is a formal definition (hadd rasmī) that mentions a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of 'ilm. While it is more specific than 'ilm (since it only applies to some sciences), it is closer to the essence than the previous definitions because the concomitant is near to the essence and provides some kind of explanation, unlike the second definition. Throughout, Ibn Taymiyya applies a rigorous standard: a true definition must clarify the quiddity (māhiyya) of the thing, not merely rename it or refer to a derived property.

Key Terms
العلم
المعرفة
الحد اللفظي
حد

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to Ibn Taymiyya, which of the following best describes the definition of 'ilm as ma'rifa?
A) It is a real definition explaining the essence.
B) It is a verbal definition that fails to explain quiddity.
C) It is a formal definition using a concomitant.
D) It is a complete definition accepted by all logicians.

Key Takeaway
A verbal definition merely repeats the meaning; a true definition must disclose the essence of what is defined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'riba' as 'increase' (al-riba). Explain why this is a verbal definition and why it is insufficient for fiqh rulings on interest.

What Is Mantiq?

The Problem of Defining by Derived Terms
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes defining 'ilm via its derivatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a definition that uses derived terms (mushtaqq) and explain why it is circular or obscure.

Core Idea
Defining something by its own derivatives is like explaining the source by the branch; the branch presupposes the source.

Ibn Taymiyya's second target is the definition of 'ilm as 'that by which one knows' (ma yu'lamu bihi). He argues that this definition uses derived forms of the very word being defined, making it even worse than a verbal definition. In this lesson, you will learn to spot such circularity and understand why it fails to clarify.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definiens
The second definition: 'that by which one knows' (ma yu'lamu bihi) and 'that by which the essence is knowing' (ma takunu al-dhatu bihi 'alima). Note that 'yu'lamu' and 'alima' are derived from the root 'ilm.
2
Identify the derivational relationship
The definiens uses a derived form of the definiendum. This creates a circle: you cannot understand 'yu'lamu' without already knowing what 'ilm means.
Derived words (mushtaqq) are typically less clear than the root.
3
Compare with Ibn Taymiyya's analogy
He compares it to defining 'silver' as 'that from which silver vessels are made'. The definition presupposes 'silver'.
This type of definition is called al-hadd al-mushtaqq and is considered a fallacy.
4
Assess why it fails as a real definition
A real definition must clarify the unknown by the known. Using derived terms obscures rather than clarifies.
Ibn Taymiyya says this definition is even more remote (ab'ad) than the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الامتحان الثاني في تعريف العلم
اختلف في حد العلم : فقيل : هو المعرفة ، وهذا حد لفظي ، وهو أضعف أنواع الحد اللفظ أضعف أنواع الحدود فإنه تكرير لفظ بذكر ما يرادفه كما يقال حد الأسد الليث وحد العقار الخمر وحد الموجود الشيء وحد الحركة النقلة
ولا يخرج عن كونه لفظيا بأن يقال معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به لأنه في حكم التطويل والتكرير إذ المعرفة لا تطلق إلا على ما هو كذلك فهو كقول القائل حد الموجود الشيء الذي له ثبوت ووجود فإن هذا التطويل لا يخرجه عن كونه لفظيا

The second examination concerning the definition of 'ilm. They differed concerning the definition of 'ilm. It was said: It is ma'rifa (cognition). This is a verbal definition (hadd lafzi), and it is the weakest of the types of verbal definition—the weakest of the types of definitions—because it is a repetition of a word by mentioning its synonym, as it is said: The definition of 'the lion' (al-asad) is 'the lion' (al-layth), the definition of 'wine' (al-‘uqar) is 'al-khamr', the definition of 'the existent' (al-mawjud) is 'the thing' (al-shay'), and the definition of 'motion' (al-haraka) is 'the movement' (al-nuqla). It is not removed from being verbal by saying 'the cognition of the known as it is' because it is in the category of elongation and repetition, since 'cognition' is not applied except to that which is so. So it is like the saying of someone: The definition of 'the existent' is 'the thing that has affirmation and existence', for this elongation does not remove it from being verbal. I do not prevent that someone name this a definition, for the word 'hadd' in language is a permissible, overused word, borrowed by whoever wants it for something in which there is a type of prevention. So if someone were to name a 'qalansuwa' (a hat) a 'hadd' because it prevents cold, he would not be prevented from it. This is if he considers 'hadd' to be an expression of a preventing word. But if he considers it an expression of a statement explaining the quiddity of a thing, depicting its reality in the mind of the questioner, then he has wronged by applying this name to his saying 'knowledge is cognition'. It was also said: It is that by which one knows, and that by which the essence is knowing. This is even more remote than the first, for it is equal to it in being empty of explanation and indication of quiddity. However, there might be imagined in the first a verbal explanation, by one of the two words being more obscure than the other, so the more obscure is explained by the more famous. The origin of derivation—as for 'the knower' and 'he knows', they are derived from the same 'ilm. If someone finds the [verbal] noun problematic, how can the derived word and that from which it is derived become clear to him while the derived is more obscure than that from which it is derived? This is like the saying of someone in the definition of silver: It is that from which silver vessels are made. This is a mention of a concomitant (lāzim). It was said: It is the description that enables the possessor to perfect his action and master it. This is a mention of a concomitant among the concomitants of 'ilm, so it is a formal definition (hadd rasmī). It is more remote than what preceded it in that it is more specific than 'ilm, for it applies to some sciences, but it is closer in another aspect than what preceded it: it is a mention of a concomitant near to the essence that gives some explanation and clarification, unlike his saying 'that by which one knows' and 'that by which the essence is knowing'.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of different definitions of 'ilm (knowledge). Ibn Taymiyya critiques three proposed definitions, evaluating their adequacy in terms of logical definition theory. The first definition identifies 'ilm with ma'rifa (cognition). He classifies this as a verbal definition (hadd lafzi), which merely repeats the meaning with a synonym. He argues it is the weakest type because it provides no new understanding, analogous to defining 'lion' as 'layth' or 'wine' as 'khamr'. Even if one elaborates by saying 'knowledge is cognition of the known as it is', it remains merely tautological, like defining 'existent' as 'a thing with existence'. Ibn Taymiyya then discusses the term 'hadd' itself, noting that in language it can mean 'prevention', but if one considers 'hadd' in the technical sense as an expression explaining the quiddity of a thing, then calling 'knowledge is cognition' a definition is unjust. The second definition states that 'ilm is 'that by which one knows' and 'that by which the essence becomes knowing'. Ibn Taymiyya deems it even worse than the first because it is equally devoid of explanation and, moreover, uses derived forms (world, yu'lamu) which presuppose the meaning of the root. He illustrates this fallacy by comparing it to defining silver as 'that from which silver vessels are made', which is a mere mention of a concomitant, not an explanation of essence. The third definition defines 'ilm as 'the description enabling the possessor to perfect and master action'. This is a formal definition (hadd rasmī) that mentions a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of 'ilm. While it is more specific than 'ilm (since it only applies to some sciences), it is closer to the essence than the previous definitions because the concomitant is near to the essence and provides some kind of explanation, unlike the second definition. Throughout, Ibn Taymiyya applies a rigorous standard: a true definition must clarify the quiddity (māhiyya) of the thing, not merely rename it or refer to a derived property.

المشتق
ما يعلم به
الفضة

D) It is a formal definition.

Key Takeaway
Defining a term through its own derivatives is logically circular and fails to provide genuine understanding.

Formal Definition (al-Hadd al-Rasmī) and Its Evaluation
Ibn Taymiyya assesses the third definition as a formal definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a formal definition (hadd rasmī) and evaluate its strengths and weaknesses using Ibn Taymiyya's criteria.

Core Idea
A formal definition describes through concomitants but may be too narrow or too broad.

The third definition Ibn Taymiyya critiques defines 'ilm as 'the description that enables perfection of action'. He classifies this as a formal definition (hadd rasmī), which uses a necessary concomitant (lāzim) rather than genus and differentia. In this lesson, you will learn to recognize such definitions and assess their adequacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiens
The third definition: 'the description that enables the possessor to perfect and master action' (al-wasf alladhi yata'anna lil-muttasif bihi itqān al-fi'l wa ihkāmuhu).
2
Recognize it as a formal definition
It describes 'ilm through a property (lāzim) that necessarily accompanies it, not through its essence. This is called hadd rasmī.
The concomitant here is 'enabling perfection of action'.
3
Test its scope
Ibn Taymiyya notes that this definition is more specific than 'ilm (akhas min al-'ilm). It applies only to some sciences (e.g., practical crafts), not to all knowledge.
A proper definition must be coextensive with the definiendum.
4
Evaluate its utility
Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges it is closer to the essence than the previous definitions because the concomitant is near to the essence, but it still fails to capture the complete quiddity.
A real definition (hadd haqiqi) via genus and differentia would be ideal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الامتحان الثاني في تعريف العلم
اختلف في حد العلم : فقيل : هو المعرفة ، وهذا حد لفظي ، وهو أضعف أنواع الحد اللفظ أضعف أنواع الحدود فإنه تكرير لفظ بذكر ما يرادفه كما يقال حد الأسد الليث وحد العقار الخمر وحد الموجود الشيء وحد الحركة النقلة
ولا يخرج عن كونه لفظيا بأن يقال معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به لأنه في حكم التطويل والتكرير إذ المعرفة لا تطلق إلا على ما هو كذلك فهو كقول القائل حد الموجود الشيء الذي له ثبوت ووجود فإن هذا التطويل لا يخرجه عن كونه لفظيا
ولست أمنع من أن يسمى مسمى هذا حدا فلفظ الحد لفظ في اللغة مباح طافح يستعيره من يريده مما فيه نوع من المنع فلو سمى مسمى قلنسوة حدا من حيث إنه يمنع البرد لم يمنع منه
هذا إن كان الحد عنده عبارة عن لفظ مانع فإن كان عنده عبارة عن قول شارح لماهية الشيء مصور حقيقته في ذهن السائل فقد ظلم بإطلاق هذا الاسم على قوله العلم هو المعرفة
وقيل أيضا إنه الذي يعلم به وإنه الذي تكون الذات عالما وهذا أبعد من الأول فإنه مساو له في الخلو عن الشرح والدلالة على الماهية
ولكن قد يتوهم في الأول شرح اللفظ بأن يكون أحد اللفظين أخفى من الآخر فيشرح الأخفى بالأشهر وأصل الاشتقاق أما العالم ويعلم فهو مشتق من نفس العلم ومن أشكل عليه المصدر كيف يتضح لديه المشتق منه والمشتق أخفى من المشتق منه وهو كقول القائل في حد الفضة إنه الذي تصاغ منه الأواني الفضي
وقيل إنه الوصف الذي يتأنى للمتصف به إتقان الفعل وإحكامه وهذا ذكر لازم من لوازم العلم فيكون رسميا وهو أبعد مما قبله من حيث إنه أخص من العلم فإنه يتناول بعض العلوم ولكنه أقرب بوجه آخر مما قبله وهو أنه ذكر لازم قريب من الذات يفيد شرحا وبيانا ما بخلاف قوله ما يعلم به وما يكون الذات به عالما
The second examination concerning the definition of 'ilm. They differed concerning the definition of 'ilm. It was said: It is ma'rifa (cognition). This is a verbal definition (hadd lafzi), and it is the weakest of the types of verbal definition—the weakest of the types of definitions—because it is a repetition of a word by mentioning its synonym, as it is said: The definition of 'the lion' (al-asad) is 'the lion' (al-layth), the definition of 'wine' (al-‘uqar) is 'al-khamr', the definition of 'the existent' (al-mawjud) is 'the thing' (al-shay'), and the definition of 'motion' (al-haraka) is 'the movement' (al-nuqla). It is not removed from being verbal by saying 'the cognition of the known as it is' because it is in the category of elongation and repetition, since 'cognition' is not applied except to that which is so. So it is like the saying of someone: The definition of 'the existent' is 'the thing that has affirmation and existence', for this elongation does not remove it from being verbal. I do not prevent that someone name this a definition, for the word 'hadd' in language is a permissible, overused word, borrowed by whoever wants it for something in which there is a type of prevention. So if someone were to name a 'qalansuwa' (a hat) a 'hadd' because it prevents cold, he would not be prevented from it. This is if he considers 'hadd' to be an expression of a preventing word. But if he considers it an expression of a statement explaining the quiddity of a thing, depicting its reality in the mind of the questioner, then he has wronged by applying this name to his saying 'knowledge is cognition'. It was also said: It is that by which one knows, and that by which the essence is knowing. This is even more remote than the first, for it is equal to it in being empty of explanation and indication of quiddity. However, there might be imagined in the first a verbal explanation, by one of the two words being more obscure than the other, so the more obscure is explained by the more famous. The origin of derivation—as for 'the knower' and 'he knows', they are derived from the same 'ilm. If someone finds the [verbal] noun problematic, how can the derived word and that from which it is derived become clear to him while the derived is more obscure than that from which it is derived? This is like the saying of someone in the definition of silver: It is that from which silver vessels are made. This is a mention of a concomitant (lāzim). It was said: It is the description that enables the possessor to perfect his action and master it. This is a mention of a concomitant among the concomitants of 'ilm, so it is a formal definition (hadd rasmī). It is more remote than what preceded it in that it is more specific than 'ilm, for it applies to some sciences, but it is closer in another aspect than what preceded it: it is a mention of a concomitant near to the essence that gives some explanation and clarification, unlike his saying 'that by which one knows' and 'that by which the essence is knowing'.

The Philosophical Definition of Opposition (Tadadd)
Author defines tadadd per philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the philosophers' condition for tadadd and distinguish it from mere difference.

Core Idea
True opposition requires utmost difference, not just non-coexistence.

The passage explains that philosophers add a condition to tadadd: the utmost possible difference within a type. This lesson will help you understand that definition and apply it to distinguish real opposites from mere differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definition of tadadd in the Arabic. What is the definiendum?
2
Extract the condition
What extra condition do the philosophers add? 'the utmost possible difference within that type'
3
Apply the condition with examples
The author gives examples: white vs. black vs. color of wood; hot vs. cold vs. lukewarm. Check which pairs satisfy the condition.
4
Distinguish from other definitions
Compare with those who only require non-coexistence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فليس بين الأحياز اختلافُ بالطبع ، ولا بين الأكوان ، بل هي متشابهة ، فهؤلاء لمْ يُسمُّوا الأكوان متضادة ، لكن المختلفات ؛ كالحرارة والبرودة . وفريق رابعٌ شرطوا زيادةً ؛ وهو أن يكونَ بينَهُما غايةُ الخلافِ الممكن في ذلك النوع مِنَ التضادِ ؛ بحيثُ لا يكون وراءَه خلافٌ ، وهؤلاءِ هُمُ الفلاسفةُ ، وهذا اصطلاحُهُمْ ، فزعموا أَنَّ البياض لا يضادُّ العودي (نسبة إلى العود، والمراد لونه هنا) ، بل السواد ، والحار لا يضادُّ الفاتر ، بل البارد . فهذا هو الشرحُ . ونری جماعةً مِنَ العميان يتنافرونَ في هذه الحدودِ ، ظانِّينَ أَنَّ فيها نزاعاً ، ولا يدركون أنَّ النظر نظرانِ : أحدُهُما : في الحقائقِ المجرَّدة دونَ الألفاظ : وهو الانقسامات المعاني والمباني التي ذكرناها أوَّلاً ، وليس في العقلاء مَنْ ينكر شيئاً منها . والثاني : في إطلاق الألفاظ والاصطلاحاتِ : وذلك يتعلَّقُ بالتشهي ؛ فإنَّ الألفاظ طافحةٌ مباحة ، لم يثبت مِنْ جهةِ الشرع وقفها على معنى معيَّن حتَّى يُمنع من استعمالها على وجه آخر . لعمري ؛ لؤ قيلَ : إِنَّ واضعَ اللغة وضعَهُ لشيء في الأصل ، فهوَ وقف عليه بالحقيقة ، ولغيره استعارةً .. فهذا لا أمنع منه ، ولكنَّ الخوض فيهِ لا يليقُ بالمحصّل الناظرِ في المعقولات ، بل بالأدباء الناظرين في اللغات . وقد ترى الواحد يغتاظ ويقولُ : كيف يستجيز العاقل أن يقول : ( الكونان لا يتضادَّانِ ) ومعلوم أنَّهُما لا يجتمعان ؟! وإِنَّما ذلك لما في نفسِهِ مِنَ القياس الخطأ الذي لا يشعر بهِ ، ونظم ذلك القياس : أنَّ قولَنا : ( يتضادانِ ) وقولنا : ( لا يجتمعان ) واحدٌ ، فكيفَ يُقالُ : يجتمعان ، ولا يجتمعانِ ؟! إِذْ نظمُهُ : أَنَّ كلَّ ما لا يجتمعان فواجب تسميتهما متضادين ، ومعلوم أنَّ الكونين لا يجتمعان ، فوجب تسميتهما متضادين . وليس يدري أن وجوب تسمية ما لا يجتمعان متضادين .. غيرُ أنَّ ثابت ، بل التسمية إلى أهل الاصطلاح .
So there is no difference by nature between 'places' (ahyaz) nor between 'accidents' (akwan), but they are similar. These people did not call the accidents opposite (mutadadd), but rather different (mukhtalifat), like heat and cold. A fourth group added an extra condition: that between them there be the utmost possible difference within that type of opposition, such that there is no difference beyond it. These are the philosophers, and this is their terminology. They claim that whiteness is not opposite to the color of wood (but blackness is), and heat is not opposite to lukewarm (but cold is). So this is the explanation. We see a group of the blind who are agitated about these definitions, thinking that there is a dispute in them, not realizing that the inquiry is of two types: one concerning abstract realities without regard to words - which is the divisions of meanings and structures that we mentioned earlier - and none among the intelligent deny any of it. The second concerns the usage of words and technical terms: that pertains to desire; for words are abundant and permissible, and no restriction from the Law has been established to reserve them for a specific meaning to prevent their use in another way. By my life! If it is said that the language inventor originally placed a word for something, it is a reserved term for it in reality, and for other things metaphorically... I do not object to that, but delving into it is not fitting for the advanced researcher into intelligibles, but rather for literary scholars studying languages. You may see someone becoming angry and saying: 'How can a rational person allow himself to say: the two accidents are not opposite, when it is known that they cannot coexist?' That is only because of a mistaken syllogism in his mind that he is not aware of. The form of that syllogism: our saying 'they are opposite' and 'they do not coexist' are the same, so how can it be said they coexist and do not coexist? That is, his syllogism: every two things that do not coexist must be called opposites, and it is known that the two accidents do not coexist, so they must be called opposites. He does not know that the necessity of calling things that do not coexist 'opposite' is not established; rather, naming is up to the people of terminology.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of tadadd (opposition) and distinguishes between conceptual reality and linguistic convention. The author begins by stating that there is no natural difference between places (ahyaz) or between accidents (akwan); they are similar. Some people refused to call accidents 'opposite' but rather called them 'different'. Then a fourth group, the philosophers, added a condition: true opposites must have the utmost possible difference within that type, such as whiteness opposite to blackness, not to the color of wood; heat opposite to cold, not to lukewarm. The author then notes that some people become agitated over these definitions, thinking there is real dispute, but they fail to realize there are two aspects: one concerning abstract realities (which are agreed upon) and one concerning linguistic terms (which are matters of convention). The author further illustrates a common logical error: someone argues that since two accidents cannot coexist, they must be opposites. But the author points out that the premise 'everything that cannot coexist is opposite' is not established; it is a terminological issue. Thus the syllogism is invalid because its major premise is false or not granted. The example given is the mistaken syllogism: 'Every two things that do not coexist must be called opposites. The two accidents do not coexist. Therefore they must be called opposites.' The author asserts that the necessity of such a naming is not established; naming belongs to the people of terminology.

الكون

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define tadadd as the extreme difference within a type, not merely non-coexistence.

The Fallacy of Confusing Linguistic and Conceptual Terms
Author critiques a fallacious syllogism and distinguishes conceptual from terminological inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the fallacious syllogism that assumes non-coexistence implies opposition, and distinguish between conceptual truth and terminological convention.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism requires true premises; terminological labels do not follow from conceptual facts alone.

The author critiques a common mistake: people see that two accidents cannot coexist and conclude they must be called opposites. This lesson will dissect the syllogism, identify its flaw, and show how to avoid confusing conceptual reality with linguistic labeling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
What is the person trying to prove? That the two accidents must be called opposites.
2
Extract the major premise
Every two things that do not coexist must be called opposites.
3
Check the major premise
Is this premise accepted? The author says it is not established; naming is conventional.
4
Recognize the confusion
The person conflates the conceptual fact (non-coexistence) with the terminological label (opposite).

فليس بين الأحياز اختلافُ بالطبع ، ولا بين الأكوان ، بل هي متشابهة ، فهؤلاء لمْ يُسمُّوا الأكوان متضادة ، لكن المختلفات ؛ كالحرارة والبرودة . وفريق رابعٌ شرطوا زيادةً ؛ وهو أن يكونَ بينَهُما غايةُ الخلافِ الممكن في ذلك النوع مِنَ التضادِ ؛ بحيثُ لا يكون وراءَه خلافٌ ، وهؤلاءِ هُمُ الفلاسفةُ ، وهذا اصطلاحُهُمْ ، فزعموا أَنَّ البياض لا يضادُّ العودي (نسبة إلى العود، والمراد لونه هنا) ، بل السواد ، والحار لا يضادُّ الفاتر ، بل البارد . فهذا هو الشرحُ . ونری جماعةً مِنَ العميان يتنافرونَ في هذه الحدودِ ، ظانِّينَ أَنَّ فيها نزاعاً ، ولا يدركون أنَّ النظر نظرانِ : أحدُهُما : في الحقائقِ المجرَّدة دونَ الألفاظ : وهو الانقسامات المعاني والمباني التي ذكرناها أوَّلاً ، وليس في العقلاء مَنْ ينكر شيئاً منها . والثاني : في إطلاق الألفاظ والاصطلاحاتِ : وذلك يتعلَّقُ بالتشهي ؛ فإنَّ الألفاظ طافحةٌ مباحة ، لم يثبت مِنْ جهةِ الشرع وقفها على معنى معيَّن حتَّى يُمنع من استعمالها على وجه آخر . لعمري ؛ لؤ قيلَ : إِنَّ واضعَ اللغة وضعَهُ لشيء في الأصل ، فهوَ وقف عليه بالحقيقة ، ولغيره استعارةً .. فهذا لا أمنع منه ، ولكنَّ الخوض فيهِ لا يليقُ بالمحصّل الناظرِ في المعقولات ، بل بالأدباء الناظرين في اللغات . وقد ترى الواحد يغتاظ ويقولُ : كيف يستجيز العاقل أن يقول : ( الكونان لا يتضادَّانِ ) ومعلوم أنَّهُما لا يجتمعان ؟! وإِنَّما ذلك لما في نفسِهِ مِنَ القياس الخطأ الذي لا يشعر بهِ ، ونظم ذلك القياس : أنَّ قولَنا : ( يتضادانِ ) وقولنا : ( لا يجتمعان ) واحدٌ ، فكيفَ يُقالُ : يجتمعان ، ولا يجتمعانِ ؟! إِذْ نظمُهُ : أَنَّ كلَّ ما لا يجتمعان فواجب تسميتهما متضادين ، ومعلوم أنَّ الكونين لا يجتمعان ، فوجب تسميتهما متضادين . وليس يدري أن وجوب تسمية ما لا يجتمعان متضادين .. غيرُ أنَّ ثابت ، بل التسمية إلى أهل الاصطلاح .
So there is no difference by nature between 'places' (ahyaz) nor between 'accidents' (akwan), but they are similar. These people did not call the accidents opposite (mutadadd), but rather different (mukhtalifat), like heat and cold. A fourth group added an extra condition: that between them there be the utmost possible difference within that type of opposition, such that there is no difference beyond it. These are the philosophers, and this is their terminology. They claim that whiteness is not opposite to the color of wood (but blackness is), and heat is not opposite to lukewarm (but cold is). So this is the explanation. We see a group of the blind who are agitated about these definitions, thinking that there is a dispute in them, not realizing that the inquiry is of two types: one concerning abstract realities without regard to words - which is the divisions of meanings and structures that we mentioned earlier - and none among the intelligent deny any of it. The second concerns the usage of words and technical terms: that pertains to desire; for words are abundant and permissible, and no restriction from the Law has been established to reserve them for a specific meaning to prevent their use in another way. By my life! If it is said that the language inventor originally placed a word for something, it is a reserved term for it in reality, and for other things metaphorically... I do not object to that, but delving into it is not fitting for the advanced researcher into intelligibles, but rather for literary scholars studying languages. You may see someone becoming angry and saying: 'How can a rational person allow himself to say: the two accidents are not opposite, when it is known that they cannot coexist?' That is only because of a mistaken syllogism in his mind that he is not aware of. The form of that syllogism: our saying 'they are opposite' and 'they do not coexist' are the same, so how can it be said they coexist and do not coexist? That is, his syllogism: every two things that do not coexist must be called opposites, and it is known that the two accidents do not coexist, so they must be called opposites. He does not know that the necessity of calling things that do not coexist 'opposite' is not established; rather, naming is up to the people of terminology.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of tadadd (opposition) and distinguishes between conceptual reality and linguistic convention. The author begins by stating that there is no natural difference between places (ahyaz) or between accidents (akwan); they are similar. Some people refused to call accidents 'opposite' but rather called them 'different'. Then a fourth group, the philosophers, added a condition: true opposites must have the utmost possible difference within that type, such as whiteness opposite to blackness, not to the color of wood; heat opposite to cold, not to lukewarm. The author then notes that some people become agitated over these definitions, thinking there is real dispute, but they fail to realize there are two aspects: one concerning abstract realities (which are agreed upon) and one concerning linguistic terms (which are matters of convention). The author further illustrates a common logical error: someone argues that since two accidents cannot coexist, they must be opposites. But the author points out that the premise 'everything that cannot coexist is opposite' is not established; it is a terminological issue. Thus the syllogism is invalid because its major premise is false or not granted. The example given is the mistaken syllogism: 'Every two things that do not coexist must be called opposites. The two accidents do not coexist. Therefore they must be called opposites.' The author asserts that the necessity of such a naming is not established; naming belongs to the people of terminology.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the syllogism 'Every two things that cannot coexist must be called opposites; the two accidents cannot coexist; therefore they must be called opposites' to be invalid?
A) The minor premise is false.
B) The syllogism is in the wrong figure.
C) The major premise is not established; naming is conventional.
D) The conclusion does not follow from the premises.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism may be formally valid but unsound if its major premise is a terminological convention, not a necessary truth.

Parsing a Definition: Life as an Accident
Author asks for the correct definition of 'life' as an accident and gives a partial answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in a definition of an accident like 'life' and assess its completeness.

Core Idea
Every definition needs a genus and differentia; for an accident, the genus is 'accident' itself.

This passage presents a question about the correct definition of 'life' when it is understood as an accident. The author begins to answer by considering the view of those who only affirm accidents, but the text is cut off. This lesson will help you parse such definitional questions in Islamic logic: what to look for, how to identify the genus and differentia, and why the context (e.g., affirming only accidents) affects the definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined. Here it is 'life' (al-hayat). Note that the question specifies it is an accident ('arad), which already gives you the genus.
The definiendum is what you are trying to define.
2
Determine the type of definition requested
The passage asks for 'the correct expression' (al-sahih min al-ibarat) for the 'hadd' of life. In Islamic logic, 'hadd' can mean a real definition (hadd haqiqi) or a verbal definition (hadd lafzi). Here, it is likely a real definition because the question is about the essence of life as an accident.
A real definition reveals the essence; a verbal definition clarifies meaning.
3
Extract the genus
The genus is the broader category. Since life is called an accident, the genus is 'accident' (al-'arad). Any definition must first state that life is a type of accident.
The genus is the first part of a definition.
4
Look for the differentia
The differentia is what distinguishes life from other accidents. The passage is cut off, but typical definitions in Islamic theology state that life is 'an accident that enables perception and voluntary movement'. Try to infer what differentia would be appropriate.
Without the differentia, the definition is incomplete (rasm naqis).
5
Contextualise the definition
The author mentions 'for one who only affirms the accident' (man la yuthbit illa al-'arad). This refers to a metaphysical position that denies self-subsistent substances. In that view, life cannot be a substance, so it must be defined purely as an accident. Understanding this context helps you judge whether the definition works within the given framework.
The definition's validity depends on the metaphysical assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نون فإن قلت : فما الصحيحُ مِنَ العباراتِ في حدّ ( الحياة ) التي تحريجة : فما حد ( الحياة ) على هيَ عَرَضٌ ؟ قلنا : أمَّا عِندَ مَن لا يثبتُ إِلَّا العَرَضَ .. فأقرب لفظ
Nun. So if you say: What is the correct expression concerning the definition of life? The objection: What is the definition of life while it is an accident? We said: As for one who only affirms the accident... the closest expression...

Commentary
This passage is a short definitional question about the term 'life' (hayat) as an accident (arad). The author presents a dialectical exchange: someone asks for the correct verbal definition of life, objecting that it is an accident. The answer begins by considering the view of those who affirm only accidents (perhaps denying a substantial soul). The final word is cut off, but the intended definition likely involves specifying life as an accident that enables certain functions. This brief excerpt serves as a parsing exercise: the student must understand the nature of 'arad' and how life fits into it.

Key Takeaway
Defining a term requires placing it in a genus and specifying its differentia; for life understood as an accident, the genus is 'accident' and the differentia must distinguish it from other accidents.

Five Types of Kawn and Terminological Mapping
Author classifies the concept of kawn into five types and presents a terminological disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the five types of 'kawn' based on number of states and locations, and identify the terminological mapping problem and the author's preferred definitions.

Core Idea
Three Arabic terms map to five conceptual divisions—a classic logical problem of definition and convention.

In this passage, the author introduces the kalam concept of 'kawn' and shows how the categories of motion and rest are defined. You'll learn to distinguish the five types of being (kawn) based on states and spatial locations, and understand the debate over which term applies to which type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two axes of classification
The author uses number of states (ahwal) and number of spatial locations (ahiyaz). States can be 1, 2, or 3+; locations can be 1, 2, or 3 for the last division.
2
Map out the five divisions
List each combination: (1) being in one state; (2) being in two states in one location; (3) being in two states in two locations; (4) being in three states in one location; (5) being in three states in three locations.
3
Contrast the three terms with the five divisions
The Arabic terms are only three: kawn, sukun, haraka. Notice the mismatch: five divisions but only three terms.
4
Extract the author's preferred definitions
Kawn = specification in a single state; Sukun = two specifications in one location across two or more consecutive states; Haraka = specification in two locations across two or more consecutive states.
5
Understand the restriction on first origination
The author's group does not apply sakin or mutaharrik to a substance at the first moment of its existence, meaning at that instant it is neither at rest nor in motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حصر
والذي يكون أكثر ينقسم : إلى ما يزيد على حالتين ، وإلى ما لا يزيد .
والذي يكون في حالتين ينقسم : إلى ما يكون في حيز واحد ، وإلى ما يكون في حيّزين ، وكذا الكونُ في ثلاثة أحوال فصاعداً : إلى ما يكونُ في حيّز واحد ، وإلى ما يكون في ثلاثة أحياز .
أقسام الكون فقد حصل ها هنا للعقلِ خمسة أقسام :
كون في حالة واحدة .
وكون في حالتين في حيز واحدٍ .
وكون في حالتين في حيّزين .
وكون في ثلاثة أحوال في حيّز واحدٍ .
وكون في ثلاثة أحوال في ثلاثة أحياز .
وكانَتِ الألفاظ ثلاثة ، والأقسام خمسةً ، فاختلف الناسُ في إطلاق تلك الألفاظ على هذه الأقسام :
فقال فريقٌ - وهو الأقربُ إلى الحقِّ ، وأعني بالحقِّ ها هنا : تقريب التعريف والاصطلاح ، وإلا .. فليس في هذا النظرِ رسم معنوي ، وإنَّما هو بحث عن اللفظ الفاشي . - : يعبر بالكونِ : عَنِ الاختصاص في حالةٍ واحدةٍ ، وبالسكون : عن الاختصاصينِ بحيّز واحدٍ في حالتين متواصلتين فصاعداً ، وبالحركة : عَنِ الاختصاص بحيّزين في حالتين متواصلتين فصاعداً .
وهؤلاء لمْ يسمُّوا الجوهر في أوَّلِ حدوثِهِ لا ساكناً ولا متحرّكاً
Summary: That which is more than two states divides into what exceeds two states and what does not exceed. That which is in two states divides into what is in one spatial location and what is in two spatial locations. Similarly, being in three states and above divides into what is in one location and what is in three locations. The divisions of being that have occurred here for the intellect are five: being in one state; being in two states in one location; being in two states in two locations; being in three states in one location; being in three states in three locations. The expressions were three, and the divisions five, so people differed in applying those expressions to these divisions. A group said – and it is closer to truth, and by truth here I mean approximation of definition and convention, otherwise there is no real definition in this inquiry, it is merely a search for the common word –: that by being (kawn) they express specification in one state; by rest (sukun) they express two specifications in one location in two consecutive states or more; and by motion (haraka) they express specification in two locations in two consecutive states or more. These people did not call the substance at the moment of its origination either at rest or in motion.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definitional discussion. The author first divides the concept of 'being' (al-kawn) based on the number of states (ahwal) and spatial locations (ahiyaz). He enumerates five categories: (1) being in one state, (2) being in two states in one location, (3) being in two states in two locations, (4) being in three states in one location, (5) being in three states in three locations. He notes that the Arabic terms are only three: kawn, sukun, and haraka, while the divisions are five, leading to disagreement on how to apply these terms. The author sides with a group that defines kawn as specification in a single state, sukun as specification in one location across two or more consecutive states, and haraka as specification in two locations across two or more consecutive states. This group does not apply the terms sakin or mutaharrik to a substance at the first moment of its existence (the moment of origination), implying that at that instant it is neither at rest nor in motion. The passage is foundational for understanding how these terms are used in kalam discussions of motion and rest.

Key Takeaway
The kalam terms kawn, sukun, and haraka have precise definitions based on number of states and locations, and they do not cover all possible situations, leaving the first moment of existence undefined.

Motion and Rest in Ahl al-Haqq Theology
Author critiques philosophical motion definition and presents Ahl al-Haqq view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define motion and rest according to Ahl al-Haqq and distinguish their view from the philosophical definition.

Core Idea
Motion is not two existences but one existence in a new place, with the substance persisting.

The passage contrasts two definitions of motion: the philosophers' view that motion is two existences in two places, which reduces time to imagination, and the Ahl al-Haqq view that motion is the existence of a substance in a place where it was not before, with the substance existing. Understanding this definition is crucial for the theological debate on atomism and divine action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate in the Arabic the philosophers' definition of motion (كونان في مكانين) and the Ahl al-Haqq definition (كون في مكان لم يكن قبلَهُ ولا بعدَهُ فيهِ). Note the critique: the philosophers' view makes time imaginary.
The philosophers' definition is indirect; they are introduced via the discussion of time.
2
Extract the elements of Ahl al-Haqq's definition
The definition has three conditions: (1) existence (كون) of the substance, (2) in a place, (3) where it was not before or after. They add 'with the existence of the substance' to avoid confusion with annihilation and re-creation.
'Before' here means the immediate prior moment, not any time.
3
Distinguish motion from rest
Rest is defined as existence in a place where it was before (كون في مكان كانَ قبلَهُ فيهِ). Compare: motion involves a new location, rest involves continuity.
4
Understand the requirement of three states
The author says the definition requires three states (prior, current, subsequent) for truth. Some accept two states. This reflects the theological context of atomism where each moment is discrete.
The three states correspond to: where it was, where it is, and where it will be.
5
Recognize the priority of meanings over expressions
The author concludes that after establishing the five categories (presumably the five universals or the five modes of existence), one may change expressions as long as meanings are clear. Otherwise, one is just a linguist, not a theologian.
This underscores that the definition of motion is a conceptual, not merely verbal, issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والزمانُ لا يوجد منه جزء ما لم يفنَ الذي قبلَهُ ، فلا يُصادف وجوده إلا في الوهم ، أمَّا في الخارج .. فلا ، وهؤلاء هم الفلاسفة . رأي أهل الحق في وقالَ أهلُ الحقِّ : لسنا نطلقُ اسم ( الحركة ) عليهِ مِنْ حيث حد ( الحركة ) إنَّه كونان في مكانين ، بلْ منْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ كون في مكانٍ لم يكن قبلَهُ ولا بعدَهُ فيهِ ، فألزموا أنَّ الجوهر الذي وُجِدَ في حالةٍ واحدةٍ لم يكن قبلَهُ ولا بعدَهُ فِيهِ ، فزادوا : ( مع وجود الجوهر ) للاحتراز . وهذا الإطلاق يستدعي صدقه ثلاثة أحوال ، فمَنْ يكتفي بحالتين تكون الحركة عندَهُ عبارة عن كون الجوهر في حيز لم يكن قبلَهُ فيهِ معَ وجودِهِ ، وأعني بهِ : القَبْلَ القريب ، فهوَ يُسمَّى حركةً مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ شُغِلَ بحيّز حصلَ بهِ فراغ الآخرِ ، وأمَّا السكون .. فهو كون في مكانٍ كانَ قبلَهُ فيهِ . ومَنْ أراد أن يغيّر هذه العباراتِ .. فلا حجْرَ عليهِ بعد أن يقرّر في عقلِهِ الأقسام الخمسةَ الأُولَ ؛ فإِنَّهُ بعد ذلك يشبه اللغوي الناظر الألفاظ ، لا المتكلّم الباحث عَنِ المعاني .
And time: no part of it exists until the part before it has passed, so its existence is only encountered in the imagination; as for external reality... no. And these are the philosophers. The view of Ahl al-Haqq: And Ahl al-Haqq said: We do not apply the name 'motion' to it from the perspective of the definition of motion as 'two existences in two places', but rather from the perspective that it is an existence in a place where it was not before nor after. So they necessitated that the substance which existed in a single state was not in it before nor after, so they added: 'with the existence of the substance' as a precaution. This application requires for its truth three states. Whoever is satisfied with two states, then motion for him is an expression for the existence of the substance in a location where it was not before, with its existence. And I mean by it: the near prior, so it is called motion from the perspective that it occupied a location which resulted in the vacating of the other. As for rest... it is an existence in a place where it was before. And whoever wants to change these expressions... there is no objection to him after he has established in his mind the first five categories; for after that he resembles a linguist who examines words, not a theologian who investigates meanings.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion of motion (حركة). The author first describes the position of the philosophers, who hold that time has no actual part until the prior part perishes, so motion only exists in the imagination. Then he presents the view of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, likely the Ash'ari or orthodox theologians). They define motion not as two 'existences' in two places (a view they criticize), but as an existence in a place where the substance was not before and will not be after. They add the condition 'with the existence of the substance' to avoid confusion. The author explains that this definition requires three states (prior, current, subsequent) for its truth; some are satisfied with two. He then distinguishes rest (سكون) as existence in a place where it was before. Finally, he notes that if one wishes to change these expressions, no objection after establishing the five categories of existence; doing so reduces one to a mere linguist playing with words rather than a theologian seeking meanings.

سكون
كون
جوهر
وهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, what is the correct definition of motion?
A) An existence in a place where one was not before, with the substance existing.
B) Two existences in two places.
C) A gradual transition through space.
D) The annihilation of one state and creation of another.

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Haqq definition of motion as a single existence in a new place, with the substance persisting, is a deliberate alternative to the philosophers' view that motion is two existences in two places, and it grounds the theological distinction between motion and annihilation.

The Problem of Multiple Definitions: Wajib as a Homonym
Author lists definitions of 'wajib' and identifies homonymy as the issue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the issue with defining a legal term when the word is an ism mushtarak (homonym), using 'wajib' as an example.

Core Idea
Homonymy causes seemingly conflicting definitions until the intended meaning is clarified.

In this passage, the author lists seven different definitions of 'wajib' given by jurists and usuliyyun. He then reveals that the root cause of this multiplicity is that the word 'wajib' itself is a homonym (ism mushtarak), used differently by theologians, jurists, and linguists. This lesson will help you recognise how homonymy can lead to definitional disagreements unless the intended meaning is specified.

Guided Path
1
List the definitions
Read the passage and note each of the seven distinct definitions of 'wajib' given (e.g., 'that which is attached to obligation', 'that which must be done', etc.). Write them down in order.
2
Identify the differences
Compare the definitions. Notice that they focus on different aspects: some mention obligation, others reward/punishment, blame, sin, etc. Ask yourself: are they defining the same thing?
3
Recognize the homonymy
The author points out that 'wajib' is an ism mushtarak. Look at his examples: the mutakallim uses it in contrast to impossible (e.g., 'God's existence is wājib'), while the linguist uses it in a different sense. Understanding that a single word can have multiple meanings explains why definitions vary.
4
Apply to a new term
Think of another Islamic legal term that might be homonymous, such as 'amr' (command / affair) or 'sunnah' (path / recommended act). How would you clarify its definition?
This step connects the lesson to broader usul al-fiqh practice.
