الامتحان الشام في تعريف الواجب عند الفقهاء والأصوليين
اختلفوا في حد ( الواجب ) : فقيل : الواجب : ما تعلَّق بهِ الإيجاب وقيل : ما لا بدَّ مِنْ فعلِهِ وقيل : ما يثاب على فعلِهِ ، ويعاقب على تركه . وقيل : ما يجب بتركه العقاب وقيل : ما لا يجوز الإقدام على تركه . وقيل : ما يصيرُ المكلف بتركه عاصياً وقيل : ما يلام تاركه شرعاً . ولو تتبَّعْتَ هذهِ الحدود .. طالَ الأمرُ ، ففيما قدَّمْتُهُ ما يعرّفُكَ وجه الخلل في المختلِ منها ، لكن أَهْديك إلى الحقِّ الواضح بتمهيد سبيل السلوكِ ، وهو أن تستنهج ما نهجتُهُ لكَ ، فتعلم . معرفة الألفاظ أنَّ الألفاظ في هذا الفن خمسةٌ : الواجب ، والمحظور ، والمندوبُ ، والمكروه ، والمباح . فدع الألفاظ جانباً ، وجرد النظر إلى المعنى أولاً . وأنت تعلمُ أنَّ ( الواجب ( اسم مشترك ؛ إذ يطلقه المتكلّمُ في مقابلة الممتنع ، ويقولُ : وجود الله واجب ، واللغوي على المصطلح بيان الاشتراك في لفظ ( الواجب ) .
Let us conclude the examinations by mentioning one definition from the definitions of the jurists and usuliyyun: The comprehensive examination in defining the 'obligatory' (al-wājib) according to the jurists and usuliyyun. They differed regarding the definition of (al-wājib). It was said: al-wājib is that which is attached to it obligation (ījāb). And it was said: that which must be done. And it was said: that for whose performance there is reward and for whose omission there is punishment. And it was said: that for whose omission punishment is incurred. And it was said: that which it is not permissible to omit. And it was said: that by whose omission the legally responsible person becomes a sinner. And it was said: that whose ommitter is blameworthy by law. And if you were to trace these definitions... the matter would be long. In what I have presented, there is what makes you know the aspect of deficiency in the deficient ones. But I guide you to the clear truth by paving the way of conduct: that you follow what I have laid out for you, so that you learn. The knowledge of words: the words in this art are five: al-wājib, al-mahẓūr, al-mandūb, al-makrūh, and al-mubāḥ. So set aside the words, and consider the meaning first. And you know that 'al-wājib' is a homonym (ism mushtarak); for the mutakallim uses it in contrast to the impossible, saying: the existence of God is wājib; and the linguist... (the presentation of the homonymy in the term 'al-wājib').
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a definitional examination focused on the term 'wājib' (obligatory) as used by jurists (fuqahā') and theorists of jurisprudence (uṣūliyyūn). The author lists seven different definitions given by them, indicating disagreement. He then states that tracing all would be lengthy, but he will point to the clear truth. His method is to first recognize that the words in this field (i.e., the five legal categories) are wājib, maḥẓūr (forbidden), mandūb (recommended), makrūh (disliked), and mubāḥ (permissible). He advises to set aside the words and consider the meaning first. Finally, he notes that 'wājib' is a homonym (ism mushtarak): the mutakallim (theologian) uses it in contrast to the impossible (e.g., 'the existence of God is wājib'), while the linguist uses it differently. The passage thus clarifies that definitions must be grounded in the intended meaning, not merely the word itself.

Setting Aside Words: A Method for Definitional Clarity
Author advises setting aside words and first considering meaning due to homonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the author's method of setting aside words and first considering the meaning to resolve definitional disputes in Islamic legal terminology.

Core Idea
To resolve definitional disputes, prioritise the intended meaning over the verbal labels.

After listing several definitions of 'wajib', the author does not simply choose one. Instead, he gives a methodological key: set aside the words and examine the meaning first. He notes that 'wajib' is a homonym, so clinging to the word leads to confusion. This lesson will teach you how to use this approach to bring clarity to any definitional discussion in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the challenge
The author says: 'they differed regarding the definition of al-wājib.' Notice that the disagreement is about the word. Your first step is to recognize that verbal differences often mask conceptual unity or confusion.
2
Identify the five categories
The author lists the five key words: wājib, maḥẓūr, mandūb, makrūh, mubāḥ. He says 'set aside the words'. Why? Because these labels are just conventions; the underlying meanings (the actual legal rulings) are what matter.
This connects to the broader classification of ahkām taklīfiyya.
3
Analyse the homonymy
The author points out that 'wājib' is used differently by the mutakallim (theologian) and the linguist. When a jurist uses 'wājib', which meaning does he intend? To answer, you must look at the context and the intended legal effect.
4
Prioritise meaning over words
The author's advice: 'فدع الألفاظ جانباً، وجرد النظر إلى المعنى أولاً' (So set aside the words, and consider the meaning first). Practically, this means: before arguing over a definition, establish what the term is supposed to refer to in reality. Only then can you evaluate whether the word captures it.
This is a foundational principle in definitional analysis (al-hadd wa al-rasm).

The Four Questions and Types of Definition
Author outlines the first rule on the four questions and types of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four questions (hal, ma, lima, ayy) and distinguish between real, formal, and verbal definitions.

Core Idea
The four questions are the keys to unlocking any definition.

This lesson introduces the first rule of definition: the four questions that every definition must answer. Understanding these questions is the first step in mastering the touchstone of definition.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the four questions
Hal (Is it?), Ma (What is it?), Lima (Why?), Ayy (Which one?). They correspond to existence, essence, cause, and differentiation.
2
Understand the types of definitions
Verbal (lafzi), formal (rasmi), real (haqiqi). Real definition captures essence through genus and differentia.
3
Connect questions to definition types
The 'ma' question is answered by a real definition; the 'hal' question is more basic.
4
Note the reduction
Most questions reduce to 'hal', meaning existence is the foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني مِنَ الكتابِ : في مِحَكِ الحَدِ - الفن الأوَّلُ : في القوانين - القانونُ الأَوَّلُ : في المطالب الأربعة وأنواع الحد - أمهات المطالب في الحدود - مطلب ( هل ) - مطلب ( ما ) - الحد اللفظي والرسمي والحقيقي - تمثيل للفرق بين الحد الحقيقي والرسـ - مطلب ( لِمَ ) - مطلب ( أي ) - غالب المطالب - عدا ما ذكر - راجع لمطلب ( هل ) - القانون الثاني : في ضرورة التفريق بين الذاتيات والعرضيات - بيان معنى الماهية - أقسام الذاتي - تمثيل لأقسام الذاتي - تحريجة : نجد ما هو أعم وأخص ، فكيف ذلك ؟ - العبرة بالذاتي لا باللازم أو العارض - الخلاف في حقيقة الجوهر بين المتكلمين والفلاسفة - إمكان تغير الماهيات - القانون الثالث : في كيفية صناعة الحد - شروط تحقيق الحد الحقيقي - بيان معنى الفصل - طلب الفصل بعد الجنس ، وإلا . . فاللوازم - تفاوت الحدود الرسمية
The second part of the book: on the touchstone of definition. The first art: on the rules. The first rule: on the four questions and types of definition. The main questions in definitions: The question 'Is it?' (hal), the question 'What is it?' (ma), the verbal, formal, and real definition. Example of the difference between real and formal definition. The question 'Why?' (lima), the question 'Which one?' (ayy). Most of the questions, except what was mentioned, refer back to the question 'Is it?'. The second rule: on the necessity of distinguishing between essential and accidental attributes. Explanation of the meaning of quiddity. Divisions of the essential. Example of the divisions of the essential. A puzzle: we find what is more general and more specific, so how is that? Consideration is given to the essential, not the necessary or accidental. Disagreement regarding the reality of substance between theologians and philosophers. Possibility of change of quiddities. The third rule: on how to construct a definition. Conditions for verifying a real definition. Explanation of the meaning of differentia. Seeking the differentia after the genus, otherwise... the necessities. Variation of formal definitions.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage outlines the structure of the second part of the book, focusing on the touchstone of definition (mihak al-hadd). The author introduces three rules. The first rule deals with the four questions (al-matalib al-arba'a): hal (Is it?), ma (What is it?), lima (Why?), and ayy (Which one?). These correspond to the different purposes of definition. Types of definition are listed: verbal (lafzi), formal (rasmi), and real (haqiqi). An example is promised to distinguish real and formal. Most questions are said to reduce to the question 'hal'. The second rule emphasizes distinguishing between essential (dhatiyyat) and accidental (aradiyyat) attributes. It explains the meaning of mahiyya (quiddity) and the divisions of the essential. A puzzle is raised about how we can have both more general and more specific terms. The rule asserts that consideration is given to the essentials, not the necessary or accidental. It notes the disagreement between theologians and philosophers about the reality of substance (jawhar). The possibility of change of quiddities is mentioned. The third rule is about how to construct a definition (sina'at al-hadd), including conditions for a real definition, the meaning of fasl (differentia), and the need to seek the differentia after the genus. Formal definitions vary in rank.

Key Terms
المطالب الأربعة
الحد الحقيقي
الحد الرسمي
الحد اللفظي

Key Takeaway
The four questions structure every inquiry into a definition; they are the analytical framework of the touchstone.

Distinguishing Essential and Accidental Attributes
Author outlines the second rule on essential vs accidental attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between essential (dhati) and accidental (aradi) attributes in a definition.

Core Idea
Essential attributes constitute quiddity; accidents are secondary.

The second rule of definition emphasizes the crucial distinction between attributes that make something what it is (essential) and those that are not necessary to its quiddity (accidental). This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Define quiddity (mahiyya)
Mahiyya is the 'what it is' of a thing. Essential attributes (dhatiyyat) are part of it; accidental (aradiyyat) are not.
2
Identify examples of essential and accidental
For 'human', 'animal' is essential (genus); 'laughing' is accidental (not part of quiddity).
Use Islamic examples: for 'salat', 'ruku' and 'sujud' are essential? No, they are parts; better: 'intention' (niyya) is essential.
3
Understand the puzzle
We find terms that are more general and more specific. The key is to consider essential attributes, not necessary or accidental ones.
4
Apply to real definitions
When constructing a real definition, only essential attributes should be included; accidents would make it a formal definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Constructing a Real Definition: Genus, Differentia, and Conditions
Author outlines the third rule on constructing a definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a real definition and the role of genus and differentia.

Core Idea
A real definition requires a proper genus and differentia that capture essence.

The third rule teaches the practical construction of a real definition. You will learn the conditions that make a definition valid, including the crucial step of seeking the differentia after the genus.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditions for a real definition
A real definition must include the genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) that together express the essence of the definiendum.
2
Learn the meaning of differentia (fasl)
Fasl is the distinctive attribute that separates a species from others within the same genus. For example, 'rational' differentiates humans from animals.
Use an Islamic example: 'speaking' (nutq) as differentia for human? But better: 'mukallaf' (accountable) is a differentia for humans in fiqh? Actually, 'rational' is standard.
3
Apply the rule: seek differentia after genus
Start with the broader genus, then find the specific differentia. If you cannot find a proper differentia, use necessary concomitants (lawazim) as a fallback.
4
Recognize the variability of formal definitions
Formal definitions can vary in rank depending on the attributes used; real definitions aim for completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني مِنَ الكتابِ : في مِحَكِ الحَدِ - الفن الأوَّلُ : في القوانين - القانونُ الأَوَّلُ : في المطالب الأربعة وأنواع الحد - أمهات المطالب في الحدود - مطلب ( هل ) - مطلب ( ما ) - الحد اللفظي والرسمي والحقيقي - تمثيل للفرق بين الحد الحقيقي والرسـ - مطلب ( لِمَ ) - مطلب ( أي ) - غالب المطالب - عدا ما ذكر - راجع لمطلب ( هل ) - القانون الثاني : في ضرورة التفريق بين الذاتيات والعرضيات - بيان معنى الماهية - أقسام الذاتي - تمثيل لأقسام الذاتي - تحريجة : نجد ما هو أعم وأخص ، فكيف ذلك ؟ - العبرة بالذاتي لا باللازم أو العارض - الخلاف في حقيقة الجوهر بين المتكلمين والفلاسفة - إمكان تغير الماهيات - القانون الثالث : في كيفية صناعة الحد - شروط تحقيق الحد الحقيقي - بيان معنى الفصل - طلب الفصل بعد الجنس ، وإلا . . فاللوازم - تفاوت الحدود الرسمية
The second part of the book: on the touchstone of definition. The first art: on the rules. The first rule: on the four questions and types of definition. The main questions in definitions: The question 'Is it?' (hal), the question 'What is it?' (ma), the verbal, formal, and real definition. Example of the difference between real and formal definition. The question 'Why?' (lima), the question 'Which one?' (ayy). Most of the questions, except what was mentioned, refer back to the question 'Is it?'. The second rule: on the necessity of distinguishing between essential and accidental attributes. Explanation of the meaning of quiddity. Divisions of the essential. Example of the divisions of the essential. A puzzle: we find what is more general and more specific, so how is that? Consideration is given to the essential, not the necessary or accidental. Disagreement regarding the reality of substance between theologians and philosophers. Possibility of change of quiddities. The third rule: on how to construct a definition. Conditions for verifying a real definition. Explanation of the meaning of differentia. Seeking the differentia after the genus, otherwise... the necessities. Variation of formal definitions.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage outlines the structure of the second part of the book, focusing on the touchstone of definition (mihak al-hadd). The author introduces three rules. The first rule deals with the four questions (al-matalib al-arba'a): hal (Is it?), ma (What is it?), lima (Why?), and ayy (Which one?). These correspond to the different purposes of definition. Types of definition are listed: verbal (lafzi), formal (rasmi), and real (haqiqi). An example is promised to distinguish real and formal. Most questions are said to reduce to the question 'hal'. The second rule emphasizes distinguishing between essential (dhatiyyat) and accidental (aradiyyat) attributes. It explains the meaning of mahiyya (quiddity) and the divisions of the essential. A puzzle is raised about how we can have both more general and more specific terms. The rule asserts that consideration is given to the essentials, not the necessary or accidental. It notes the disagreement between theologians and philosophers about the reality of substance (jawhar). The possibility of change of quiddities is mentioned. The third rule is about how to construct a definition (sina'at al-hadd), including conditions for a real definition, the meaning of fasl (differentia), and the need to seek the differentia after the genus. Formal definitions vary in rank.

A real definition is built by identifying the proper genus and then the specific differentia that captures the essence.

The Method of Capturing Definition: Ṭard and ‘Aks
Enumeration of the fourth law on definition method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the method of coextensiveness (ṭard) and counter-coextensiveness (‘aks) as the proper way to establish a definition, and contrast it with demonstration (burhān).

Core Idea
Definition is proven by coextensiveness, not demonstration.

This lesson introduces the fourth law: the path to capturing a definition. The author emphasizes that definition is not sought through demonstration but through the method of ṭard and ‘aks. We will explore what these terms mean and how they ensure a definition is correct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The text states that definition is not sought by demonstration (burhān). Why might that be? Burhān is a syllogistic proof; definition requires a different approach.
Definition aims to capture essence, not to prove existence.
2
Understand ṭard and ‘aks
Ṭard means the definition applies to every instance of the defined; ‘aks means only instances of the defined satisfy the definition. Together they guarantee coextensiveness.
This is a test for a proper definition.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the definition of 'wine' (khamr) as 'anything that intoxicates'. Check ṭard: does every intoxicant count as wine? In Islamic law, yes. Check ‘aks: is every wine an intoxicant? Yes. So it passes.
If a non-intoxicant were considered wine, ṭard would fail.
4
Contrast with burhān
In burhān, we use premises to deduce a conclusion. Here, we do not derive the definition; we verify it by testing its coverage.
The definition is not the conclusion of a syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القانون الرابع : في طريق اقتناص الحد
- مثال لبيان أن الحد لا يُطلب بالبرهان
- سبيل إثبات المحدود هو الطرد والانعكاس
- تمثيل لإثبات المحدود بالطرد والانعكاس - كيف يتحقق الناظرُ الحدَّ في نفسه
القانون الخامس : في حصر مداخل الخلل في الحد، وهي ثلاثة
الخلل الناشئ عن الجنس
اعتبار المحل بدل الجنس
اعتبار ما كان بدل الجنس
اعتبار الجزء بدل الجنس
اعتبار القدرة بدل المقدور
اعتبار اللوازم غير الذاتية بدل الجنس
اعتبار النوع بدل الجنس
الخلل الناشئ من جهة الفصل
الخلل الناشئ من القوانين المشتركة
- خطأ حدّ الشيء بما هو أخفى وبالمساوي والفرعي
- خطأ حد الضد بضده
- خطأ المضاف في حد المضاف إليه وجود
- إدخال العلة في حد المعلول
القانون السادس : في كون المعنى البسيط يشرح أو يرسم ولا يحد
- لا سبيل إلى حدّ المعنى المفرد
- تمثيل لرسم المعنى المفرد
- تعليل لعدم حدّ المفرد
- الاستفهام عن المفرد يراد به حصر المعنى والحقيقة
- مثال بديع يوضّحُ العجز عن حدّ المفرد - أثر اعتقاد ( الحال ) ثبوتاً ونفياً في الحدِ
- التكرار والمرادف لا يدفع النقض
- انحصار حدّ المفرد في الرسمي واللفظي
- حد المركبات بتأليف بعض المفردات

- التصديقات تنتهي إلى علوم أولية
- حقيقة المفرد ثابتة بالعقل تحتاج إلى بيان لفظي أو رسمي فقط
الفن الثاني مِن مِحَلّ الحد : في الامتحاناتِ
الامتحان الأوَّلُ : في تعريف الحدِ
- يتصور الاختلاف إلا بعد التوافق على شيء واحد
The fourth law: On the way of capturing the definition. - Example to show that the definition is not sought by demonstration. - The way of proving the defined is by coextensiveness and counter-coextensiveness. - Illustration of proving the defined by coextensiveness and counter-coextensiveness. - How the investigator realizes the definition in himself. The fifth law: On enumerating the entry points of error in the definition, and they are three. Error arising from the genus: considering the location in place of the genus; considering that which was in place of the genus; considering the part in place of the genus; considering the power in place of the thing powered; considering the non-essential concomitants in place of the genus; considering the species in place of the genus. Error arising from the differentia. Error arising from the shared rules. - Error of defining a thing by what is more obscure, by the equal, and by the subordinate. - Error of defining an opposite by its opposite. - Error of the relative in the definition of that to which it is related as existence. - Inserting the cause into the definition of the effect. The sixth law: On the simple meaning being explained or described but not defined. - No way to define the single term. - Illustration of the description of the single term. - Explanation for the non-definition of the single term. - Questioning about the single term intends to limit the meaning and the reality. - An original example clarifying the incapacity to define the single term. - The effect of believing (the state) in terms of affirmation and negation in the definition. - Repetition and synonym do not repel refutation. - The definition of the single term is confined to the descriptive and the verbal. - The definition of compounds is by composing some single terms. - We must end up with self-evident single terms. - Assents end with primary sciences. - The reality of the single term is fixed by the mind, needing only verbal or descriptive explanation. The second art of the place of definition: On the examinations. The first examination: On the definition of definition. - Difference can only be conceived after agreement on one thing.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a structured outline of the laws (qawānīn) governing definition (ḥadd) in logic, as presented by the author of Mahakk al-Naẓar. It is a memorization anchor because it lists the key principles and common errors without detailed expansion. The fourth law states that definition is not sought through demonstration (burhān); rather, the method to establish the defined is through coextensiveness (ṭard) and counter-coextensiveness (‘aks). The author then gives an example and describes how the investigator arrives at the definition in his own mind. The fifth law enumerates three sources of error in definition: errors from the genus (al-jins), such as mistaking the substrate for the genus or a part for the whole; errors from the differentia (al-faṣl); and errors from shared rules, like defining something by what is more obscure or by its opposite. The sixth law addresses simple concepts (al-ma‘nā al-basīṭ): they cannot be truly defined but only explained or described (rasm). The passage ends with the beginning of the second art (al-fann al-thānī) concerning examinations, starting with the definition of definition itself, noting that disagreement presupposes agreement on something. Each point is a compressed rule meant for memorization; later commentaries expand on the examples and reasoning.

Key Terms
الحد (al-ḥadd): definition
الطرد والانعكاس (al-ṭard wa al-‘aks): coextensiveness and counter-coextensiveness
البرهان (al-burhān): demonstration, proof

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth law, what is the proper way to establish a definition?
A) Demonstration (burhān)
B) Coextensiveness and counter-coextensiveness (ṭard and ‘aks)
C) Induction (istiqrā’)
D) Argument from authority

Key Takeaway
A definition is validated by ensuring it covers all instances (ṭard) and only those instances (‘aks), not by syllogistic proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Common Errors in Definition: The Fifth Law
Enumeration of the fifth law on sources of error in definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize and identify common errors in definition (khala'l) by distinguishing errors from the genus, the differentia, and shared rules.

Core Idea
Errors in definition come from three sources: wrong genus, wrong differentia, or violating logical rules.

The fifth law provides a systematic classification of errors that plague definitions. Understanding these pitfalls helps you craft precise definitions and critique flawed ones. We will explore each category and its subtypes.

Guided Path
1
List the three error categories
The text says there are three entry points for error: from the genus (jins), from the differentia (faṣl), and from shared rules (qawānīn mushtaraka).
These are broad categories; the genus errors have many subtypes.
2
Subcategorize genus errors
Genus errors include: using the location instead of the genus, using a substitute, using a part, using power in place of the thing powered, using non-essential concomitants, and using the species instead of the genus.
Each of these mistakes something else for the true genus.
3
Understand differentia errors
The text does not elaborate on differentia errors, but generally they involve using a non-distinctive or incorrect property to separate the species.
A proper differentia must be unique to the species.
4
Recognize shared rule errors
These include: defining by something more obscure, by something equal, by a subordinate, by an opposite, by a relative (as if the relative's definition includes the relation), and by inserting the cause into the definition of the effect.
These violate logical rules about definiens and definiendum.
5
Identify an error in an example
Take the definition 'Human is a being that laughs'. Here 'laughs' is a non-essential concomitant, not a differentia. That is an error from the genus category (non-essential concomitant in place of genus).
Practice spotting the error type.

Simple Concepts Cannot Be Defined: The Sixth Law
Enumeration of the sixth law on simple meanings and description

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between simple and compound concepts, and explain why simple concepts cannot be defined (ḥadd) but only described (rasm) or explained verbally.

Core Idea
Simple concepts have no genus or differentia; they can only be described, not defined.

The sixth law sets a crucial boundary: some concepts are so fundamental that they cannot be broken down into genus and differentia. Such simple meanings (maʿānī basīṭa) must be described or pointed to, not defined. We will see why and how this works.

Guided Path
1
Define a simple concept
A simple concept is one that cannot be divided into genus and differentia because it has no components. For example, 'existence' or 'unity'.
These are like the atoms of thought.
2
Accept the limitation
The text says there is no way to define a single term (maʿnā mufrad). If you try, you either repeat it or use a synonym, which does not add knowledge.
True definition requires a genus and differentia.
3
Explore description instead
Instead of ḥadd, we can use rasm (description) by mentioning properties or concomitants, or we can give a verbal explanation (lafẓī) by clarifying the term's usage.
This still helps understanding without defining essence.
4
Recognize the hierarchy
Compound concepts are defined by combining simple ones, but eventually we reach simple terms that are self-evident (badīhī). Their reality is known by the mind; we only need verbal or descriptive expression.
This prevents infinite regress in definitions.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the concept 'wujūd' (existence). It is simple; we cannot define it as 'something that is' without circularity. Instead, we describe it as the opposite of non-existence or as the reality that grounds all things.
This is a common example in kalām.

- يتصور الاختلاف إلا بعد التوافق على شيء واحد
The fourth law: On the way of capturing the definition. - Example to show that the definition is not sought by demonstration. - The way of proving the defined is by coextensiveness and counter-coextensiveness. - Illustration of proving the defined by coextensiveness and counter-coextensiveness. - How the investigator realizes the definition in himself. The fifth law: On enumerating the entry points of error in the definition, and they are three. Error arising from the genus: considering the location in place of the genus; considering that which was in place of the genus; considering the part in place of the genus; considering the power in place of the thing powered; considering the non-essential concomitants in place of the genus; considering the species in place of the genus. Error arising from the differentia. Error arising from the shared rules. - Error of defining a thing by what is more obscure, by the equal, and by the subordinate. - Error of defining an opposite by its opposite. - Error of the relative in the definition of that to which it is related as existence. - Inserting the cause into the definition of the effect. The sixth law: On the simple meaning being explained or described but not defined. - No way to define the single term. - Illustration of the description of the single term. - Explanation for the non-definition of the single term. - Questioning about the single term intends to limit the meaning and the reality. - An original example clarifying the incapacity to define the single term. - The effect of believing (the state) in terms of affirmation and negation in the definition. - Repetition and synonym do not repel refutation. - The definition of the single term is confined to the descriptive and the verbal. - The definition of compounds is by composing some single terms. - We must end up with self-evident single terms. - Assents end with primary sciences. - The reality of the single term is fixed by the mind, needing only verbal or descriptive explanation. The second art of the place of definition: On the examinations. The first examination: On the definition of definition. - Difference can only be conceived after agreement on one thing.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a structured outline of the laws (qawānīn) governing definition (ḥadd) in logic, as presented by the author of Mahakk al-Naẓar. It is a memorization anchor because it lists the key principles and common errors without detailed expansion. The fourth law states that definition is not sought through demonstration (burhān); rather, the method to establish the defined is through coextensiveness (ṭard) and counter-coextensiveness (‘aks). The author then gives an example and describes how the investigator arrives at the definition in his own mind. The fifth law enumerates three sources of error in definition: errors from the genus (al-jins), such as mistaking the substrate for the genus or a part for the whole; errors from the differentia (al-faṣl); and errors from shared rules, like defining something by what is more obscure or by its opposite. The sixth law addresses simple concepts (al-ma‘nā al-basīṭ): they cannot be truly defined but only explained or described (rasm). The passage ends with the beginning of the second art (al-fann al-thānī) concerning examinations, starting with the definition of definition itself, noting that disagreement presupposes agreement on something. Each point is a compressed rule meant for memorization; later commentaries expand on the examples and reasoning.

Key Terms
المعنى البسيط (al-maʿnā al-basīṭ): simple meaning
المعنى المفرد (al-maʿnā al-mufrad): single term
الرسم (al-rasm): description
البديهي (al-badīhī): self-evident

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the sixth law, why can a simple concept not be defined?
A) It is too complex
B) It has no genus and differentia
C) It is unknown to the mind
D) It is a compound of simpler terms

Key Takeaway
Simple concepts cannot be defined; they can only be described or explained verbally, and their reality is known directly by the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example of a simple concept in Islamic theology (e.g., 'existence', 'being', 'knowledge') and explain why it cannot be defined by genus and differentia. Then provide a descriptive (rasm) explanation as the author would.

What Is Mantiq?

The Sixth Rule: Simple Terms Cannot Be Defined Essentially
Section headings on the sixth rule of definition and types of definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential (hadd) and descriptive (rasm) definitions and explain why simple terms require explanation rather than definition.

Core Idea
Simple terms are known by description or verbal explanation, not by essential definition.

This passage presents the sixth rule of definition from al-Ghazali's 'Mahakk al-Nazar': a simple meaning can only be explained or described, never defined in the essential sense. This is foundational because definitions are built from simpler components; without self-evident simples, the chain would be infinite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Sixth Rule
Find the line beginning with 'القانون السادس' and note the rule: 'في كون المعنى البسيط يشرح أو يرسم ولا يحد'.
This is the core claim about simple terms.
2
Understand 'Simple Meaning'
A simple meaning is an indivisible concept like 'existence' or 'unity' that cannot be broken down into genus and differentia.
Simple terms are basic building blocks of definitions.
3
Distinguish Hadd and Rasm
The passage states that simple terms are limited to verbal (lafzi) and descriptive (rasmi) definitions. Essential definition (hadd) requires a genus and differentia, which simples lack.
Hadd gives essence; rasm gives characteristics.
4
Apply the Rule
Choose a simple concept like 'wujud' (existence) and explain why it cannot be defined essentially—it is known directly by reason.
This illustrates the rule's application in theology.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
5 min
Real vs Descriptive Definition: The Uniqueness of Hadd Haqiqi
Query on real definition and its uniqueness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real definition (hadd haqiqi) and a descriptive definition (hadd rasm), and explain why a thing can have only one real definition.

Core Idea
The real definition captures the essence; it is unique. Descriptive definitions vary.

In this passage, the author poses a key question: Can there be multiple real definitions for a single thing? He answers with a firm 'no'. To understand why, we must first grasp what a real definition aims to do: it captures the quiddity (essence) of a thing, not just its accompanying properties. This lesson will help you parse this distinction and apply it to Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of definition mentioned
The passage opens by stating that the necessary concomitant (lazim) is used in the descriptive definition (hadd rasm). Then it asks about the real definition (hadd haqiqi). Notice that two types are contrasted: one that uses non-essential attributes, and one that seeks the essence.
Descriptive definitions can vary because different concomitants can be chosen.
2
Extract the central question about multiplicity
The author asks: 'هل تتعدد الحدود للشيء الواحد؟' (Can there be multiple definitions for one thing?). The immediate answer is 'لا تعدُّد في الحد الحقيقي' (There is no multiplicity in the real definition). This is the key claim of the passage.
The question is specifically about the real definition, not descriptive definitions.
3
Understand why the real definition is unique
A real definition expresses the essence or quiddity of a thing. Since each thing has a single essence, its real definition must be unique. In contrast, descriptive definitions list necessary concomitants, and many such to non-essential attributes can exist for one thing.
For example, knowledge can be described as 'that which necessitates judgment' or 'that which is opposed to ignorance', but its real definition is one.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic science example
Consider the definition of 'salah' in fiqh. Its real definition might be 'specific words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim' (essence). A descriptive definition might be 'a preventer of indecency' (a lazim). The first is unique; the second is one of many possible descriptions.
Knowing the real definition helps avoid conflating essential with accidental properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر اللازم يكون في الحد الرسمي - تحريجة : فما الحدُّ الحقيقي للعلم ؟ ما سنح به الخاطر الحاضر من حدّ ( العلم ) مقصد الحدّ الحقيقي - تحريجة : فهل تتعدد الحدود للشيء الواحد ؟ لا تعدُّد في الحد الحقيقي
Mentioning the necessary occurs in the descriptive definition. Query: What is the real definition of knowledge? What occurred to my present mind regarding the definition of knowledge. The aim of the real definition. Query: Can there be multiple definitions for one thing? There is no multiplicity in the real definition.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a query about the real definition (hadd haqiqi) of knowledge. The author first mentions that the necessary (al-lazim) is used in the descriptive definition (al-hadd al-rasmi). Then he poses a question: What is the real definition of knowledge? He mentions what spontaneously occurred to his mind. Then he states the purpose is the real definition. Then he asks whether a single thing can have multiple definitions, and answers that there is no multiplicity in the real definition. This establishes that the real definition is unique per thing, distinguishing it from descriptive definitions which may vary.

Key Terms

اللازم

Key Takeaway
A thing can have only one real definition because its essence is singular; descriptive definitions that rely on necessary concomitants can vary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
